And Abram was 86 years old when Hagar bore Ishmael to Abram.note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–3 · אוּלַי אִבָּנֶה מִמֶּנָּה ulai ibbaneh mimmennah

"An Egyptian slave-girl, and her name was Hagar." After the night of stars, the morning after. Sarai — introduced as barren back at 11:30, the sentence the whole promise strains against — has now waited ten years in Canaan (v3), and takes matters into her own hands. Hagar is a shiphchah, a female slave belonging to the mistress. The shelf upholsters the title — KJV, ASV and Douay "handmaid," Geneva "maid," NWT "maidservant" — while NIV (2011) says "slave"; MiSTeR keeps it plain: slave-girl. That she is Egyptian is not decoration: Pharaoh's parting gifts in the Egypt sojourn included "male and female servants" (12:16) — the likeliest door by which an Egyptian girl entered this household (rabbinic tradition went further and made her Pharaoh's own daughter; legend, but it shows how old the question is).

"Perhaps I shall be built up through her." The idiom is exact and this translation keeps it: ibbaneh, "I shall be built," puns on ben, "son" — a woman is "built up" by children (Rachel will use the identical formula with her own slave-girl, Genesis 30:3; the smoother shelf renderings — KJV "obtain children by her" — pave the pun). And this was law before it was desperation: a barren wife giving her slave as a surrogate, the child counting as the wife's, is written into the region's law codes (Hammurabi's among them) and marriage contracts. The Torah reports the custom; what it thinks of it, the next three verses show. Meanwhile the narrator reaches for the garden's exact grammar: Abram "listened to the voice of" his wife — the phrase of the verdict on Adam, "because you listened to the voice of your wife" (3:17, word for word) — and Sarai took and gave to her husband (v3) as the woman took and gave in 3:6. The scene is written to make you hear Eden: a plan that looks good in human eyes, and the wreckage after.

Verses 4–6 · וַתֵּקַל גְּבִרְתָּהּ בְּעֵינֶיהָ vateqal gevirtah be'eineha

"Her mistress became slight in her eyes." The verb is qalal, "to be light, slight" — the very root of the promise's warning, "the one who belittles you I will curse" (12:3). The moment Hagar conceives, the household's weights shift: the fruitful slave outweighs the barren mistress, and everyone feels it. Sarai's explosion runs on the same scales — "I became slight in her eyes" — and on a heavier word: "the wrong done to me is on you!" is chamasi, from chamas, the flood's word for violence (6:11), as if to say the household is filling with the old poison. "May Jehovah judge between me and you" — yishpot, from shaphat; hold that word, because the Judge of all the earth is two chapters away (18:25).

"And Sarai afflicted her." Abram, handed the quarrel, hands the girl back — "your slave-girl is in your hand; do to her what is good in your eyes" — and goes quiet. (Notice that neither he nor Sarai ever speaks Hagar's name; in the whole chapter only one voice will.) Then the verse the chapter turns on: vate'anneha, "she afflicted her" — the precise verb of the prophecy one chapter back, "they will be enslaved and afflicted" (15:13). Before Egypt afflicts Israel for four hundred years, Israel's own house afflicts an Egyptian. The Torah writes the reversal down and does not flinch — and Hagar flees (barach, the verb Exodus will use of Israel fleeing Egypt, Exodus 14:5): the Bible's first runaway slave runs from Abram's tents.

Verses 7–8 · מַלְאַךְ יְהוָה mal'akh YHWH

"The angel of Jehovah" — the first angel in the Bible, and he appears not to Abram but to a runaway Egyptian slave-girl. Mal'akh means simply messenger (the Greek angelos is the same word; wings and halos are later furniture — the word is used of human couriers too). Who is he? The text is deliberately double-edged: the messenger speaks in God's own first person ("I will greatly multiply," v10), and the narrator calls the speaker simply "Jehovah" (v13). Three readings, each with a pedigree: an envoy who bears his sender's very words and may be addressed as the sender, as ancient messengers were; a visible manifestation of Jehovah himself, the view of many commentators ancient and modern; and the later Christian reading that sees in "the angel of Jehovah" the pre-incarnate Son (the arguments both ways are catalogued on the Is Jesus God? page). The text slides between "the angel of Jehovah" and "Jehovah" without embarrassment, and this translation leaves the seam showing.

"On the way to Shur." Geography tells the story: Shur is the desert fronting Egypt's north-east frontier, and the road Hagar is on is the road home — an Egyptian, pregnant and alone, walking back toward Egypt. The angel's question is the Bible's third divine question to a human on the run — "Where are you?" (3:9), "Where is Abel your brother?" (4:9), and now "where have you come from, and where are you going?" She can answer only half of it: from the face of my mistress; about where to, silence. And notice — "Hagar, slave-girl of Sarai": the messenger is the first speaker in the chapter to call her by name.

Verses 9–12 · וְקָרָאת שְׁמוֹ יִשְׁמָעֵאל veqarat shemo Yishma'el

"Return… and submit." The hard verse first, and honestly: hit'anni is the reflexive of the very verb of verse 6 — "submit yourself," literally "let yourself be afflicted, under her hands." The angel does not pretend the house is safe; he sends her back into it, with a promise strapped to the order. Readers have wrestled with this ever since, and this translation leaves the difficulty standing (as it left "the iniquity of the Amorite" standing at 15:16). Note, too, the drumbeat: three verses in a row each restart "And the angel of Jehovah said to her" (vv 9, 10, 11) — three stacked oracles: an order, a promise, a name. Hebrew narrative repeats; this translation keeps all three.

The promise, and the name. "I will greatly multiply your offspring, so many they cannot be counted" — the counting verb of the stars ("count the stars, if you are able to count them," 15:5), the scale of the dust (13:16): the patriarchal promise itself, and Hagar is the only woman in Genesis to receive it from God directly. Then the Bible's first annunciation — "look — you are pregnant, and you shall bear a son, and you shall call his name…" — a formula that will return for Isaac (17:19), for Samson (Judges 13), in Isaiah's Immanuel sign (Isaiah 7:14), and at Nazareth (Luke 1:31). It begins with Hagar. The name is the sentence: Yishma-El, "God hears," given "for Jehovah has heard (shama) your affliction" — and "your affliction" (onyekh) is the anah root a third time, now in God's mouth: what Sarai dealt, God heard. Israel will one day be heard in the identical vocabulary: "I have seen the affliction of my people… and have heard their cry" (Exodus 3:7).

"A wild donkey of a man." Not a slur in the desert's own ears: the pere is the untamable free-ranger of the steppe — Job 39:5-8 sings its freedom (it "laughs at the city's commotion" and "hears no driver's shout") — and to a slave, whose child would otherwise be born property, "no one will ever hold his halter" is a promise. (KJV "he will be a wild man" loses the animal and reads like an insult; the Hebrew names the animal.) "His hand against all, and the hand of all against him" — the free life has its price — "and in the face of all his brothers he shall dwell": al-penei can mean "alongside, over against" his kin (as 25:18 will settle his descendants) or "in defiance of" them; the versions split, and the phrase likely winks both ways. One more thread: the woman fleeing "from the face of" her mistress (vv 6, 8) carries a son who will live "in the face of" everyone — nobody's runaway.

Verses 13–14 · אַתָּה אֵל רֳאִי attah El Ro'i

"You are El Ro'i." The astonishing thing, easy to read past: Hagar names God. Everywhere else in the Bible God announces his own names; here a foreign slave-girl coins one, and the text keeps it — she is the only person in the Bible who confers a name on God. El Ro'i — "God of seeing" — and the Hebrew holds two readings at once: "the God who sees me" and "the God whom I have seen." She means both, and the chapter has been building the word all along: slight in her eyes (vv 4–5), good in your eyes (v6), the spring — Hebrew ayin, the same word as eye (v7) — and now the God of seeing. The woman nobody in the household would name, names God: the God who saw her.

"Have I even here seen after the one who sees me?" The second half of verse 13 is famously rough, and this translation keeps the roughness rather than paving it. Word-for-word: "Have I even here (halom) seen after (acharei) the one who sees me?" The shelf scatters: KJV, Geneva and ASV "have I also here looked after him that seeth me"; Douay, translating the Vulgate, reads "seen the hinder parts of him that seeth me" — taking "after" as God's back, the way Moses will be shown "my back" because "no one may see my face and live" (Exodus 33:20-23); NIV (2011) smooths to "I have now seen the One who sees me"; and many scholars suspect a phrase like "God — and lived" has dropped out (compare "we shall surely die, for we have seen God," Judges 13:22). The astonishment is not in doubt, only its wording: I looked at the One looking at me — and I am still here.

"Beer-lahai-roi." The well keeps her sentence: Be'er-lachai-ro'i, "the well of the Living One who sees me" — a place-name minted from a slave-girl's theology, fixed "between Kadesh" (the En-mishpat of the war of the kings, 14:7) "and Bered" — a place named nowhere else, so the well is lost to the map (said plainly). And the story is not done with it: Isaac — the son who will displace Ishmael — will one day arrive from, and settle beside, this same well (24:62; 25:11). Hold that thought for eight chapters.

Verses 15–16 · וַיִּקְרָא אַבְרָם שֶׁם-בְּנוֹ … יִשְׁמָעֵאל vayiqra Avram shem-beno … Yishma'el

"And Abram called the name of his son… Ishmael." A quiet detail with a story inside it: the angel told Hagar to name the boy ("you shall call his name," v11 — the mother, unusually, as name-giver); verse 15 has Abram do it. Between the two verses sits an unrecorded conversation: she went back, and she told the household everything — and the name on Abram's lips means the desert revelation was received as a word from God. The ledger then closes in the census style of chapter 5: Abram was 86 — and the numbers square: 75 leaving Haran (12:4), ten years in Canaan (16:3), 86 at the birth. Thirteen silent years now pass between this verse and the next ("Abram was 99 years old," 17:1) — years in which Ishmael is Abram's only son, which is exactly the tension the next chapter walks into. Genesis will give Ishmael twelve princes (25:12-16) and a place beside Isaac at their father's grave (25:9); later tradition — Jewish, Christian, and Islamic — remembers him as ancestor of the Arab peoples. The closed scroll-break {ס} draws the line under the ledger.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "built up through her" (the ben/"build" pun kept, not smoothed to "obtain children"), "a wild donkey of a man" (the animal kept, against KJV's "wild man"), and verse 13 left rough ("seen after the one who sees me") rather than paved.

Where it goes its own way: "slave-girl" for shiphchah (where the shelf's "handmaid" upholsters the reality), "became slight in her eyes" (keeping the qalal scales visible), and "afflicted" kept identical across 16:6, 16:9 and 16:11 so the wire back to 15:13 stays live.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: anah, "afflict" — prophesied of Israel in Egypt (15:13), dealt first by Israel's house to an Egyptian; the star-count verb of 15:5 returns in "so many they cannot be counted"; chamas (6:11) resurfaces in Sarai's "the wrong done to me"; qalal — "the one who belittles you" (12:3) — trips on Hagar's contempt; "listened to the voice of" reaches back to the garden (3:17); and Kadesh was fixed on the map at 14:7. Planted: shaphat, "may Jehovah judge" (18:25 is coming); the annunciation formula ("you are pregnant, and you shall bear a son…") that returns at 17:19 and far beyond; Beer-lahai-roi, where Isaac will settle (24:62); and thirteen silent years until "Abram was 99."

Next installment: Genesis 17 — El Shaddai, the covenant in the flesh, and two new names: Abraham and Sarah.

Genesis 17 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
וַיְהִי אַבְרָם, בֶּן-תִּשְׁעִים שָׁנָה וְתֵשַׁע שָׁנִים; וַיֵּרָא יְהוָה אֶל-אַבְרָם, וַיֹּאמֶר אֵלָיו אֲנִי-אֵל שַׁדַּי--הִתְהַלֵּךְ לְפָנַי, וֶהְיֵה תָמִים.
And when Abram was 99 years old, Jehovah appeared to Abram and said to him, "I am El Shaddai. Walk before me, and be blameless,note
2

and I will set my covenant between me and you, and I will multiply you very, very much."note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–2 · אֲנִי-אֵל שַׁדַּי ani El Shaddai

Thirteen silent years. Between the last verse of chapter 16 (Abram 86) and the first verse here (Abram 99), the text says nothing at all — thirteen years pass in the width of a chapter break, with Ishmael growing up as the only son. Then "Jehovah appeared" — the second appearance (the first was at Shechem, 12:7) — and announces a new name: "I am El Shaddai." The traditional rendering "God Almighty" (KJV ASV NIV NWT; Douay "the Almighty God") is a guess inherited from the Greek and Latin Bibles (pantokratōr, omnipotens) — the Hebrew meaning is genuinely uncertain: "God of the mountain" (Akkadian šadû), "God of the steppe," or a fertility word (Genesis 49:25 blesses, in one breath, "Shaddai… blessings of the breasts (shaddayim) and of the womb" — apt, in a chapter about a barren couple made fruitful). Geneva alone reads "God all sufficient" (an old rabbinic etymology, she-dai, "who is enough"). MiSTeR keeps it as what it is — a name — untranslated.

"Walk before me, and be blameless." Two loaded words. Hithalekh is the walking-with-God verb of the early chapters — but Enoch and Noah walked with God (5:22, 6:9), and Abram is told to walk before him: not a stroll alongside, but a life lived in full view, out ahead, answerable. And "blameless" (tamim) is Noah's own word (6:9) — wholeness, integrity, soundness of a piece; not sinless perfection (the next chapters will show plenty of flaw). Unlike the covenant of chapter 15 — where Abram slept and God alone passed between the pieces — this covenant comes with commands attached: walk, be whole, keep, circumcise. The two chapters are a matched pair: the unconditional promise, and the answering obligation. "I will multiply you very, very much" — bimod me'od, the doubled "very" this translation keeps (as at the flood's "very, very" triumph, 7:19).

Verses 3–8 · וְהָיָה שִׁמְךָ אַבְרָהָם vehayah shimkha Avraham

Abram becomes Abraham. The first person in the Bible God renames. The text supplies its own gloss: Avraham because av hamon goyim, "father of a multitude of nations" — a sound-echo more than a strict etymology (to the philologists, Abraham is most likely a dialect variant of Abram, "exalted father"; the text is punning, and says so by its ki, "for"). Hear the long arc: Babel grasped at a name (11:4), God promised to make Abram's name great (12:2) — and now he remakes the name itself, folding the promise into the syllables a man hears every time he is called. From this verse on the narration never says "Abram" again. Note also who is speaking: verse 1 says Jehovah appeared; from verse 3 the speaker is God (Elohim) throughout — the divine-name alternation the flood chapters showed, visible again here; readings differ on why, and this translation prints what stands.

The everlasting covenant, and its formula. "Kings shall come out of you" — planted, for Judah and David, a long way downstream. The covenant is brit olam, an everlasting covenant — the bow's phrase (9:16) — and verses 7–8 speak the sentence the rest of the Bible will never stop repeating: "to be God to you… and I will be their God." This is the covenant formula's first appearance — the line that returns at the exodus ("I will take you as my people, and I will be your God," Exodus 6:7), in the law, the prophets, and at the very end ("they will be his people, and God himself will be with them," Revelation 21:3). It begins here, said to one old man. And the land is granted under a striking title: "the land of your sojournings" (megurekha, from the root of ger, "resident stranger" — 15:13's word) — an everlasting possession, promised to a man who owns not one field of it and lives in it as a guest.

Verses 9–14 · וְהָיָה לְאוֹת בְּרִית vehayah le'ot brit

"You shall keep my covenant." The verb is shamar — keep, guard, watch over: the human's garden vocation (2:15), the duty Cain disowned ("am I my brother's keeper?", 4:9) — now the covenant itself is the thing to keep. And its sign: as the bow was "the sign of the covenant" hung in the sky for God to see (9:12-13), this sign is cut into the body and carried — ot brit, the covenant made flesh, at eight days old. Circumcision itself was not new (Egyptians and other neighbors practiced it — Jeremiah 9:25-26 lists them); what is new is the meaning: not a rite of manhood at puberty or marriage, but a mark of membership, given to an infant who has done nothing and can promise nothing — grace before achievement, in the flesh.

The whole household — and the hard pun. The sign crosses the bloodline: "the one born in your house, and the one bought with money from any foreigner" — the household's slaves, that is; the text includes them in the covenant without blushing, and this translation names what they are rather than upholstering it. The covenant community begins as a mixed multitude. Then verse 14 closes on wordplay with teeth: Hebrew "cuts" covenants (karat brit, 15:18 — the cut animals), and the male who will not be cut "shall be cut off (venikhretah) from his people" — the penalty rhymes with the refusal. "He has broken my covenant" — hefar, annulled, made void. The closed break {ס} ends the paragraph.

Verses 15–19 · וַיִּפֹּל אַבְרָהָם עַל-פָּנָיו, וַיִּצְחָק vayippol Avraham al-panav, vayitschaq

Sarai becomes Sarah. The second renaming — and the first woman God renames. This time the text offers no gloss: both forms mean "princess" (Sarai the older spelling, Sarah the newer); the change is not in the meaning but in the standing — the next verse crowns it: "kings of peoples shall come from her." For the first time the promise is fastened explicitly to Sarah's own body — "I will give you a son from her" — and she is blessed twice in one verse (the Hebrew repeats; this translation keeps both). After chapter 16's workaround through Hagar, the covenant line runs, God insists, through the barren woman herself.

Abraham laughed. Face down before God — and laughing: vayitschaq. "Shall a child be born to a man a hundred years old?" The shelf all admit the laugh (KJV "fell upon his face, and laughed"), though the old Aramaic Targum softened it to "rejoiced" — while leaving Sarah's laugh in the next chapter as laughter, a double standard the commentators have enjoyed ever since. God is not offended; he is exact. "No — Sarah your wife shall bear you a son, and you shall call his name Isaac" — Yitschaq, "he laughs." The name is minted from the laugh itself: every time the boy is called, the father's incredulity — turned to joy — is spoken aloud. (The annunciation formula, "you shall call his name…," is the very syntax spoken over Ishmael at 16:11; the laughter, like the name, will run on — Sarah's turn comes at 18:12, and her "God has made laughter for me" at 21:6.) In between stands Abraham's counter-offer, tender and human: "If only Ishmael might live before you!" — he already has a son; let the one who exists be enough. Before you — the same "before" as "walk before me" (v1).

Verses 20–22 · וּלְיִשְׁמָעֵאל, שְׁמַעְתִּיךָ ule-Yishma'el, shema'tikha

"As for Ishmael — I have heard you." The pun pays the boy's name in full: Yishma-El means "God hears" (16:11), and God answers the father's plea with the name's own verb — shema'tikha, "I have heard you." Even in the sentence that passes him over, Ishmael's name is honored. And the blessing is not small: fruitful, multiplied "very, very much," twelve princes (Genesis will list them, 25:12-16), and "a great nation" — the very phrase of Abram's own call (12:2). Ishmael receives Genesis-12 language; what he does not receive is the covenant: "but my covenant I will establish with Isaac." The line is drawn gently and absolutely at once.

"At this appointed time next year." Mo'ed — the "appointed times" of creation's calendar (1:14): the God who installed the fixed times of sun and moon now fixes one for a birth. It is the first hard date ever put on the promise — after twenty-four years of "I will," a year on the clock. Then "God went up from Abraham" — the audience ends as formally as it began.

Verses 23–27 · בְּעֶצֶם הַיּוֹם הַזֶּה be'etsem hayom hazeh

"On that very same day." The phrase is a formula, and it has appeared once before: "on that very same day Noah entered the ark" (7:13) — the text's stamp for exact, unhesitating compliance. Abraham — who laughed on his face an hour ago in story-time — says not one recorded word; he takes Ishmael, the house-born and the money-bought, "every male among the men of Abraham's house," and does the thing, that day. The ledger then closes census-style, in the manner of chapter 5: Abraham 99, Ishmael 13 — a detail later tradition remembered: circumcision at thirteen remained the custom among Ishmael's Arab descendants, as the eighth day did in Israel. The first covenant community, counted on its first day, is a mixed multitude — one son, and a household of slaves and foreigners-by-birth, all bearing the same sign. The open break {פ} ends the chapter.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: the doubled "very, very much" kept all three times (vv 2, 6, 20), the doubled "I will bless her" kept (v16), and "walk before me and be blameless" with tamim rendered as at 6:9 (Noah's word).

Where it goes its own way: "El Shaddai" kept as a name, untranslated (the shelf's "Almighty" is a Greek-and-Latin guess; Geneva's lonely "God all sufficient" flagged), "father of a multitude" kept as the text's own sound-gloss on Abraham, and "cut off" left rhyming with the covenant that is "cut."

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: tamim and the walking verb from Noah and Enoch (6:9, 5:22 — with the shift from with God to before him); "everlasting covenant" and "the sign of the covenant" from the bow (9:12, 9:16); "on that very same day" from Noah's boarding (7:13); "I will make your name great" (12:2) fulfilled in the name itself; and "I have heard you" pays Ishmael's name (16:11), whose annunciation formula returns on Isaac. Planted: the covenant formula "I will be their God" (Exodus 6:7, and on to Revelation 21:3); kings from Abraham and Sarah; the laughter — Sarah's at 18:12, and "God has made laughter for me" at 21:6; "at this appointed time" (18:10, 14; 21:2); and the twelve princes of 25:12-16.

Next installment: Genesis 18 — three visitors at Mamre, Sarah's laugh, and "Shall not the Judge of all the earth do right?"

Genesis 18 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
וַיֵּרָא אֵלָיו יְהוָה, בְּאֵלֹנֵי מַמְרֵא; וְהוּא יֹשֵׁב פֶּתַח-הָאֹהֶל, כְּחֹם הַיּוֹם.
And Jehovah appeared to him by the oaks of Mamre, as he was sitting at the door of the tent in the heat of the day.note
2
וַיִּשָּׂא עֵינָיו, וַיַּרְא, וְהִנֵּה שְׁלֹשָׁה אֲנָשִׁים, נִצָּבִים עָלָיו; וַיַּרְא, וַיָּרָץ לִקְרָאתָם מִפֶּתַח הָאֹהֶל, וַיִּשְׁתַּחוּ, אָרְצָה.
And he lifted up his eyes and looked, and look — three men standing near him. When he saw them, he ran from the tent door to meet them and bowed to the ground,note
3
וַיֹּאמַר: אֲדֹנָי, אִם-נָא מָצָאתִי חֵן בְּעֵינֶיךָ--אַל-נָא תַעֲבֹר, מֵעַל עַבְדֶּךָ.
and said, "My Lord, if I have found favor in your eyes, do not pass your servant by.note
4
יֻקַּח-נָא מְעַט-מַיִם, וְרַחֲצוּ רַגְלֵיכֶם; וְהִשָּׁעֲנוּ, תַּחַת הָעֵץ.
Let a little water be brought — wash your feet, and rest yourselves under the tree,note
5
וְאֶקְחָה פַת-לֶחֶם וְסַעֲדוּ לִבְּכֶם, אַחַר תַּעֲבֹרוּ--כִּי-עַל-כֵּן עֲבַרְתֶּם, עַל-עַבְדְּכֶם; וַיֹּאמְרוּ, כֵּן תַּעֲשֶׂה כַּאֲשֶׁר דִּבַּרְתָּ.
and let me bring a piece of bread, so you can refresh yourselves — after that you may pass on, now that you have come by your servant." And they said, "Do just as you have said."note
6
וַיְמַהֵר אַבְרָהָם הָאֹהֱלָה, אֶל-שָׂרָה; וַיֹּאמֶר, מַהֲרִי שְׁלֹשׁ סְאִים קֶמַח סֹלֶת--לוּשִׁי, וַעֲשִׂי עֻגוֹת.
And Abraham hurried into the tent to Sarah and said, "Quick — three seahs of fine flour! Knead it, make cakes!"note
7
וְאֶל-הַבָּקָר, רָץ אַבְרָהָם; וַיִּקַּח בֶּן-בָּקָר רַךְ וָטוֹב, וַיִּתֵּן אֶל-הַנַּעַר, וַיְמַהֵר, לַעֲשׂוֹת אֹתוֹ.
And Abraham ran to the herd, and took a calf, tender and good, and gave it to the servant boy, who hurried to prepare it.note
8
וַיִּקַּח חֶמְאָה וְחָלָב, וּבֶן-הַבָּקָר אֲשֶׁר עָשָׂה, וַיִּתֵּן, לִפְנֵיהֶם; וְהוּא-עֹמֵד עֲלֵיהֶם תַּחַת הָעֵץ, וַיֹּאכֵלוּ.
And he took curds and milk and the calf he had prepared, and set it before them; and he stood over them under the tree while they ate.note
9
וַיֹּאמְרוּ אֵלָיו, אַיֵּה שָׂרָה אִשְׁתֶּךָ; וַיֹּאמֶר, הִנֵּה בָאֹהֶל.
And they said to him, "Where is Sarah your wife?" And he said, "Look — in the tent."note
10
וַיֹּאמֶר, שׁוֹב אָשׁוּב אֵלֶיךָ כָּעֵת חַיָּה, וְהִנֵּה-בֵן, לְשָׂרָה אִשְׁתֶּךָ; וְשָׂרָה שֹׁמַעַת פֶּתַח הָאֹהֶל, וְהוּא אַחֲרָיו.
And he said, "I will surely return to you at this time next year — and look, Sarah your wife shall have a son." And Sarah was listening at the tent door, which was behind him.note
11
וְאַבְרָהָם וְשָׂרָה זְקֵנִים, בָּאִים בַּיָּמִים; חָדַל לִהְיוֹת לְשָׂרָה, אֹרַח כַּנָּשִׁים.
Now Abraham and Sarah were old, well along in days; the way of women had ceased for Sarah.note
12
וַתִּצְחַק שָׂרָה, בְּקִרְבָּהּ לֵאמֹר: אַחֲרֵי בְלֹתִי הָיְתָה-לִּי עֶדְנָה, וַאדֹנִי זָקֵן.
So Sarah laughed within herself: "After I am worn out, shall I have delight — and my lord so old?"note
13
וַיֹּאמֶר יְהוָה, אֶל-אַבְרָהָם: לָמָּה זֶּה צָחֲקָה שָׂרָה לֵאמֹר, הַאַף אֻמְנָם אֵלֵד--וַאֲנִי זָקַנְתִּי.
And Jehovah said to Abraham, "Why did Sarah laugh and say, 'Shall I really bear a child, old as I am?'note
14
הֲיִפָּלֵא מֵיְהוָה, דָּבָר; לַמּוֹעֵד אָשׁוּב אֵלֶיךָ, כָּעֵת חַיָּה--וּלְשָׂרָה בֵן.
Is anything too wondrous for Jehovah? At the appointed time I will return to you — at this time next year — and Sarah shall have a son."note
15
וַתְּכַחֵשׁ שָׂרָה לֵאמֹר לֹא צָחַקְתִּי, כִּי יָרֵאָה; וַיֹּאמֶר לֹא, כִּי צָחָקְתְּ.
But Sarah denied it — "I did not laugh" — for she was afraid. And he said, "No — you did laugh."note
16
וַיָּקֻמוּ מִשָּׁם הָאֲנָשִׁים, וַיַּשְׁקִפוּ עַל-פְּנֵי סְדֹם; וְאַבְרָהָם--הֹלֵךְ עִמָּם, לְשַׁלְּחָם.
And the men rose from there and looked down over the face of Sodom; and Abraham walked with them, to send them on their way.note
17
וַיהוָה, אָמָר: הַמְכַסֶּה אֲנִי מֵאַבְרָהָם, אֲשֶׁר אֲנִי עֹשֶׂה.
And Jehovah said, "Shall I hide from Abraham what I am about to do,note
18
וְאַבְרָהָם--הָיוֹ יִהְיֶה לְגוֹי גָּדוֹל, וְעָצוּם; וְנִבְרְכוּ-בוֹ--כֹּל, גּוֹיֵי הָאָרֶץ.
when Abraham shall surely become a great and mighty nation, and through him all the nations of the earth will be blessed?note
19
כִּי יְדַעְתִּיו, לְמַעַן אֲשֶׁר יְצַוֶּה אֶת-בָּנָיו וְאֶת-בֵּיתוֹ אַחֲרָיו, וְשָׁמְרוּ דֶּרֶךְ יְהוָה, לַעֲשׂוֹת צְדָקָה וּמִשְׁפָּט--לְמַעַן, הָבִיא יְהוָה עַל-אַבְרָהָם, אֵת אֲשֶׁר-דִּבֶּר, עָלָיו.
For I have known him, so that he will command his children and his household after him, and they will keep the way of Jehovah, to do righteousness and justice — so that Jehovah may bring on Abraham what he has spoken concerning him."note
20
וַיֹּאמֶר יְהוָה, זַעֲקַת סְדֹם וַעֲמֹרָה כִּי-רָבָּה; וְחַטָּאתָם--כִּי כָבְדָה, מְאֹד.
And Jehovah said, "The outcry of Sodom and Gomorrah — how great it is! And their sin — how very heavy!note
21
אֵרְדָה-נָּא וְאֶרְאֶה, הַכְּצַעֲקָתָהּ הַבָּאָה אֵלַי עָשׂוּ כָּלָה; וְאִם-לֹא, אֵדָעָה.
Let me go down and see whether they have done entirely as the outcry that has come to me — and if not, I will know."note
22
וַיִּפְנוּ מִשָּׁם הָאֲנָשִׁים, וַיֵּלְכוּ סְדֹמָה; וְאַבְרָהָם--עוֹדֶנּוּ עֹמֵד, לִפְנֵי יְהוָה.
And the men turned from there and went toward Sodom, but Abraham remained standing before Jehovah.note
23
וַיִּגַּשׁ אַבְרָהָם, וַיֹּאמַר: הַאַף תִּסְפֶּה, צַדִּיק עִם-רָשָׁע.
And Abraham drew near and said, "Will you really sweep away the righteous with the wicked?note
24
אוּלַי יֵשׁ חֲמִשִּׁים צַדִּיקִם, בְּתוֹךְ הָעִיר; הַאַף תִּסְפֶּה וְלֹא-תִשָּׂא לַמָּקוֹם, לְמַעַן חֲמִשִּׁים הַצַּדִּיקִם אֲשֶׁר בְּקִרְבָּהּ.
Suppose there are fifty righteous within the city — will you really sweep it away, and not spare the place for the sake of the fifty righteous inside it?note
25
חָלִלָה לְּךָ מֵעֲשֹׂת כַּדָּבָר הַזֶּה, לְהָמִית צַדִּיק עִם-רָשָׁע, וְהָיָה כַצַּדִּיק, כָּרָשָׁע; חָלִלָה לָּךְ--הֲשֹׁפֵט כָּל-הָאָרֶץ, לֹא יַעֲשֶׂה מִשְׁפָּט.
Far be it from you to do such a thing — to put the righteous to death with the wicked, so that the righteous fare as the wicked. Far be it from you! Shall not the Judge of all the earth do justice?"note
26
וַיֹּאמֶר יְהוָה, אִם-אֶמְצָא בִסְדֹם חֲמִשִּׁים צַדִּיקִם בְּתוֹךְ הָעִיר--וְנָשָׂאתִי לְכָל-הַמָּקוֹם, בַּעֲבוּרָם.
And Jehovah said, "If I find in Sodom fifty righteous within the city, I will spare the whole place for their sake."note
27
וַיַּעַן אַבְרָהָם, וַיֹּאמַר: הִנֵּה-נָא הוֹאַלְתִּי לְדַבֵּר אֶל-אֲדֹנָי, וְאָנֹכִי עָפָר וָאֵפֶר.
And Abraham answered, "Look now — I have taken it on myself to speak to my Lord, and I am dust and ashes.note
28
אוּלַי יַחְסְרוּן חֲמִשִּׁים הַצַּדִּיקִם, חֲמִשָּׁה--הֲתַשְׁחִית בַּחֲמִשָּׁה, אֶת-כָּל-הָעִיר; וַיֹּאמֶר, לֹא אַשְׁחִית, אִם-אֶמְצָא שָׁם, אַרְבָּעִים וַחֲמִשָּׁה.
Suppose the fifty righteous fall short by five — will you ruin the whole city for five?" And he said, "I will not ruin it, if I find there forty-five."note
29
וַיֹּסֶף עוֹד לְדַבֵּר אֵלָיו, וַיֹּאמַר, אוּלַי יִמָּצְאוּן שָׁם, אַרְבָּעִים; וַיֹּאמֶר לֹא אֶעֱשֶׂה, בַּעֲבוּר הָאַרְבָּעִים.
And once more he spoke to him: "Suppose forty are found there?" And he said, "I will not do it, for the sake of the forty."note
30
וַיֹּאמֶר אַל-נָא יִחַר לַאדֹנָי, וַאֲדַבֵּרָה--אוּלַי יִמָּצְאוּן שָׁם, שְׁלֹשִׁים; וַיֹּאמֶר לֹא אֶעֱשֶׂה, אִם-אֶמְצָא שָׁם שְׁלֹשִׁים.
And he said, "Let not my Lord be angry if I speak — suppose thirty are found there?" And he said, "I will not do it, if I find thirty there."note
31
וַיֹּאמֶר, הִנֵּה-נָא הוֹאַלְתִּי לְדַבֵּר אֶל-אֲדֹנָי--אוּלַי יִמָּצְאוּן שָׁם, עֶשְׂרִים; וַיֹּאמֶר לֹא אַשְׁחִית, בַּעֲבוּר הָעֶשְׂרִים.
And he said, "Look now — I have taken it on myself to speak to my Lord. Suppose twenty are found there?" And he said, "I will not ruin it, for the sake of the twenty."note
32
וַיֹּאמֶר אַל-נָא יִחַר לַאדֹנָי, וַאֲדַבְּרָה אַךְ-הַפַּעַם--אוּלַי יִמָּצְאוּן שָׁם, עֲשָׂרָה; וַיֹּאמֶר לֹא אַשְׁחִית, בַּעֲבוּר הָעֲשָׂרָה.
And he said, "Let not my Lord be angry if I speak just this once more — suppose ten are found there?" And he said, "I will not ruin it, for the sake of the ten."note
33
וַיֵּלֶךְ יְהוָה--כַּאֲשֶׁר כִּלָּה, לְדַבֵּר אֶל-אַבְרָהָם; וְאַבְרָהָם, שָׁב לִמְקֹמוֹ.
And Jehovah went his way, when he had finished speaking with Abraham; and Abraham returned to his place.note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–8 · שְׁלֹשָׁה אֲנָשִׁים sheloshah anashim

"Three men." A new reading-portion opens here (Vayera, "and he appeared") — Jehovah's third appearance (12:7; 17:1), at the oaks of Mamre, Abraham's home ground since chapter 13. But what Abraham sees is "three men," and the chapter slides between "Jehovah," "he," and "the men" without ever explaining itself; the arithmetic resolves only at 19:1, where "the two angels came to Sodom" — so the oldest readings take one of the three as Jehovah himself (the one who speaks from verse 10 on) and two as his messengers. The traditions diverge from there, each with a pedigree: the Talmud makes all three angels, one per errand (to announce Isaac, to heal, to destroy); most Christian readers follow the Jehovah-plus-two reading (see the angel note at 16:7); and the icon tradition — Rublev's famous Trinity — reads the three visitors as a picture of the triune God, a devotional reading the text itself never makes. This translation reports the seam and does not vote. One small crux inside it: "My Lord" (v3) is pointed by the Masoretes as the holy Adonai — addressed to God — though with other vowels the same letters read "my lords," plain courtesy to three strangers. The pointing is a reading decision a thousand years younger than the consonants; both are reported here — and the shelf itself splits over it: KJV prints "My Lord" (the divine title), ASV prints "my lord" — lowercase, plain courtesy — Douay, translating the Vulgate's Domine, sides with the title, and the NWT prints the name itself, "Jehovah," counting this verse among the 134 places where, by the scribal tradition's own lists, an original YHWH was exchanged for Adonai. One vowel-point, four Bibles, three readings.

The hospitality. Count the verbs: Abraham runs (v2), hurries (v6), says "Quick!" (v6), runs again (v7), and the boy hurries (v7) — a 99-year-old, in the heat of the day (and, the rabbis add, still recovering from chapter 17's surgery — tradition, not text). He promises "a little water… a piece of bread" and delivers a feast: three seahs of fine flour (several gallons of it), a tender calf, curds and milk — the understatement-then-abundance of real hospitality. (KJV domesticates the seahs to "three measures of fine meal"; NIV, like this translation, keeps the Hebrew measure.) "If I have found favor in your eyes" is Noah's idiom (6:8). And "they ate" — angels, eating — made later tradition squirm (Josephus and the Targums say they only seemed to eat; the angel of Tobit 12:19 confesses as much); the text itself is unbothered. From this scene the whole tradition of sacred hospitality descends — "some have entertained angels unawares" (Hebrews 13:2) is about this very afternoon.

Verses 9–15 · וַתִּצְחַק שָׂרָה בְּקִרְבָּהּ vatitschaq Sarah beqirbah

Sarah's laugh. "Where is Sarah your wife?" — the visitors know her name, and her new one. The promise gets its clock again: "at this time next year," and in verse 14 "at the appointed time" — mo'ed, the very word of 17:21. Sarah, listening at the tent door, "laughed within herself" — as Abraham laughed on his face a chapter ago (17:17); the boy will be named for both laughs. Her monologue is worn and wry: "After I am worn out" (beloti — the word for worn-out clothes) "shall I have delight" — ednah, almost certainly the root of Eden: shall the garden's word come back, at ninety? — "and my lord so old?" KJV keeps the old music — "After I am waxed old shall I have pleasure, my lord being old also?" — where TLB trades the idiom for the scoff: "A woman my age?"

The merciful misquote, and the denial. When Jehovah repeats her words to Abraham, he edits them: she said "my lord is old"; he reports "shall I bear a child, old as I am?" The rabbis noticed (Bava Metzia 87a) and drew the lesson: even God bent a quotation for peace between husband and wife. Then the question that hangs over the whole chapter — "Is anything too wondrous for Jehovah?" Pala is the wonder-word (KJV and NIV alike read "too hard," which flattens it); Jeremiah will ask it back to God almost word for word (Jeremiah 32:17, 27). And the scene ends on the most human exchange in Genesis: "I did not laugh" — for she was afraid — "No — you did laugh." The fear is recorded without comment, and the laugh is not scolded away: it is kept, and in three chapters Sarah will name it out loud with joy ("God has made laughter for me," 21:6).

Verses 16–21 · זַעֲקַת סְדֹם וַעֲמֹרָה za'aqat Sedom va'Amorah

"Shall I hide from Abraham what I am about to do?" The men rise and look down over the face of Sodom — and the narration opens the divine deliberation to the reader. Abraham is brought into the counsel because of what he is for: "I have known him" — yada, the intimacy word (4:1); chosen, befriended — "so that he will command his children… to keep (shamar) the way of Jehovah, to do righteousness and justice" (tzedaqah u-mishpat). The righteousness reckoned to him (15:6) has become the family's job description, and the old promise "all the nations of the earth will be blessed through him" (12:3) is restated as the very reason he must be shown how judgment works. (The shelf divides over that verb like a seminar: ASV translates the word — "for I have known him" — NIV the meaning: "for I have chosen him." This translation keeps the word and lets the meaning ride inside it.)

The outcry, and the going-down. "The outcry of Sodom and Gomorrah" — za'aqah, the legal scream of the wronged: the word-family of Abel's blood "crying out from the ground" (4:10), and one day of Israel's own cry in Egypt (Exodus 3:7, 9). Note what is not said: Sodom's sin is not named here — what reaches heaven is its victims' voices (when Ezekiel finally names the charge, it is "pride, plenty of bread… and she did not strengthen the hand of the poor and needy," Ezekiel 16:49). Then: "Let me go down and see" — the exact procedure of Babel (11:5, 7). Heaven does not sentence on hearsay; judgment comes only after inspection — "and if not, I will know." (The clause "whether they have done entirely as the outcry" is compressed, rough Hebrew — asu khalah — and every version paraphrases it; this one keeps it close and lets it be rough.)

Verses 22–26 · הֲשֹׁפֵט כָּל-הָאָרֶץ, לֹא יַעֲשֶׂה מִשְׁפָּט hashophet kol-ha'arets lo ya'aseh mishpat

A corrected verse — says the margin itself. "Abraham remained standing before Jehovah." The Masoretes preserve a remarkable tradition about this half-verse: it is counted among the tiqqune soferim, the "corrections of the scribes" — by that tradition the sentence originally ran the other way, "Jehovah remained standing before Abraham" (waiting, while the two went on toward Sodom), and early scribes reversed it as too bold: one does not keep God standing before a man. This translation prints the received text and reports the margin — behind our verse may stand an even more daring one.

The audacity. "And Abraham drew near" — courtroom language — and files the Bible's first argument with God: "Will you really sweep away the righteous with the wicked?… Far be it from you!" — twice — "Shall not the Judge of all the earth do justice?" Mishpat: the word Sarai hurled in anger ("may Jehovah judge between me and you," 16:5), the name of the desert spring (En-mishpat, 14:7), now aimed at heaven itself, by a man arguing from God's own character against God's announced plan. English owes its famous cadence here to the KJV — "Shall not the Judge of all the earth do right?" (Geneva, a generation earlier: "the Judge of all the world") — but the Hebrew's closing word is the noun mishpat itself, so this translation reads "do justice" and keeps the echoes audible. And heaven takes the point without offense: "If I find in Sodom fifty righteous, I will spare the whole place for their sake" — "spare" is nasa, to lift, to carry (the load-bearing verb of 13:6): forgiveness pictured as the innocent few carrying the town.

Verses 27–33 · וְאָנֹכִי עָפָר וָאֵפֶר ve'anokhi afar va'efer

"Dust and ashes." The negotiation runs 50 → 45 → 40 → 30 → 20 → 10, each round more deferential ("let not my Lord be angry… just this once more"), each granted without a moment's hesitation. Abraham's self-description is a sound-pun the English can nearly keep: afar va'efer, "dust and ashes" — the dust he was formed from (2:7) and sentenced to (3:19), owned at last in the first person. And listen to his verb: "will you ruin the whole city?" — shachat, the flood's own word (6:11-13). Abraham is asking, in the flood's vocabulary, whether God is about to do that again — and the answer, this time, is measured: inspection first, and mercy at every step of the count.

Why stop at ten? The text doesn't say. The traditions guess: ten as the smallest viable community (the synagogue's quorum of ten, the minyan, is traced to this verse); or below ten, the righteous can be carried out rather than the city spared — which is exactly what the next chapter does with one family. What the exchange establishes, it establishes for good: the Judge of all the earth accepts audit; mercy outbids arithmetic six rounds running; and the man promised the whole land spends his standing on a city that isn't his — one he has already rescued once (ch. 14), and where his nephew lives. "And Jehovah went his way… and Abraham returned to his place" — the court adjourns; the verdict waits on the inspection, next chapter.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: the hurry-verbs of the hospitality kept at full speed, "dust and ashes" with its near-rhyme, the doubled "Far be it from you!", and the count-down left as plain numbers.

Where it goes its own way: "too wondrous" for ha-yippale (the shelf's "too hard" flattens the wonder-word), "delight" for ednah with the Eden root flagged, "ruin" for shachat so the flood's verb stays audible, and the tiqqun soferim at verse 22 reported rather than hidden.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: Sarah's laugh answers Abraham's (17:17); "the appointed time" repeats (17:21); "may Jehovah judge between me and you" (16:5) becomes "shall not the Judge of all the earth do justice?"; the outcry rises like Abel's blood (4:10); "let me go down and see" is Babel's procedure (11:5); "dust" comes home in the first person (3:19); and "ruin" is the flood's verb (6:11). Planted: "at this time next year" (21:2 will keep it); Sarah's "God has made laughter for me" (21:6); the outcry that will one day be Israel's (Exodus 3:7); and the rescue-of-the-few, due immediately — next chapter.

Next installment: Genesis 19 — Sodom's last night: the two angels at the gate, Lot's terrible bargain, and the fire.

Genesis 19 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
וַיָּבֹאוּ שְׁנֵי הַמַּלְאָכִים סְדֹמָה, בָּעֶרֶב, וְלוֹט, יֹשֵׁב בְּשַׁעַר-סְדֹם; וַיַּרְא-לוֹט וַיָּקָם לִקְרָאתָם, וַיִּשְׁתַּחוּ אַפַּיִם אָרְצָה.
And the two angels came to Sodom in the evening, as Lot was sitting in the gate of Sodom. When Lot saw them, he rose to meet them and bowed with his face to the ground,note
2
וַיֹּאמֶר הִנֶּה נָּא-אֲדֹנַי, סוּרוּ נָא אֶל-בֵּית עַבְדְּכֶם וְלִינוּ וְרַחֲצוּ רַגְלֵיכֶם, וְהִשְׁכַּמְתֶּם, וַהֲלַכְתֶּם לְדַרְכְּכֶם; וַיֹּאמְרוּ לֹּא, כִּי בָרְחוֹב נָלִין.
and said, "Look now, my lords — turn aside, please, to your servant's house; spend the night, wash your feet, and rise early and go on your way." And they said, "No — we will spend the night in the square."note
3
וַיִּפְצַר-בָּם מְאֹד--וַיָּסֻרוּ אֵלָיו, וַיָּבֹאוּ אֶל-בֵּיתוֹ; וַיַּעַשׂ לָהֶם מִשְׁתֶּה, וּמַצּוֹת אָפָה וַיֹּאכֵלוּ.
But he pressed them hard, and they turned aside to him and came into his house; and he made them a feast, and baked unleavened bread, and they ate.note
4
טֶרֶם, יִשְׁכָּבוּ, וְאַנְשֵׁי הָעִיר אַנְשֵׁי סְדֹם נָסַבּוּ עַל-הַבַּיִת, מִנַּעַר וְעַד-זָקֵן: כָּל-הָעָם, מִקָּצֶה.
Before they lay down, the men of the city — the men of Sodom — surrounded the house, young and old, all the people, from every quarter,note
5
וַיִּקְרְאוּ אֶל-לוֹט וַיֹּאמְרוּ לוֹ, אַיֵּה הָאֲנָשִׁים אֲשֶׁר-בָּאוּ אֵלֶיךָ הַלָּיְלָה; הוֹצִיאֵם אֵלֵינוּ, וְנֵדְעָה אֹתָם.
And they called to Lot: "Where are the men who came to you tonight? Bring them out to us, that we may know them."note
6
וַיֵּצֵא אֲלֵהֶם לוֹט, הַפֶּתְחָה; וְהַדֶּלֶת, סָגַר אַחֲרָיו.
And Lot went out to them at the doorway, and shut the door behind him,note
7
וַיֹּאמַר: אַל-נָא אַחַי, תָּרֵעוּ.
and said, "Please, my brothers — do not do this evil.note
8
הִנֵּה-נָא לִי שְׁתֵּי בָנוֹת, אֲשֶׁר לֹא-יָדְעוּ אִישׁ--אוֹצִיאָה-נָּא אֶתְהֶן אֲלֵיכֶם, וַעֲשׂוּ לָהֶן כַּטּוֹב בְּעֵינֵיכֶם; רַק לָאֲנָשִׁים הָאֵל, אַל-תַּעֲשׂוּ דָבָר, כִּי-עַל-כֵּן בָּאוּ, בְּצֵל קֹרָתִי.
Look now — I have two daughters who have not known a man. Let me bring them out to you, and do to them what is good in your eyes; only do nothing to these men, for they have come under the shadow of my roof."note
9
וַיֹּאמְרוּ גֶּשׁ-הָלְאָה, וַיֹּאמְרוּ הָאֶחָד בָּא-לָגוּר וַיִּשְׁפֹּט שָׁפוֹט--עַתָּה, נָרַע לְךָ מֵהֶם; וַיִּפְצְרוּ בָאִישׁ בְּלוֹט מְאֹד, וַיִּגְּשׁוּ לִשְׁבֹּר הַדָּלֶת.
But they said, "Stand back!" And they said, "This one came here to sojourn, and now he keeps playing the judge! Now we will deal worse with you than with them." And they pressed hard against the man, against Lot, and moved in to break down the door.note
10
וַיִּשְׁלְחוּ הָאֲנָשִׁים אֶת-יָדָם, וַיָּבִיאוּ אֶת-לוֹט אֲלֵיהֶם הַבָּיְתָה; וְאֶת-הַדֶּלֶת, סָגָרוּ.
But the men reached out their hands and pulled Lot in to them, into the house, and shut the door;note
11
וְאֶת-הָאֲנָשִׁים אֲשֶׁר-פֶּתַח הַבַּיִת, הִכּוּ בַּסַּנְוֵרִים, מִקָּטֹן, וְעַד-גָּדוֹל; וַיִּלְאוּ, לִמְצֹא הַפָּתַח.
and the men at the doorway of the house they struck with blindness, small and great alike, so that they wore themselves out groping for the doorway.note
12
וַיֹּאמְרוּ הָאֲנָשִׁים אֶל-לוֹט, עֹד מִי-לְךָ פֹה--חָתָן וּבָנֶיךָ וּבְנֹתֶיךָ, וְכֹל אֲשֶׁר-לְךָ בָּעִיר: הוֹצֵא, מִן-הַמָּקוֹם.
And the men said to Lot, "Who else do you have here? A son-in-law, your sons, your daughters — whomever you have in the city — bring them out of this place.note
13
כִּי-מַשְׁחִתִים אֲנַחְנוּ, אֶת-הַמָּקוֹם הַזֶּה: כִּי-גָדְלָה צַעֲקָתָם אֶת-פְּנֵי יְהוָה, וַיְשַׁלְּחֵנוּ יְהוָה לְשַׁחֲתָהּ.
For we are about to ruin this place, for the outcry against them has grown great before Jehovah, and Jehovah has sent us to ruin it."note
14
וַיֵּצֵא לוֹט וַיְדַבֵּר אֶל-חֲתָנָיו לֹקְחֵי בְנֹתָיו, וַיֹּאמֶר קוּמוּ צְּאוּ מִן-הַמָּקוֹם הַזֶּה, כִּי-מַשְׁחִית יְהוָה, אֶת-הָעִיר; וַיְהִי כִמְצַחֵק, בְּעֵינֵי חֲתָנָיו.
So Lot went out and spoke to his sons-in-law, who were to marry his daughters: "Up — get out of this place, for Jehovah is about to ruin the city!" But in his sons-in-law's eyes, he seemed like one who was joking.note
15
וּכְמוֹ הַשַּׁחַר עָלָה, וַיָּאִיצוּ הַמַּלְאָכִים בְּלוֹט לֵאמֹר: קוּם קַח אֶת-אִשְׁתְּךָ וְאֶת-שְׁתֵּי בְנֹתֶיךָ, הַנִּמְצָאֹת--פֶּן-תִּסָּפֶה, בַּעֲוֺן הָעִיר.
As dawn was breaking, the angels urged Lot on: "Up — take your wife and your two daughters who are here, or you will be swept away in the punishment of the city."note
16
וַיִּתְמַהְמָהּ--וַיַּחֲזִיקוּ הָאֲנָשִׁים בְּיָדוֹ וּבְיַד-אִשְׁתּוֹ וּבְיַד שְׁתֵּי בְנֹתָיו, בְּחֶמְלַת יְהוָה עָלָיו; וַיֹּצִאֻהוּ וַיַּנִּחֻהוּ, מִחוּץ לָעִיר.
But he lingered — and the men seized his hand, and his wife's hand, and the hands of his two daughters, in the compassion of Jehovah on him; and they brought him out and set him down outside the city.note
17
וַיְהִי כְהוֹצִיאָם אֹתָם הַחוּצָה, וַיֹּאמֶר הִמָּלֵט עַל-נַפְשֶׁךָ--אַל-תַּבִּיט אַחֲרֶיךָ, וְאַל-תַּעֲמֹד בְּכָל-הַכִּכָּר: הָהָרָה הִמָּלֵט, פֶּן-תִּסָּפֶה.
And when they had brought them outside, he said, "Escape for your life! Do not look behind you, and do not stop anywhere in the plain. Escape to the hills, or you will be swept away."note
18
וַיֹּאמֶר לוֹט, אֲלֵהֶם: אַל-נָא, אֲדֹנָי.
And Lot said to them, "Oh no, my Lord —note
19

Look now — your servant has found favor in your eyes, and great is the kindness you have shown me in keeping my life alive. But I — I cannot escape to the hills; the disaster will overtake me, and I will die.note
20
הִנֵּה-נָא הָעִיר הַזֹּאת קְרֹבָה, לָנוּס שָׁמָּה--וְהִוא מִצְעָר; אִמָּלְטָה נָּא שָׁמָּה, הֲלֹא מִצְעָר הִוא--וּתְחִי נַפְשִׁי.
Look — this town is near enough to flee to, and it is a little one. Let me escape there — is it not a little one? — and my life will be saved."note
21
וַיֹּאמֶר אֵלָיו--הִנֵּה נָשָׂאתִי פָנֶיךָ, גַּם לַדָּבָר הַזֶּה: לְבִלְתִּי הָפְכִּי אֶת-הָעִיר, אֲשֶׁר דִּבַּרְתָּ.
And he said to him, "Look — I have granted you this favor too: I will not overthrow the town of which you have spoken.note
22
מַהֵר, הִמָּלֵט שָׁמָּה, כִּי לֹא אוּכַל לַעֲשׂוֹת דָּבָר, עַד-בֹּאֲךָ שָׁמָּה; עַל-כֵּן קָרָא שֵׁם-הָעִיר, צוֹעַר.
Hurry — escape there, for I can do nothing until you arrive there." Therefore the name of the town was called Zoar.note
23
הַשֶּׁמֶשׁ, יָצָא עַל-הָאָרֶץ; וְלוֹט, בָּא צֹעֲרָה.
The sun had risen over the earth when Lot came to Zoar.note
24
וַיהוָה, הִמְטִיר עַל-סְדֹם וְעַל-עֲמֹרָה--גָּפְרִית וָאֵשׁ: מֵאֵת יְהוָה, מִן-הַשָּׁמָיִם.

and he overthrew those cities, and all the plain, and all the inhabitants of the cities, and what grew from the ground.note

But his wife looked back from behind him — and she became a pillar of salt.note
27
וַיַּשְׁכֵּם אַבְרָהָם, בַּבֹּקֶר: אֶל-הַמָּקוֹם--אֲשֶׁר-עָמַד שָׁם, אֶת-פְּנֵי יְהוָה.
And Abraham rose early in the morning and went to the place where he had stood before Jehovah,note
28
וַיַּשְׁקֵף, עַל-פְּנֵי סְדֹם וַעֲמֹרָה, וְעַל-כָּל-פְּנֵי, אֶרֶץ הַכִּכָּר; וַיַּרְא, וְהִנֵּה עָלָה קִיטֹר הָאָרֶץ, כְּקִיטֹר, הַכִּבְשָׁן.
and he looked down over the face of Sodom and Gomorrah, and over the whole face of the land of the plain — and he saw, and look: the smoke of the land went up like the smoke of a kiln.note

וַיְהִי, בְּשַׁחֵת אֱלֹהִים אֶת-עָרֵי הַכִּכָּר, וַיִּזְכֹּר אֱלֹהִים, אֶת-אַבְרָהָם; וַיְשַׁלַּח אֶת-לוֹט, מִתּוֹךְ הַהֲפֵכָה, בַּהֲפֹךְ אֶת-הֶעָרִים, אֲשֶׁר-יָשַׁב בָּהֵן לוֹט.
And so it was, when God ruined the cities of the plain, that God remembered Abraham — and sent Lot out of the midst of the overthrow, when he overthrew the cities where Lot had lived.note
30
וַיַּעַל לוֹט מִצּוֹעַר וַיֵּשֶׁב בָּהָר, וּשְׁתֵּי בְנֹתָיו עִמּוֹ, כִּי יָרֵא, לָשֶׁבֶת בְּצוֹעַר; וַיֵּשֶׁב, בַּמְּעָרָה--הוּא, וּשְׁתֵּי בְנֹתָיו.
And Lot went up from Zoar and lived in the hills, his two daughters with him, for he was afraid to stay in Zoar; and he lived in a cave, he and his two daughters.note
31
וַתֹּאמֶר הַבְּכִירָה אֶל-הַצְּעִירָה, אָבִינוּ זָקֵן; וְאִישׁ אֵין בָּאָרֶץ לָבוֹא עָלֵינוּ, כְּדֶרֶךְ כָּל-הָאָרֶץ.
And the firstborn said to the younger, "Our father is old, and there is no man on earth to come in to us, after the way of all the earth.note
32
לְכָה נַשְׁקֶה אֶת-אָבִינוּ יַיִן, וְנִשְׁכְּבָה עִמּוֹ; וּנְחַיֶּה מֵאָבִינוּ, זָרַע.
Come — let us give our father wine to drink, and lie with him, and keep offspring alive from our father."note
33
וַתַּשְׁקֶיןָ אֶת-אֲבִיהֶן יַיִן, בַּלַּיְלָה הוּא; וַתָּבֹא הַבְּכִירָה וַתִּשְׁכַּב אֶת-אָבִיהָ, וְלֹא-יָדַע בְּשִׁכְבָהּ וּבְקוּמָהּ.
So they gave their father wine to drink that night, and the firstborn went in and lay with her father; and he knew nothing of her lying down or of her rising.note
34
וַיְהִי, מִמָּחֳרָת, וַתֹּאמֶר הַבְּכִירָה אֶל-הַצְּעִירָה, הֵן-שָׁכַבְתִּי אֶמֶשׁ אֶת-אָבִי; נַשְׁקֶנּוּ יַיִן גַּם-הַלַּיְלָה, וּבֹאִי שִׁכְבִי עִמּוֹ, וּנְחַיֶּה מֵאָבִינוּ, זָרַע.
And the next day the firstborn said to the younger, "Look — last night I lay with my father. Let us give him wine to drink tonight also; then go in and lie with him, and we will keep offspring alive from our father."note
35
וַתַּשְׁקֶיןָ גַּם בַּלַּיְלָה הַהוּא, אֶת-אֲבִיהֶן--יָיִן; וַתָּקָם הַצְּעִירָה וַתִּשְׁכַּב עִמּוֹ, וְלֹא-יָדַע בְּשִׁכְבָהּ וּבְקֻמָהּ.
So they gave their father wine to drink that night also, and the younger rose and lay with him; and he knew nothing of her lying down or of her rising.note
36
וַתַּהֲרֶיןָ שְׁתֵּי בְנוֹת-לוֹט, מֵאֲבִיהֶן.
And both of Lot's daughters became pregnant by their father.note
37
וַתֵּלֶד הַבְּכִירָה בֵּן, וַתִּקְרָא שְׁמוֹ מוֹאָב: הוּא אֲבִי-מוֹאָב, עַד-הַיּוֹם.
And the firstborn bore a son and called his name Moab; he is the father of Moab to this day.note
38
וְהַצְּעִירָה גַם-הִוא יָלְדָה בֵּן, וַתִּקְרָא שְׁמוֹ בֶּן-עַמִּי: הוּא אֲבִי בְנֵי-עַמּוֹן, עַד-הַיּוֹם. {ס}
And the younger, she too bore a son, and called his name Ben-Ammi; he is the father of the children of Ammon to this day.note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–3 · שְׁנֵי הַמַּלְאָכִים shenei hamal'akhim

"The two angels." Chapter 18's arithmetic quietly resolves: three visitors came to Mamre; two arrive at Sodom — the third, on the oldest reading, was Jehovah, who stayed behind in the road to hear Abraham's argument (18:22) and "went his way" when it ended. Lot is "sitting in the gate of Sodom" — the gate is the ancient city's forum and courtroom, where elders sit and cases are heard: the man who pitched his tent toward Sodom (13:12) and was living in it by chapter 14 now has a seat in its civic life. The drift is complete.

The mirror. Lot's welcome is written deliberately against his uncle's, one chapter back: he sees, rises, bows, presses, feasts — a good host, genuinely. But every detail is a shadow-version: Mamre's blazing noon becomes Sodom's evening; the tent becomes a city house; and where Sarah baked rich cakes, Lot bakes unleavened bread (matzot) — the bread of haste, and its first appearance in the Bible: the same flat loaves will mark the other great rescue-by-night, out of Egypt (Exodus 12). "We will spend the night in the square," the angels first answer — in Sodom, an open threat the reader doesn't yet understand; Lot does, and presses them hard.

Verses 4–11 · וְנֵדְעָה אֹתָם venede'ah otam

"That we may know them." The verb is yada — Hebrew's own word for intimacy ("the man knew his wife," 4:1), and the very word of Abraham's election two verses back across the chapter seam: "I have known him" (18:19). One verb, covenant and violation. The demand is the sexual assault of guests, and the text makes the city's guilt total on purpose: "young and old, all the people, from every quarter" — the inspection of 18:21 has arrived, and there are not ten. The shelf splits over the verb by translation philosophy: KJV, Geneva and ASV keep it — "that we may know them" — while NIV spells it out ("so that we can have sex with them") and the NWT likewise ("have intercourse with them"). This translation keeps "know," not from delicacy but for the echo: spell the verb away, and the terrible rhyme with "I have known him" goes with it.

Lot's terrible bargain. "I have two daughters… do to them what is good in your eyes." The text reports it and does not approve it: hospitality's sacred duty ("they have come under the shadow of my roof") has been twisted into a father's unforgivable arithmetic, and the chapter will not let it go unanswered — what Lot offered of his daughters, his daughters will take of him, in the cave at the chapter's end. The mob's sneer, meanwhile, is loaded with chapter 18: "This one came here to sojourn" — a ger, a resident alien, 15:13's word — "and now he keeps playing the judge!" (vayishpot shaphot, the doubled verb of 18:25 — KJV renders the doubling "he will needs be a judge") — they mock the amateur judge at the door while the Judge of all the earth stands inside it. The angels end the scene: Lot pulled in, the door shut, and the mob struck with blindness (sanverim, a rare word — perhaps a dazzling — found again only at 2 Kings 6:18), and still they "wore themselves out groping for the doorway": blind, and trying to the last.

Verses 12–14 · וַיְהִי כִמְצַחֵק vayehi khimtsacheq

"Like one who was joking." The angels state their commission in the chapter's now-familiar vocabulary — "we are about to ruin this place" (shachat, the flood's verb, twice in one verse), "for the outcry has grown great before Jehovah" (18:20's word, quoted back). But the verse to hear is the last one: to his sons-in-law Lot "seemed like one who was joking" — metsacheq, the laugh-root, tsachaq. Abraham laughed face-down (17:17); Sarah laughed behind the tent flap (18:12); and now the same sound turns fatal — doom announced in Sodom lands as a punch line. Three laughs in three chapters: incredulous joy, frightened wonder, and unseriousness unto death. (The root has one more dark turn coming, at 21:9.) The sons-in-law stay, and are never mentioned again.

Verses 15–22 · וַיִּתְמַהְמָהּ vayitmahmah

"But he lingered" — and over this one word the Masoretes hung the shalshelet, the rarest cantillation mark in the Torah (it occurs four times in the whole Pentateuch): a zigzag sign sung as a long, wavering trill. The melody itself dawdles — the reader's voice hesitates with Lot, at dawn, in a city with hours to live. What saves him is not his resolve: "the men seized his hand, and his wife's hand, and the hands of his two daughters, in the compassion of Jehovah (chemlah) on him" — four people dragged bodily out of the story's way. (A shelf note: the ASV prints the name natively here — "Jehovah being merciful unto him" — exactly this project's convention, a century and a quarter early; KJV reads the substitute title.)

Bargaining with angels, mid-apocalypse. Told to run for the hills, Lot negotiates — nephew of his uncle to the last, but for a smaller prize: not fifty righteous, one small town. "Great is the kindness you have shown me" — chesed, its first appearance in the Bible: the steadfast, covenant-keeping love that becomes one of Scripture's great untranslatables (behind the "mercy" of the Psalms, and — paired with truth — behind John's "grace and truth," see the charis entry in the dictionary). "This town is near… and it is a little one (mits'ar)… is it not a little one?" — and the town is named from his own plea: Zoar, "Little." The answer grants it with an idiom worth keeping in the notes: "I have lifted your face" (nasati fanekha, here "granted you this favor") — nasa again, the carrying verb that "spared" Sodom's hypothetical fifty (18:26): grace pictured as a face lifted up off the ground. "I can do nothing until you arrive there" — the destruction waits, literally, on one slow man's walking pace.

Verses 23–29 · גָּפְרִית וָאֵשׁ gophrit va'esh

"Sulfur and fire — from Jehovah, out of the heavens." The sentence doubles strangely: "Jehovah rained on Sodom… from Jehovah, out of the heavens." Readings, each with a pedigree: Hebrew grammarians hear a solemn idiom of self-reference (kings speak of themselves this way in decrees, and the Bible does it elsewhere); some ancient Christian readers, as early as Justin Martyr, heard two divine persons — Jehovah on earth (the visitor of ch. 18) and Jehovah in heaven — and the verse has served in that argument ever since (the whole case, both ways, is on the Is Jesus God? page). This translation prints the doubled text and reports the argument. As for what fell: KJV, Geneva, Douay and ASV all rain "brimstone and fire" — the phrase that gave English its "fire and brimstone" — while NIV modernizes to "burning sulfur"; this translation reads "sulfur and fire," the plain mineral, in the Hebrew's own order. The verb of the event is haphakh, "overthrew" — and in verse 29 the event becomes a noun, "the overthrow" (hahafekhah): from here on, "like the overthrow of Sodom" is the Bible's standing measure of total ruin (Deuteronomy 29:23; Isaiah 13:19; Jeremiah 49:18).

Lot's wife, and Abraham's window. "His wife looked back from behind him — and she became a pillar of salt." One line, no psychology; the command had been flight without a backward tether, and she is caught mid-tether — fixed forever in the direction of what she could not leave. (The southern Dead Sea shore is salt-pillar country to this day — Mount Sodom's spires; the old tradition of Wisdom 10:7 already pointed at "a standing pillar of salt" as the story's monument. "Remember Lot's wife," Luke 17:32, needs only three words.) Then the camera climbs: Abraham, early in the morning, at "the place where he had stood before Jehovah" — yesterday's courtroom (18:22) — looks down over the plain and sees only smoke "like the smoke of a kiln" (kivshan — the word that will describe Sinai's smoke, Exodus 19:18). No word is recorded. And the narrator hands us the hinge quietly: "God remembered Abraham" — zakhar, the remembering-that-acts of "God remembered Noah" (8:1) — "and sent Lot out of the midst of the overthrow." Ten righteous were not found; but the argument of chapter 18 was not wasted — its other mercy, carrying the few out, is exactly what happened.

Verses 30–38 · מוֹאָב … בֶּן-עַמִּי Moav … Ben-Ammi

The cave. Lot — who begged for a town because he feared the hills (v19) — now fears the town too, and ends in the hills after all, in a cave, with nothing: the man who chose the well-watered plain "like the garden of Jehovah" (13:10) has run out of Sodoms. His daughters, watching the smoke of the only world they knew, draw a conclusion the text lets us hear in full: "there is no man on earth to come in to us" — they appear to believe the overthrow was the end of the world, a second flood in fire, and themselves its Noah's household. Their phrase is ark-vocabulary: "to keep offspring alive" (lehachayot zera) is what the animals were carried aboard to do (7:3). It is desperation, not appetite — and it is also the dark answer to verse 8: the father who once offered his daughters' bodies to a mob has his own body taken by his daughters, wine-blind, "and he knew nothing" — yada again, negated twice. (Over "and of her rising" in verse 33 the scribes set a row of dots — one of ten such dotted spots in the whole Torah, an ancient scribal flag; the rabbis read it as a wink that his ignorance was not quite total. Reported as received.)

Moab and Ben-Ammi. The sons are named with the text's own sound-glosses — Moav, heard as me'av, "from father"; Ben-Ammi, "son of my kin" — and they father Moab and Ammon, Israel's two kin-nations across the Jordan, whose long, prickly cousinhood with Israel the rest of the Bible remembers ("do not harass Moab… I have given Ar to the sons of Lot," Deuteronomy 2:9). Genesis tells its neighbors' origin without flattery — and then history hands the story its grace note: from Moab, from this cave's line, comes Ruth — "your people shall be my people" — great-grandmother of David (Ruth 4:17) and named in the genealogy of Jesus (Matthew 1:5). Lot leaves the story here, silent since Zoar, unmourned by the text; the last thing his family line is given is redemption. The closed break {ס} ends the chapter.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "that we may know them" (the verb kept, not paraphrased away), "pillar of salt" plain, the doubled "Jehovah… from Jehovah" printed as it stands, and the daughters' ark-phrase "keep offspring alive" kept exact.

Where it goes its own way: "ruin" for shachat again (the flood's verb, audible through the angels' commission), "he seemed like one who was joking" (keeping the tsachaq laugh-root in view), and the scribal apparatus reported — the shalshelet trill on "but he lingered," and the ten-dots tradition at verse 33.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: "that we may know them" against "I have known him" (18:19); the mob's "playing the judge" against the Judge of all the earth (18:25); the joking-laugh pays the laugh-thread (17:17, 18:12); the kikkar "like the garden of Jehovah" burns as chapter 13 foretold (13:10); Abraham returns to where he "stood before Jehovah" (18:22); and "God remembered Abraham" is Noah's hinge (8:1). Planted: matzot, the bread of the night-rescue (Exodus 12); the laugh-root's last dark turn (21:9); "like the overthrow of Sodom" as the prophets' byword; and Ruth the Moabite, the cave's unlikely grace (Ruth 4; Matthew 1:5).

Next installment: Genesis 20 — Abraham tries the sister-story again, at Gerar — and this time the king gets a dream.

Genesis 20 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
וַיִּסַּע מִשָּׁם אַבְרָהָם אַרְצָה הַנֶּגֶב, וַיֵּשֶׁב בֵּין-קָדֵשׁ וּבֵין שׁוּר; וַיָּגָר, בִּגְרָר.
And Abraham journeyed from there toward the land of the Negev and settled between Kadesh and Shur; and he sojourned in Gerar.note
2
וַיֹּאמֶר אַבְרָהָם אֶל-שָׂרָה אִשְׁתּוֹ, אֲחֹתִי הִוא; וַיִּשְׁלַח, אֲבִימֶלֶךְ מֶלֶךְ גְּרָר, וַיִּקַּח, אֶת-שָׂרָה.
And Abraham said of Sarah his wife, "She is my sister." And Abimelech king of Gerar sent, and took Sarah.note
3
וַיָּבֹא אֱלֹהִים אֶל-אֲבִימֶלֶךְ, בַּחֲלוֹם הַלָּיְלָה; וַיֹּאמֶר לוֹ, הִנְּךָ מֵת עַל-הָאִשָּׁה אֲשֶׁר-לָקַחְתָּ, וְהִוא, בְּעֻלַת בָּעַל.
But God came to Abimelech in a dream by night, and said to him, "Look — you are a dead man, because of the woman you have taken, for she is a married woman."note
4
וַאֲבִימֶלֶךְ, לֹא קָרַב אֵלֶיהָ; וַיֹּאמַר--אֲדֹנָי, הֲגוֹי גַּם-צַדִּיק תַּהֲרֹג.
Now Abimelech had not come near her. And he said, "Lord, will you kill a nation even though righteous?note
5
הֲלֹא הוּא אָמַר-לִי אֲחֹתִי הִוא, וְהִיא-גַם-הִוא אָמְרָה אָחִי הוּא; בְּתָם-לְבָבִי וּבְנִקְיֹן כַּפַּי, עָשִׂיתִי זֹאת.
Did he not himself say to me, 'She is my sister'? And she — she herself — said, 'He is my brother.' In the integrity of my heart and the innocence of my hands I have done this."note
6
וַיֹּאמֶר אֵלָיו הָאֱלֹהִים בַּחֲלֹם, גַּם אָנֹכִי יָדַעְתִּי כִּי בְתָם-לְבָבְךָ עָשִׂיתָ זֹּאת, וָאֶחְשֹׂךְ גַּם-אָנֹכִי אוֹתְךָ, מֵחֲטוֹ-לִי; עַל-כֵּן לֹא-נְתַתִּיךָ, לִנְגֹּעַ אֵלֶיהָ.
And God said to him in the dream, "I too know that in the integrity of your heart you did this — and it was I who held you back from sinning against me; that is why I did not let you touch her.note
7
וְעַתָּה, הָשֵׁב אֵשֶׁת-הָאִישׁ כִּי-נָבִיא הוּא, וְיִתְפַּלֵּל בַּעַדְךָ, וֶחְיֵה; וְאִם-אֵינְךָ מֵשִׁיב--דַּע כִּי-מוֹת תָּמוּת, אַתָּה וְכָל-אֲשֶׁר-לָךְ.
Now return the man's wife — for he is a prophet, and he will pray for you, and you shall live. But if you do not return her, know that you will surely die, you and all who are yours."note
8
וַיַּשְׁכֵּם אֲבִימֶלֶךְ בַּבֹּקֶר, וַיִּקְרָא לְכָל-עֲבָדָיו, וַיְדַבֵּר אֶת-כָּל-הַדְּבָרִים הָאֵלֶּה, בְּאָזְנֵיהֶם; וַיִּירְאוּ הָאֲנָשִׁים, מְאֹד.
And Abimelech rose early in the morning and called all his servants, and spoke all these words in their hearing; and the men were very afraid.note
9
וַיִּקְרָא אֲבִימֶלֶךְ לְאַבְרָהָם, וַיֹּאמֶר לוֹ מֶה-עָשִׂיתָ לָּנוּ וּמֶה-חָטָאתִי לָךְ, כִּי-הֵבֵאתָ עָלַי וְעַל-מַמְלַכְתִּי, חֲטָאָה גְדֹלָה: מַעֲשִׂים אֲשֶׁר לֹא-יֵעָשׂוּ, עָשִׂיתָ עִמָּדִי.
Then Abimelech called Abraham and said to him, "What have you done to us? How have I sinned against you, that you have brought on me and on my kingdom so great a sin? Things that are not done, you have done to me."note
10
וַיֹּאמֶר אֲבִימֶלֶךְ, אֶל-אַבְרָהָם: מָה רָאִיתָ, כִּי עָשִׂיתָ אֶת-הַדָּבָר הַזֶּה.
And Abimelech said to Abraham, "What did you see, that you did such a thing?"note
11
וַיֹּאמֶר, אַבְרָהָם, כִּי אָמַרְתִּי רַק אֵין-יִרְאַת אֱלֹהִים, בַּמָּקוֹם הַזֶּה; וַהֲרָגוּנִי, עַל-דְּבַר אִשְׁתִּי.
And Abraham said, "Because I said to myself, Surely there is no fear of God in this place, and they will kill me over my wife.note
12
וְגַם-אָמְנָה, אֲחֹתִי בַת-אָבִי הִוא--אַךְ, לֹא בַת-אִמִּי; וַתְּהִי-לִי, לְאִשָּׁה.
And besides, she truly is my sister — my father's daughter, though not my mother's daughter — and she became my wife.note
13
וַיְהִי כַּאֲשֶׁר הִתְעוּ אֹתִי, אֱלֹהִים מִבֵּית אָבִי, וָאֹמַר לָהּ, זֶה חַסְדֵּךְ אֲשֶׁר תַּעֲשִׂי עִמָּדִי: אֶל כָּל-הַמָּקוֹם אֲשֶׁר נָבוֹא שָׁמָּה, אִמְרִי-לִי אָחִי הוּא.
And when God caused me to wander from my father's house, I said to her, 'This is the kindness you must do me: at every place we come to, say of me, He is my brother.'"note
14
וַיִּקַּח אֲבִימֶלֶךְ צֹאן וּבָקָר, וַעֲבָדִים וּשְׁפָחֹת, וַיִּתֵּן, לְאַבְרָהָם; וַיָּשֶׁב לוֹ, אֵת שָׂרָה אִשְׁתּוֹ.
Then Abimelech took sheep and cattle, and male and female slaves, and gave them to Abraham; and he returned Sarah his wife to him.note
15
וַיֹּאמֶר אֲבִימֶלֶךְ, הִנֵּה אַרְצִי לְפָנֶיךָ: בַּטּוֹב בְּעֵינֶיךָ, שֵׁב.
And Abimelech said, "Look — my land is before you; settle wherever is good in your eyes."note
16
וּלְשָׂרָה אָמַר, הִנֵּה נָתַתִּי אֶלֶף כֶּסֶף לְאָחִיךְ--הִנֵּה הוּא-לָךְ כְּסוּת עֵינַיִם, לְכֹל אֲשֶׁר אִתָּךְ; וְאֵת כֹּל, וְנֹכָחַת.
And to Sarah he said, "Look — I have given your brother a thousand pieces of silver. Look, it is for you a covering of the eyes, for all who are with you; and in everything, you are cleared."note
17
וַיִּתְפַּלֵּל אַבְרָהָם, אֶל-הָאֱלֹהִים; וַיִּרְפָּא אֱלֹהִים אֶת-אֲבִימֶלֶךְ וְאֶת-אִשְׁתּוֹ, וְאַמְהֹתָיו--וַיֵּלֵדוּ.
Then Abraham prayed to God, and God healed Abimelech, and his wife, and his slave-women, and they bore children;note
18
כִּי-עָצֹר עָצַר יְהוָה, בְּעַד כָּל-רֶחֶם לְבֵית אֲבִימֶלֶךְ, עַל-דְּבַר שָׂרָה, אֵשֶׁת אַבְרָהָם. {ס}
for Jehovah had shut fast every womb of the house of Abimelech, because of Sarah, Abraham's wife.note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–2 · אֲחֹתִי הִוא achoti hi

"She is my sister" — again. Abraham moves south into Beer-lahai-roi country ("between Kadesh and Shur," 16:14's map) and sojourns in Gerar — the verb is gur, the resident-stranger's verb, and the town's own name nearly rhymes with it: the sojourner sojourns in Sojournville. And there he runs the Egypt play again, word for word — "Say, please, that you are my sister" was 12:13; here the narrator needs only three words, achoti hi, because the reader has seen this show before. So has the tradition: chapters 12, 20, and (with Isaac, ahead) 26 are the Bible's most famous doublet — three tellings of the wife-sister ruse. Readings differ on why: source critics hear the same tale told by different hands (this version has all the marks of the strand scholars call E — God in dreams, moral argument, "prophet"); traditional readers see a man repeating a real habit of fear — and the text supports at least the habit, for verse 13 will reveal the ruse was a standing arrangement, "at every place we come to." Shown, not sanded. One thing said honestly: Sarah is about ninety here, and readers ancient and modern have asked how she endangers a royal harem — the rabbis gave her restored youth (a tradition the next chapter's conception invites), the source critics an original setting earlier in her story. The text itself is silent, and it is the silence this translation keeps.

Verses 3–7 · כִּי-נָבִיא הוּא ki navi hu

The pagan king gets the courtroom. God comes to Abimelech "in a dream by night" — no plagues this time, as in Egypt; a hearing. "Look — you are a dead man" (KJV: "thou art but a dead man" — the "but" is the translators'; the Hebrew is blunter), "for she is a married woman" — literally be'ulat ba'al, "owned by an owner": the ancient legal idiom, printed here in its plain modern sense. And Abimelech answers with a sentence the reader has heard before: "Lord, will you kill a nation even though righteous?" — tsaddiq, the word of Abraham's own argument two chapters ago ("will you sweep away the righteous with the wicked?", 18:23-25). The pagan king of Gerar, without knowing it, files Abraham's brief back at heaven — and the Judge of all the earth honors it from a Philistine's lips exactly as from a prophet's. His plea rests on "the integrity of my heart" — tom-levavi, the noun of tamim, the very wholeness God demanded of Abraham at 17:1 ("walk before me and be blameless"; KJV ASV "integrity of my heart," NIV "clear conscience") — and the chapter's sharpest irony is that in this scene the tom belongs to the pagan, and the scheming to the prophet.

"For he is a prophet." The first occurrence of navi in the Bible — and note what defines it: not prediction but intercession — "he will pray for you, and you shall live." A prophet is one who stands between; it is exactly what Abraham did for Sodom (18:23-32), and God describes him by it. God also volunteers something extraordinary: "it was I who held you back from sinning against me" — Abimelech's innocence was itself grace, restraint given before it was known to be needed. The alternative is stated in Eden's own formula: "know that you will surely die" — mot tamut, the doubled death-sentence of 2:17, served here on a king.

Verses 8–13 · מֶה-עָשִׂיתָ לָּנוּ meh-asita lanu

"What have you done to us?" Pharaoh's exact question (12:18), now asked at Gerar — and Abimelech goes further: "things that are not done, you have done to me" — the pagan king lecturing the patriarch on basic decency, and the text lets the lecture stand. Abraham's defense comes in three descending steps. First the assumption: "Surely there is no fear of God in this place" — demonstrably false; Gerar's whole court "was very afraid" at one dream, and its king argued righteousness with God at midnight. Abraham's fear slandered a town. Second, the technicality: "she truly is my sister — my father's daughter, though not my mother's daughter" — after twenty chapters, the text finally discloses Sarah's parentage, and it is a half-truth doing full-time work (later law will close even this loophole, Leviticus 18:9). Third, the habit: "at every place we come to, say of me, He is my brother" — the ruse was the standing travel policy, from the very first wandering; and he calls it chesed — "this is the kindness you must do me" — the great covenant word of 19:19, spent here on a survival scheme. The grammar of that same sentence carries a famous oddity: "when God caused me to wander" uses a plural verb with Elohim — the one word the dictionary entry calls "plural in form, singular in verb" here takes its plural verb after all (Hebrew grammar allows it in a Gentile's hearing, say some; a trace of an older idiom, say others). The versions all hide it in English, which cannot decline "God"; the note is where it survives.

Verses 14–16 · כְּסוּת עֵינַיִם kesut einayim

The generous ending Egypt never had. Pharaoh loaded Abram with gifts before the truth and expelled him after it (12:16, 19-20); Abimelech gives the flocks and slaves after being wronged — and instead of "take her and go," says "my land is before you; settle wherever is good in your eyes." Then he turns to Sarah, and speaks the chapter's most disputed sentence. "I have given your brother" — the word chosen with an edge, her own cover-story handed back to her — "a thousand pieces of silver. Look, it is for you a covering of the eyes…" The idiom (kesut einayim) is found nowhere else, and the shelf scatters like startled birds: KJV "behold, he is to thee a covering of the eyes" (reading the covering as the husband); Geneva adds its margin that the money is "to buy thee a vaile"; ASV "it is for thee a covering of the eyes"; NIV paraphrases the sense — "to cover the offense against you"; TLB flattens it to compensation. Most likely it means the payment publicly "covers the eyes" of onlookers — no one may look at Sarah as a woman dishonored; the final word, venokhachat ("you are cleared," or "set right"), is itself rough enough that the Douay, following the Vulgate, reads a rebuke instead: "remember thou wast taken." This translation keeps the strange idiom and the clearing, and files the dispute here.

Verses 17–18 · כִּי-עָצֹר עָצַר יְהוָה ki atsor atsar YHWH

The prophet prays, and wombs open. The first thing the Bible's first-named prophet does with the title is intercede for the foreigner who wronged-and-was-wronged-by him — "and God healed Abimelech, and his wife, and his slave-women, and they bore children." Then the narrator's closing line lands the verb: Jehovah had "shut fast every womb" — atsor atsar, the doubled form of atsar — the very verb Sarai used of herself: "Jehovah has kept me from bearing" (16:2). The house that took Sarah was given, for a season, Sarah's own condition. And hold the shape of this ending: Abraham prays for other wombs, and they open — and the very next verse in the book (21:1) begins, "And Jehovah visited Sarah." The man who interceded for strangers' children receives his own. The closed break {ס} draws the line, and the twenty-five-year promise is one verse from payment.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "integrity of my heart" (with KJV/ASV, against the paraphrase), "covering of the eyes" kept as the strange idiom it is, "you are a dead man" blunt (dropping KJV's softening "but"), and "shut fast" for the doubled atsor atsar.

Where it goes its own way: "a married woman" for the legal idiom be'ulat ba'al ("owned by an owner" — reported in the note, not printed in the verse), "sojourned in Gerar" with the gur-Gerar near-rhyme flagged, and the plural verb of "God caused me to wander" preserved in the notes where English grammar cannot keep it.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: the ruse replays Egypt beat for beat — "say you are my sister" (12:13) and "What have you done?" (12:18); Abimelech's "will you kill a nation even though righteous?" files back Abraham's own brief (18:25); "integrity" is the tamim asked of Abraham (17:1); "you will surely die" is Eden's formula (2:17); chesed (19:19) is spent on the ruse; and atsar shuts Abimelech's wombs with Sarai's own verb (16:2). Planted: the prophet-as-intercessor (Moses at Sinai will inherit the office); the third telling of the ruse, waiting for Isaac (ch. 26); Abimelech himself, who returns to cut a treaty at Beersheba (21:22-34); and one open womb, next verse.

Next installment: Genesis 21 — "And Jehovah visited Sarah": Isaac is born, laughter is named — and Hagar and Ishmael are sent into the wilderness.

8
οὐκ ἦν ἐκεῖνος τὸ φῶς, ἀλλʼ ἵνα μαρτυρήσῃ περὶ τοῦ φωτός.

9
ἦν τὸ φῶς τὸ ἀληθινὸν ὃ φωτίζει πάντα ἄνθρωπον ἐρχόμενον εἰς τὸν κόσμον.

10
Ἐν τῷ κόσμῳ ἦν, καὶ ὁ κόσμος διʼ αὐτοῦ ἐγένετο, καὶ ὁ κόσμος αὐτὸν οὐκ ἔγνω.
He was in the world, and the world came to be through him, yet the world did not know him.note
11
εἰς τὰ ἴδια ἦλθεν, καὶ οἱ ἴδιοι αὐτὸν οὐ παρέλαβον.

But to all who did receive him, to those who believe in his name, he gave the right to become children of God —note

οἳ οὐκ ἐξ αἱμάτων οὐδὲ ἐκ θελήματος σαρκὸς οὐδὲ ἐκ θελήματος ἀνδρὸς ἀλλʼ ἐκ θεοῦ ἐγεννήθησαν.
children born not of blood, nor of the will of the flesh, nor of the will of a husband, but of God.note

Καὶ ὁ λόγος σὰρξ ἐγένετο καὶ ἐσκήνωσεν ἐν ἡμῖν, καὶ ἐθεασάμεθα τὴν δόξαν αὐτοῦ, δόξαν ὡς μονογενοῦς παρὰ πατρός, πλήρης χάριτος καὶ ἀληθείας·
And the Word became flesh and pitched his tent among us, and we saw his glory — glory as of the only Son from the Father — full of grace and truth.note
15
Ἰωάννης μαρτυρεῖ περὶ αὐτοῦ καὶ κέκραγεν λέγων· Οὗτος ἦν ὃν εἶπον· Ὁ ὀπίσω μου ἐρχόμενος ἔμπροσθέν μου γέγονεν, ὅτι πρῶτός μου ἦν·
(John testifies about him; he cried out, "This was the one I meant when I said, 'The one coming after me ranks ahead of me, because he was before me.'")note
16

17
ὅτι ὁ νόμος διὰ Μωϋσέως ἐδόθη, ἡ χάρις καὶ ἡ ἀλήθεια διὰ Ἰησοῦ Χριστοῦ ἐγένετο.

21
καὶ ἠρώτησαν αὐτόν· Τί οὖν; σὺ Ἠλίας εἶ; καὶ λέγει· Οὐκ εἰμί. Ὁ προφήτης εἶ σύ; καὶ ἀπεκρίθη· Οὔ.
So they asked him, "What then? Are you Elijah?" He says, "I am not." "Are you the Prophet?" He answered, "No."note
