This is the one I meant when I said, 'After me comes a man who ranks ahead of me, because he was before me.'note

κἀγὼ οὐκ ᾔδειν αὐτόν, ἀλλʼ ἵνα φανερωθῇ τῷ Ἰσραὴλ διὰ τοῦτο ἦλθον ἐγὼ ἐν ὕδατι βαπτίζων.
I myself did not know him; but I came baptizing with water so that he might be revealed to Israel."note

And John testified: "I have seen the Spirit descending like a dove out of heaven, and it remained on him.note
33

I myself did not know him, but the one who sent me to baptize with water — he told me, 'The one on whom you see the Spirit descend and remain, this is the one who baptizes with the Holy Spirit.'note

Nathanael says to him, "How do you know me?" Jesus answered him, "Before Philip called you, while you were under the fig tree, I saw you."note
49
ἀπεκρίθη αὐτῷ Ναθαναήλ· Ῥαββί, σὺ εἶ ὁ υἱὸς τοῦ θεοῦ, σὺ βασιλεὺς εἶ τοῦ Ἰσραήλ.
Nathanael answered him, "Rabbi, you are the Son of God; you are the King of Israel!"note
50
ἀπεκρίθη Ἰησοῦς καὶ εἶπεν αὐτῷ· Ὅτι εἶπόν σοι ὅτι εἶδόν σε ὑποκάτω τῆς συκῆς πιστεύεις; μείζω τούτων ὄψῃ.
Jesus answered him, "Because I told you I saw you under the fig tree, you believe? You will see greater things than these."note
51
καὶ λέγει αὐτῷ· Ἀμὴν ἀμὴν λέγω ὑμῖν, ὄψεσθε τὸν οὐρανὸν ἀνεῳγότα καὶ τοὺς ἀγγέλους τοῦ θεοῦ ἀναβαίνοντας καὶ καταβαίνοντας ἐπὶ τὸν υἱὸν τοῦ ἀνθρώπου.
And he says to him, "Amen, amen, I tell you: you will see heaven opened, and the angels of God ascending and descending on the Son of Man."note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. The Greek shown is the SBL Greek New Testament (ed. Michael W. Holmes, Society of Biblical Literature, 2010), a modern critical text in the Alexandrian tradition; quotations from the copyrighted English versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison, and the KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims, and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–2 · Ἐν ἀρχῇ ἦν ὁ λόγος … θεὸς ἦν ὁ λόγος en archē ēn ho logos … theos ēn ho logos

"In the beginning." John opens on the first two words of Genesis — the Septuagint's ἐν ἀρχῇ, the very phrase the Greek Old Testament uses in Genesis 1:1 — and reaches back past the first day to what already was before anything was made. Every version keeps "In the beginning"; the echo is the point, and this translation flags it as the first cross-reference between the testaments.

"the Word" — ho logos. Logos means "word, reason, account," and John is reaching for two streams at once. There is the Hebrew stream: the "word of Jehovah" by which the world is made (Genesis 1 says "and God said" ten times), and Wisdom, who was "with" God at creation in Proverbs 8. And there is the Greek stream: the logos of the philosophers — from Heraclitus to the Stoics to Philo — the rational principle that orders the cosmos. No English word carries all of that, so every version simply keeps "the Word," and so does this one.

"was with God" — pros ton theon. The preposition pros with the accusative suggests being turned toward, face-to-face; the Word is not merely near God but in relationship with him. "With" is the standard rendering across the shelf and here.

"and the Word was God" — or "divine," or "a god"? — kai theos ēn ho logos. This is the most argued clause in the New Testament, and the honest starting point is that the Greek grammar underdetermines it. The predicate noun theos has no article and stands before the verb, while the subject ho logos has the article. Three readings compete: definite ("the Word was God"), qualitative ("the Word was divine / what God was, the Word was" — stressing the Word's nature), and indefinite ("the Word was a god"). A definite predicate noun placed before its verb regularly drops the article in Greek — the observation behind "Colwell's rule" — so the missing article does not by itself force the indefinite "a god"; the closest study of the construction (Harner, 1973) found it usually qualitative, and concluded this clause is neither "the Word was God" nor "a god" but "the Word had the same nature as God." Six of the seven shelf versions read "the Word was God" (NIV KJV GNV DRB ASV TLB); the NWT alone reads "the Word was a god," resting its case on the absent article.

MiSTeR takes the qualitative road — "and the Word was divine" — because it says what the construction most likely says (the Word shares fully in what God is) while keeping the distinction the next clause insists on: he was with God, so he is not simply the person "God." "Divine" is deliberately neither the flat "was God" (which an English reader can hear as "the Word is the Father" — a collapse the verse itself rules out) nor "a god" (which the grammar least supports, and which reads awkwardly against "besides me there is no god," Isaiah 44:6). This is the one place in the Gospel where whole traditions divide — is the Son fully God, a lesser divine being, or the highest of created beings? — so the note refuses to cast the deciding vote. The full argument, on every side (the grammar, "firstborn of all creation," the Angel of Jehovah, the prayer to the Father, and the counter-testimony), is gathered in the Ask Mr. Librarian post on this verse.

Verses 3–4 · πάντα διʼ αὐτοῦ ἐγένετο … ὃ γέγονεν panta di' autou egeneto … ho gegonen

"came to be" — the drumbeat. The verb egeneto ("came to be, came into being") tolls through the whole Prologue, and it is set in deliberate contrast to the Word, who does not "come to be" but simply was (ēn, verses 1–2). Creation "came to be" through him; a man will "come" (verse 6); and finally the Word himself will "become" flesh (verse 14). Rendering it consistently "came to be" keeps the beat audible — the same instinct behind this translation's "and God said… and so it was" in Genesis 1.

The famous punctuation. The words ho gegonen ("that which has come to be") can be read with the end of verse 3 ("…not one thing came to be that has come to be") or with the start of verse 4 ("What has come to be — in him was life…"). The oldest manuscripts carried no punctuation at all, so both were heard in antiquity, and several early church fathers took it with verse 4. Modern critical editions are split; the SBL text shown here prints it at the close of verse 3, and MiSTeR follows suit, as do KJV NIV ASV. It is a genuine fork in the road, and the note is here to say so rather than hide it.

Verse 5 · ἡ σκοτία αὐτὸ οὐ κατέλαβεν hē skotia auto ou katelaben

"has not overcome" / "did not comprehend." The verb katelaben carries two senses at once: to seize, overpower and to grasp, understand. KJV GNV DRB hear the second — "the darkness comprehended it not"; NIV ASV and most moderns hear the first — "has not overcome it." John may well intend both: the darkness neither understood the light nor defeated it. MiSTeR renders "has not overcome it" and flags the double meaning, because a footnote can hold what a single English verb cannot.

Verses 6–8 · Ἐγένετο ἄνθρωπος … εἰς μαρτυρίαν egeneto anthrōpos … eis martyrian

The drumbeat lands on a man. After three verses of the Word who simply was, the verb egeneto — "came to be" — now marks the appearance of a man in history: "there came a man." The hymn drops for a moment into a name and a date.

"witness" / "testify" — martyria, martyreō. This is one of the Gospel's master words, and it enters here. John's whole role is to testify; the text is careful, even insistent, that he "was not the light" but only its witness — a demotion the Gospel keeps making, probably against those in its own day who prized the Baptist too highly. (Note that the Gospel of John never names its own author; every "John" in this book is John the Baptist.)

Verses 9–13 · τέκνα θεοῦ … ἐκ θεοῦ ἐγεννήθησαν tekna theou … ek theou egennēthēsan

What "was coming into the world" (v. 9)? The Greek participle can attach to "the true light" (the light was coming into the world) or to "everyone" (every person coming into the world). KJV takes the second — "every man that cometh into the world"; most moderns take the first, and so does MiSTeR: it is the light that is coming.

"children of God," not "sons" (vv. 12–13). John keeps a careful distinction the whole Gospel through: he reserves "Son" (huios) for Jesus and calls believers "children" (tekna). NIV ASV and this translation keep "children"; KJV GNV blur it with "sons of God." These children are "born not of blood, nor of the will of the flesh, nor of the will of a husband, but of God" — a birth from above. (The Greek is plural, "who were born"; a few Old Latin witnesses read a singular "who was born," turning the line into a statement about the virgin birth — but the plural, about believers, is the text.)

Verse 14 · ὁ λόγος σὰρξ ἐγένετο καὶ ἐσκήνωσεν ho logos sarx egeneto kai eskēnōsen

"became flesh." The drumbeat reaches its shock: the Word who was in the beginning now became — egeneto — flesh. And it is flesh (sarx), the frail human stuff, not "a body" or "a man": the plainest, earthiest word available, chosen on purpose.

"pitched his tent among us." The verb eskēnōsen is built on skēnē, "tent, tabernacle." John says the Word literally "tented" among us — an unmistakable echo of the tabernacle of Exodus, where Jehovah's glory came to dwell in the middle of Israel's camp (the later Hebrew term for that indwelling presence, shekinah, is from the same root as the Hebrew for "tent"). Most versions flatten it — KJV "dwelt among us," NIV "made his dwelling among us." MiSTeR keeps the tent: "pitched his tent among us," so the tabernacle and its glory — the next word — stay in view.

monogenēs — "only," not "only-begotten." The word is mono- ("only") + -genēs (from genos, "kind, kin") — "one of a kind, an only child" — not mono- + gennaō ("to beget"), despite the long "only-begotten" tradition, which entered English through the Latin unigenitus. KJV GNV ASV DRB NWT all read "only-begotten"; NIV reads "the one and only." MiSTeR renders "the only Son" — "only" for monogenēs, "Son" supplied because the verse plainly means the Son — and lets the etymology-driven "begotten," which reads a later doctrine back into the word, go.

"grace and truth." The pair charis kai alētheia is almost certainly the Greek for a famous Hebrew pair — chesed ve-emet, "steadfast love and faithfulness" — the words Jehovah speaks over himself to Moses in Exodus 34:6. Held here, it sets a trap that verse 17 will spring.

Verses 15–17 · χάριν ἀντὶ χάριτος … διὰ Ἰησοῦ Χριστοῦ charin anti charitos … dia Iēsou Christou

John breaks in (v. 15). The Baptist's voice interrupts the hymn — the parenthesis is his — to say the thing he keeps saying: the one who comes after me (born later) ranks ahead of me, "because he was before me" (existed first). Later in time, first in rank and in being.

"grace upon grace" (v. 16). Charin anti charitos: anti can mean "in place of" (one grace replacing another) or "upon, in return for" (grace heaped on grace). Most render it "grace upon grace" and so does MiSTeR.

Moses and the trap sprung (v. 17). "The law was given through Moses; grace and truth came through Jesus Christ." This is not law against grace as enemies — it is the chesed ve-emet of Sinai (verse 14), now arriving in a person. And here, for the first time, the name: Jesus Christ. "Christ" (Christos) is Greek for "anointed," the Messiah — a title, not a surname, and the note flags it as such the first time it lands.

Verse 18 · μονογενὴς θεὸς ὁ ὢν εἰς τὸν κόλπον τοῦ πατρός monogenēs theos ho ōn eis ton kolpon tou patros

"No one has ever seen God" echoes Exodus 33:20 — no one sees God and lives. What follows is one of the sharpest manuscript decisions in the Gospel, and exactly the kind of thing the New Testament introduction and its apparatus exist for.

The variant: "only God" or "only Son"? The earliest witnesses read monogenēs theos, "the only God" (or "God the only Son") — this is the text of the papyri P66 and P75 (c. 200), together with Codex Sinaiticus and Codex Vaticanus. The later Byzantine majority, and the King James tradition, read ho monogenēs huios, "the only Son." This split is precisely why NIV reads "the one and only Son, who is himself God" while KJV GNV ASV DRB read "the only begotten Son." MiSTeR follows the early papyri: "the only God, who is at the Father's side." The harder, older reading is the one the manuscripts push us toward.

"has made him known" — exēgēsato, the root of "exegesis." The Son "explains, narrates" the God no one has seen. It is a quiet claim about the whole book to follow: the Gospel is that explanation.

Verses 19–23 · οἱ Ἰουδαῖοι … φωνὴ βοῶντος ἐν τῇ ἐρήμῳ hoi Ioudaioi … phōnē boōntos en tē erēmō

"the Judeans." John's term hoi Ioudaioi can mean "the Jews" broadly or "the Judean authorities" specifically — here, the temple establishment in Jerusalem sending its priests and Levites. The blanket rendering "the Jews," carried by KJV and most versions, has done real historical harm, read across later centuries as a charge against a whole people. MiSTeR renders "the Judeans" where the context is plainly the authorities, and says why — the same plainness this translation brought to the curse-of-Ham verses in Genesis 9.

"I am not…" The delegation offers John three end-time roles and he refuses all three: the Messiah; Elijah, expected to return before the day of Jehovah (Malachi 4:5); and the Prophet like Moses (Deuteronomy 18:15). He claims only to be a voice — quoting Isaiah 40:3, "make straight the way of the Lord." Not the Word; a voice for it.

Verses 24–28 · τὸν ἱμάντα τοῦ ὑποδήματος … ἐν Βηθανίᾳ πέραν τοῦ Ἰορδάνου ton himanta tou hypodēmatos … en Bēthania peran tou Iordanou

"whose sandal-strap I am not worthy to untie." Untying a master's sandal was a slave's job; John ranks himself below a slave to the one coming after him. Every version keeps the image.

"Bethany beyond the Jordan." Not the Bethany near Jerusalem (a different town two miles from the city) — a place east of the Jordan whose exact site was already lost early: Origen, in the third century, said he could not find a "Bethany" there and proposed reading "Bethabara" instead, a variant that entered some manuscripts (and the KJV). MiSTeR keeps "Bethany beyond the Jordan," the reading of the earliest witnesses, and the map marks the site as undetermined rather than guess a pin.

Verses 29–34 · ὁ ἀμνὸς τοῦ θεοῦ … ὁ ἐκλεκτὸς τοῦ θεοῦ ho amnos tou theou … ho eklektos tou theou

"the Lamb of God." The image gathers several at once: the Passover lamb of Exodus 12, whose blood spared Israel; the lamb "led to the slaughter" of Isaiah 53; and, some hear, the ram God provided in place of Isaac at the binding (Genesis 22, where Abraham says "God will provide the lamb"). "Who takes away the sin of the world" widens the lamb's work from one nation to all.

"Look —" The Baptist's word here, and again in verse 36 and verse 47, is ide, "behold, look." This translation renders the Hebrew hineh as "Look —" all the way through Genesis; ide is its natural Greek heir, so MiSTeR keeps "Look —" here, and the pointing gesture of the Old Testament carries straight into the New.

"the Chosen One of God" (v. 34) — another variant. The earliest text read here, "the Chosen One" (ho eklektos, echoing the "chosen servant" of Isaiah 42:1); the majority of later manuscripts read "the Son of God" (ho huios). MiSTeR follows the early reading — "the Chosen One of God" — and notes the familiar "Son of God" of KJV and the majority. As at verse 18, the older reading and the later reading are both shown, with their pedigrees.

Verses 35–42 · Ῥαββί … Μεσσίαν … Κηφᾶς Rhabbi … Messian … Kēphas

The evangelist as translator. Three times in a few verses the Gospel stops to render a word for its Greek readers: "Rabbi (which means Teacher)," "the Messiah (which means Christ)," "Cephas (which is translated Peter)." The writer is doing, inside his own text, exactly what this project does in the margin — catching a word in the source tongue and handing across its meaning.

"the tenth hour" — about four in the afternoon, counting from dawn. It is the kind of detail a man remembers because he was there.

"you will be called Cephas." Aramaic kepha means "rock"; its Greek rendering is Petros. Jesus renames Simon on sight — a God-given name-change for a destiny, in the exact tradition of Abram becoming Abraham and (as Genesis will soon reach) Jacob becoming Israel. This translation surfaced every Hebrew name-pun in Genesis; here the Gospel hands us the pun already translated, and we simply keep both halves — "Cephas … Peter."

Verses 43–46 · Ἐκ Ναζαρὲτ δύναταί τι ἀγαθὸν εἶναι; ek Nazaret dynatai ti agathon einai?

"Follow me." The first direct call in this Gospel — two words, to Philip.

"Can anything good come out of Nazareth?" Nazareth was an obscure Galilean village, never named in the Old Testament, and Nathanael's snobbery is honest and unedited — the Gospel leaves it standing. Philip does not argue back; he answers with an invitation this chapter has already used once (verse 39): "Come and see." Behind it stands the larger claim — that Jesus is "the one Moses wrote about in the Law, and the prophets" — the whole Old Testament pointing forward.

Verses 47–51 · Ἰσραηλίτης ἐν ᾧ δόλος οὐκ ἔστιν … τοὺς ἀγγέλους … ἀναβαίνοντας καὶ καταβαίνοντας Israēlitēs en hō dolos ouk estin … tous angelous … anabainontas kai katabainontas

"an Israelite in whom there is no deceit." The word for deceit is dolos, "guile," and it is chosen with care. The nation "Israel" is named for Jacob — and Jacob earned that name after a lifetime of guile: it is the very word the Greek Genesis puts in Isaac's mouth — "your brother came with deceit and took your blessing" (Genesis 27:35). Jesus sees in Nathanael a true son of Israel without Jacob's guile — a Jacob undeceived. (Genesis 27 is still ahead in this project; when it lands, this becomes a live cross-reference.)

Jacob's ladder, and the whole chapter's key (v. 51). "You will see heaven opened, and the angels of God ascending and descending on the Son of Man" quotes Jacob's dream at Bethel, where angels go up and down a stairway between earth and heaven and Jacob wakes to say "this is the gate of heaven" (Genesis 28:12). Jesus puts himself where the ladder stood: the Son of Man — his own favored title, from Daniel 7:13, and its first appearance here — is now the meeting-place of heaven and earth, the true Bethel. That a chapter which opened "In the beginning" should close on Jacob's ladder is the Gospel telling you how to read it: Genesis, all the way down. (Genesis 28 is also still ahead; the cross-reference waits for it.)

"Amen, amen." Jesus' solemn double amēn — his signature formula, unique to this Gospel in its doubled form. KJV renders it "Verily, verily," NIV "Very truly."MiSTeR keeps "Amen, amen," because amen is itself a Hebrew word the Greek simply carried over untranslated — a loanword all the way down, best left as it stands.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf (ASV, and the KJV where it is plain): "children of God" not "sons," keeping the evangelist's own Aramaic glosses ("Rabbi … Teacher," "Messiah … Christ," "Cephas … Peter"), and keeping "Amen, amen" as the loanword it is.

Where it follows the earliest manuscripts over the King James tradition: "the only God" at verse 18 and "the Chosen One of God" at verse 34 — both on the strength of the papyri P66 and P75 and the great fourth-century codices, exactly the witnesses the New Testament introduction sets out.

Where it goes its own way: "the Word was divine" for the article-less theos of 1:1 (the qualitative reading — see the note and the post on this verse), "pitched his tent" for eskēnōsen (holding the tabernacle echo), "the only Son" for monogenēs without "begotten," "the Judeans" where the authorities are meant, and "Look —" for ide, carried straight over from the hineh of Genesis.

Echoes planted. "In the beginning" is paid now against Genesis 1:1; light-in-the-darkness against Genesis 1:3–4; the Spirit descending against Genesis 1:2. Two more are planted and wait for Genesis to reach them: Nathanael as a Jacob "without deceit" (Genesis 27:35), and the angels ascending and descending on the Son of Man — Jacob's ladder (Genesis 28:12).

Next installment: John 2 — the wedding at Cana, water into wine, and the first of the signs.

John 2 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
Καὶ τῇ ἡμέρᾳ τῇ τρίτῃ γάμος ἐγένετο ἐν Κανὰ τῆς Γαλιλαίας, καὶ ἦν ἡ μήτηρ τοῦ Ἰησοῦ ἐκεῖ·
And on the third day there was a wedding at Cana of Galilee, and the mother of Jesus was there;note
2
ἐκλήθη δὲ καὶ ὁ Ἰησοῦς καὶ οἱ μαθηταὶ αὐτοῦ εἰς τὸν γάμον.
and Jesus too was invited to the wedding, with his disciples.note
3
καὶ ὑστερήσαντος οἴνου λέγει ἡ μήτηρ τοῦ Ἰησοῦ πρὸς αὐτόν· Οἶνον οὐκ ἔχουσιν.
And when the wine ran short, the mother of Jesus said to him, "They have no wine."note
4
καὶ λέγει αὐτῇ ὁ Ἰησοῦς· Τί ἐμοὶ καὶ σοί, γύναι; οὔπω ἥκει ἡ ὥρα μου.
And Jesus said to her, "Woman, what is that to me and to you? My hour has not yet come."note
5
λέγει ἡ μήτηρ αὐτοῦ τοῖς διακόνοις· Ὅ τι ἂν λέγῃ ὑμῖν ποιήσατε.
His mother said to the servants, "Whatever he tells you, do it."note
6
ἦσαν δὲ ἐκεῖ λίθιναι ὑδρίαι ἓξ κατὰ τὸν καθαρισμὸν τῶν Ἰουδαίων κείμεναι, χωροῦσαι ἀνὰ μετρητὰς δύο ἢ τρεῖς.
Now six stone water-jars were standing there, for the purification of the Jews, each holding two or three measures.note
7
λέγει αὐτοῖς ὁ Ἰησοῦς· Γεμίσατε τὰς ὑδρίας ὕδατος· καὶ ἐγέμισαν αὐτὰς ἕως ἄνω.
Jesus said to them, "Fill the jars with water." And they filled them up to the brim.note
8
καὶ λέγει αὐτοῖς· Ἀντλήσατε νῦν καὶ φέρετε τῷ ἀρχιτρικλίνῳ· οἱ δὲ ἤνεγκαν.
And he said to them, "Now draw some out, and take it to the master of the feast." And they took it.note
9
ὡς δὲ ἐγεύσατο ὁ ἀρχιτρίκλινος τὸ ὕδωρ οἶνον γεγενημένον, καὶ οὐκ ᾔδει πόθεν ἐστίν, οἱ δὲ διάκονοι ᾔδεισαν οἱ ἠντληκότες τὸ ὕδωρ, φωνεῖ τὸν νυμφίον ὁ ἀρχιτρίκλινος
When the master of the feast tasted the water now become wine — and he did not know where it had come from, though the servants who had drawn the water knew — the master of the feast called the bridegroomnote
10
καὶ λέγει αὐτῷ· Πᾶς ἄνθρωπος πρῶτον τὸν καλὸν οἶνον τίθησιν, καὶ ὅταν μεθυσθῶσιν τὸν ἐλάσσω· σὺ τετήρηκας τὸν καλὸν οἶνον ἕως ἄρτι.
and said to him, "Everyone sets out the good wine first, and the lesser wine once they have drunk freely. You have kept the good wine until now."note
11
ταύτην ἐποίησεν ἀρχὴν τῶν σημείων ὁ Ἰησοῦς ἐν Κανὰ τῆς Γαλιλαίας καὶ ἐφανέρωσεν τὴν δόξαν αὐτοῦ, καὶ ἐπίστευσαν εἰς αὐτὸν οἱ μαθηταὶ αὐτοῦ.
This, the beginning of the signs, Jesus did at Cana of Galilee, and revealed his glory; and his disciples believed in him.note
12
Μετὰ τοῦτο κατέβη εἰς Καφαρναοὺμ αὐτὸς καὶ ἡ μήτηρ αὐτοῦ καὶ οἱ ἀδελφοὶ καὶ οἱ μαθηταὶ αὐτοῦ, καὶ ἐκεῖ ἔμειναν οὐ πολλὰς ἡμέρας.
After this he went down to Capernaum — he and his mother and his brothers and his disciples — and they stayed there a few days.note
13
Καὶ ἐγγὺς ἦν τὸ πάσχα τῶν Ἰουδαίων, καὶ ἀνέβη εἰς Ἱεροσόλυμα ὁ Ἰησοῦς.
And the Passover of the Jews was near, and Jesus went up to Jerusalem.note
14
καὶ εὗρεν ἐν τῷ ἱερῷ τοὺς πωλοῦντας βόας καὶ πρόβατα καὶ περιστερὰς καὶ τοὺς κερματιστὰς καθημένους,
And in the temple courts he found the sellers of cattle and sheep and doves, and the money-changers at their seats;note
15
καὶ ποιήσας φραγέλλιον ἐκ σχοινίων πάντας ἐξέβαλεν ἐκ τοῦ ἱεροῦ τά τε πρόβατα καὶ τοὺς βόας, καὶ τῶν κολλυβιστῶν ἐξέχεεν τὰ κέρματα καὶ τὰς τραπέζας ἀνέστρεψεν,
and he made a whip of cords and drove them all out of the temple courts, the sheep and the cattle too; and he poured out the money-changers' coins and overturned the tables;note
16
καὶ τοῖς τὰς περιστερὰς πωλοῦσιν εἶπεν· Ἄρατε ταῦτα ἐντεῦθεν, μὴ ποιεῖτε τὸν οἶκον τοῦ πατρός μου οἶκον ἐμπορίου.
and to the sellers of the doves he said, "Take these things away from here. Stop making my Father's house a house of trade."note
17
ἐμνήσθησαν οἱ μαθηταὶ αὐτοῦ ὅτι γεγραμμένον ἐστίν· Ὁ ζῆλος τοῦ οἴκου σου καταφάγεταί με.
His disciples remembered that it is written: "Zeal for your house will consume me."note
18
ἀπεκρίθησαν οὖν οἱ Ἰουδαῖοι καὶ εἶπαν αὐτῷ· Τί σημεῖον δεικνύεις ἡμῖν, ὅτι ταῦτα ποιεῖς;
Then the Jews answered him, "What sign do you show us, that you do these things?"note
19
ἀπεκρίθη Ἰησοῦς καὶ εἶπεν αὐτοῖς· Λύσατε τὸν ναὸν τοῦτον καὶ ἐν τρισὶν ἡμέραις ἐγερῶ αὐτόν.
Jesus answered them, "Destroy this temple, and in three days I will raise it up."note
20
εἶπαν οὖν οἱ Ἰουδαῖοι· Τεσσεράκοντα καὶ ἓξ ἔτεσιν οἰκοδομήθη ὁ ναὸς οὗτος, καὶ σὺ ἐν τρισὶν ἡμέραις ἐγερεῖς αὐτόν;
Then the Jews said, "Forty-six years this temple has been under building — and you will raise it up in three days?"note
21
ἐκεῖνος δὲ ἔλεγεν περὶ τοῦ ναοῦ τοῦ σώματος αὐτοῦ.
But he was speaking of the temple of his body.note
22
ὅτε οὖν ἠγέρθη ἐκ νεκρῶν, ἐμνήσθησαν οἱ μαθηταὶ αὐτοῦ ὅτι τοῦτο ἔλεγεν, καὶ ἐπίστευσαν τῇ γραφῇ καὶ τῷ λόγῳ ὃν εἶπεν ὁ Ἰησοῦς.
So when he was raised from the dead, his disciples remembered that he had said this; and they believed the scripture, and the word that Jesus had spoken.note
23
Ὡς δὲ ἦν ἐν τοῖς Ἱεροσολύμοις ἐν τῷ πάσχα ἐν τῇ ἑορτῇ, πολλοὶ ἐπίστευσαν εἰς τὸ ὄνομα αὐτοῦ, θεωροῦντες αὐτοῦ τὰ σημεῖα ἃ ἐποίει·
Now while he was in Jerusalem at the Passover, at the festival, many believed in his name, watching the signs he was doing.note
24
αὐτὸς δὲ Ἰησοῦς οὐκ ἐπίστευεν αὑτὸν αὐτοῖς διὰ τὸ αὐτὸν γινώσκειν πάντας,
But Jesus himself did not entrust himself to them, because he knew them all,note
25
καὶ ὅτι οὐ χρείαν εἶχεν ἵνα τις μαρτυρήσῃ περὶ τοῦ ἀνθρώπου, αὐτὸς γὰρ ἐγίνωσκεν τί ἦν ἐν τῷ ἀνθρώπῳ.
and because he had no need for anyone to bear witness about the human; for he himself knew what was in the human.note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. The Greek shown is the SBL Greek New Testament (ed. Michael W. Holmes, Society of Biblical Literature, 2010), a modern critical text in the Alexandrian tradition; quotations from the copyrighted English versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison, and the KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims, and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–5 · Τί ἐμοὶ καὶ σοί, γύναι; ti emoi kai soi, gynai?

"On the third day." Count the days from chapter 1 and something appears: the Baptist's testimony, then "the next day" three times (1:29, 35, 43), then "on the third day" — a running week, and at its end, a wedding. Many readers hear a deliberate echo of the creation week: the Gospel that opened "In the beginning" walks its own seven days and ends them, as Genesis ends its second chapter, with a marriage. (A structural reading, not a doctrine — reported because the day-count is really there.) The mother of Jesus is present, and stays unnamed: John's Gospel never once says "Mary" — she is "the mother of Jesus" here and "woman… your mother" at the cross (19:26-27), the two scenes framing the whole story.

"Woman, what is that to me and to you?" The idiom first: ti emoi kai soi is Hebrew wearing Greek clothes — mah-li valakh (Judges 11:12; 2 Samuel 16:10), "what business is this of ours?" — distance, not disrespect. And "Woman" (gynai) was a courteous address in Greek — Jesus uses the same word tenderly from the cross (19:26). But the shelf shows how hard the line is to carry into English: KJV "Woman, what have I to do with thee?" (harsher than the Greek), Douay "what is that to me and to thee?" (closest, and the reading kept here), NIV "why do you involve me?", TLB famously overcorrected to "I can't help you now" — and his mother, who hears no refusal at all, simply turns to the servants: "Whatever he tells you, do it" — her last recorded words in scripture. "My hour has not yet come": the first tick of John's great clock — hōra, the hour of cross-and-glory, will sound through the whole Gospel until "the hour has come" (12:23).

Verses 6–12 · ἀρχὴν τῶν σημείων archēn tōn sēmeiōn

Six stone jars, and the arithmetic of abundance. Stone, because stone vessels do not contract ritual impurity (a detail archaeology confirms everywhere in first-century Galilee) — these are jars "for the purification of the Jews," holding "two or three measures" each (metrētēs, roughly 39 liters; KJV charmingly reads "two or three firkins apiece"). Do the sum: something like five to seven hundred bottles of wine, for one village wedding, after the guests have already "drunk freely" — and not table wine: "you have kept the good wine until now." The purification water becomes celebration wine, in industrial quantity; the sign is not a conjuring trick but a statement about what has arrived. The steward's line to the bridegroom is the Gospel's theology in one sentence, spoken by a man who has no idea what he is saying.

"The beginning of the signs." John never says "miracle": his word is sēmeion, sign — a deed that points past itself — and he numbers Cana "the beginning" of a series that structures the Gospel's whole first half (seven signs, by the usual count). The reader of this site has met the root before, in the other John: "he made it known in signs" (Revelation 1:1) — sign-language is this author's declared method at both ends of the shelf. "And revealed his glory" — the glory the Prologue announced (1:14) — "and his disciples believed in him." Then a quiet travel verse with a loaded phrase: down to Capernaum, "he and his mother and his brothers" — and on those brothers the traditions divide: full siblings, children of Mary (the plain reading, held by most Protestants); sons of Joseph by a former marriage (the Eastern reading, as old as the second century); cousins (the Western reading behind Douay's tradition, argued by Jerome to protect Mary's perpetual virginity). The word says brothers; what the word covers, the church has argued for eighteen centuries. Reported, not settled.

Verses 13–17 · μὴ ποιεῖτε τὸν οἶκον τοῦ πατρός μου οἶκον ἐμπορίου mē poieite ton oikon tou patros mou oikon emporiou

The cleansing — at the beginning? Here is the Gospels' most famous chronology puzzle: Matthew, Mark and Luke set the temple cleansing in the last week of Jesus' life, the act that triggers the end; John sets it at the very start, first Passover, chapter 2. Readings with pedigrees: two cleansings, one early and one late (the traditional harmonization, held from Augustine to many moderns); or one event, placed theologically — John moving it forward as the programmatic opening of a ministry whose whole story is the replacement of the temple (most modern scholarship). This translation reports both and notes what is at stake in each: harmonizers preserve every text's clock; the one-event reading takes John's arrangement as meaning rather than memory. Shown, not sanded.

The scene itself. A whip of cords (KJV "a scourge of small cords") — made, the Greek implies, on the spot, and aimed at driving the animals; the coins poured out, the tables overturned, and the dove-sellers — the poor folk's traders — told, not whipped: "Take these things away." John's version of the word differs from the synoptics' famous "den of robbers": here it is "stop making my Father's house a house of trade" (KJV "house of merchandise," NIV "a market!") — the charge is not extortion but commerce as such, in the courts built for prayer. The disciples remember Psalm 69: "Zeal for your house will consume me" — and the psalm's verb is future tense, and the psalm is the passion-psalm the New Testament quotes more than almost any other: the memory is already pointing at the cross. (A note on "the Jews" in this Gospel, said once and meant throughout: in John the phrase most often means the Judean authorities — the whole cast of this Gospel, its author included, is Jewish, and this translation renders the Greek as it stands while flagging the idiom here.)

Verses 18–22 · λύσατε τὸν ναὸν τοῦτον lysate ton naon touton

Two temple-words, one riddle. Greek has a word for the whole temple complex — hieron, the precinct with its courts, where the traders sat (vv 14-15, "temple courts" here) — and a word for the sanctuary-house itself: naos. When Jesus answers the demand for a sign, he switches words: "Destroy this naos, and in three days I will raise it up." His hearers take the building; John glosses the riddle: "he was speaking of the naos of his body" — the first full example of this Gospel's signature device, the misunderstanding that carries the meaning (Nicodemus and the Samaritan woman are next). The Prologue said it already, in tent-language: the Word "tabernacled among us" (1:14) — the dwelling of God is a person now; Revelation will finish the thought ("I saw no temple in the city," 21:22).

"Forty-six years" — and for once, a number you can put on a calendar: Josephus dates Herod's reconstruction of the temple from the eighteenth year of his reign, 20/19 BC; forty-six years of building lands this Passover at about AD 27/28 — one of the very few countable dates in the Gospels, and a peg for the whole ministry (see the site's Chronology page). The retort itself misunderstands grandly; and John adds the note that makes his whole book's method explicit: "when he was raised from the dead, his disciples remembered… and they believed the scripture, and the word that Jesus had spoken" — this Gospel is written from after, by memory that only made sense in reverse.

Verses 23–25 · αὐτὸς γὰρ ἐγίνωσκεν τί ἦν ἐν τῷ ἀνθρώπῳ autos gar eginōsken ti ēn en tō anthrōpō

The wordplay English can just barely keep. "Many believed in his name… but Jesus did not entrust himself to them" — in Greek it is the same verb twice (episteusan… ouk episteuen): they trusted him; he did not trust them back. Sign-faith is real but thin — the crowd believes what it watches, and Jesus knows exactly how far that goes. Then the chapter's last line, rendered here with a word this project's readers know from the other Testament: "he himself knew what was in the human" (anthrōpos — generic humanity, exactly the register of Genesis's adam; KJV "he knew what was in man"). The claim is quietly enormous: reading what is in the human heart is, in Genesis, God's own work — "Jehovah saw… every inclination of the thoughts of their heart" (6:5). The next verse of the Gospel (3:1) opens on "a human of the Pharisees, named Nicodemus" — the first specimen brought to the one who knows what is in the human.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "what is that to me and to you?" (with Douay, against KJV's harsher idiom and the paraphrases), "house of trade" plain, "measures" for metrētēs (the note does the arithmetic; KJV's "firkins" saluted), and the same-verb wordplay of verses 23-24 kept visible ("believed… did not entrust himself").

Where it goes its own way: "the human" for the generic anthrōpos of verse 25 — the register of Genesis's adam, carried across Testaments — "temple courts" vs "temple" to keep the hieron/naos switch audible, and the cleansing-placement question reported in full rather than harmonized silently.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: "the beginning of the signs" speaks the method Revelation declared — "he made it known in signs" (Rev 1:1); "the naos of his body" completes the Prologue's tent — the Word who "tabernacled among us" (1:14); the third day closes the week of days begun at (1:29–43); and "he knew what was in the human" is the heart-reading that was God's own work before the flood (6:5). Planted: the hour (12:23; 13:1; 17:1); the three Passovers that give the ministry its length; the brothers "who did not believe in him" (7:5); Psalm 69, waiting at the cross; and Capernaum, the ministry's base camp, entered here by a travel-verse.

Next installment in this book: John 3 — Nicodemus comes by night: "born again," or "born from above" — the Greek means both at once — and the Bible's most quoted sentence.

Revelation 1 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
Ἀποκάλυψις Ἰησοῦ Χριστοῦ, ἣν ἔδωκεν αὐτῷ ὁ θεὸς δεῖξαι τοῖς δούλοις αὐτοῦ, ἃ δεῖ γενέσθαι ἐν τάχει, καὶ ἐσήμανεν ἀποστείλας διὰ τοῦ ἀγγέλου αὐτοῦ τῷ δούλῳ αὐτοῦ Ἰωάννῃ,

2
ὃς ἐμαρτύρησεν τὸν λόγον τοῦ θεοῦ καὶ τὴν μαρτυρίαν Ἰησοῦ Χριστοῦ, ὅσα εἶδεν.
who bore witness to the word of God and the testimony of Jesus Christ — all that he saw.note
3
μακάριος ὁ ἀναγινώσκων καὶ οἱ ἀκούοντες τοὺς λόγους τῆς προφητείας καὶ τηροῦντες τὰ ἐν αὐτῇ γεγραμμένα, ὁ γὰρ καιρὸς ἐγγύς.
Blessed is the one who reads aloud, and those who hear, the words of this prophecy, and keep what is written in it — for the time is near.note

and he made us a kingdom, priests to his God and Father — to him be the glory and the might forever and ever. Amen.note
7
Ἰδοὺ ἔρχεται μετὰ τῶν νεφελῶν, καὶ ὄψεται αὐτὸν πᾶς ὀφθαλμὸς καὶ οἵτινες αὐτὸν ἐξεκέντησαν, καὶ κόψονται ἐπʼ αὐτὸν πᾶσαι αἱ φυλαὶ τῆς γῆς. ναί, ἀμήν.

8
Ἐγώ εἰμι τὸ Ἄλφα καὶ τὸ Ὦ, λέγει κύριος, ὁ θεός, ὁ ὢν καὶ ὁ ἦν καὶ ὁ ἐρχόμενος, ὁ παντοκράτωρ.
"I am the Alpha and the Omega," says the Lord God — the one who is, and who was, and who is coming — the Almighty.note
9
Ἐγὼ Ἰωάννης, ὁ ἀδελφὸς ὑμῶν καὶ συγκοινωνὸς ἐν τῇ θλίψει καὶ βασιλείᾳ καὶ ὑπομονῇ ἐν Ἰησοῦ ἐγενόμην ἐν τῇ νήσῳ τῇ καλουμένῃ Πάτμῳ διὰ τὸν λόγον τοῦ θεοῦ καὶ τὴν μαρτυρίαν Ἰησοῦ.
I, John — your brother, and companion in the affliction and kingdom and endurance in Jesus — came to be on the island called Patmos, because of the word of God and the testimony of Jesus.note
10
ἐγενόμην ἐν πνεύματι ἐν τῇ κυριακῇ ἡμέρᾳ, καὶ ἤκουσα ὀπίσω μου φωνὴν μεγάλην ὡς σάλπιγγος

11
λεγούσης· Ὃ βλέπεις γράψον εἰς βιβλίον καὶ πέμψον ταῖς ἑπτὰ ἐκκλησίαις, εἰς Ἔφεσον καὶ εἰς Σμύρναν καὶ εἰς Πέργαμον καὶ εἰς Θυάτειρα καὶ εἰς Σάρδεις καὶ εἰς Φιλαδέλφειαν καὶ εἰς Λαοδίκειαν.
saying, "What you see, write in a scroll and send to the seven congregations: to Ephesus, and to Smyrna, and to Pergamum, and to Thyatira, and to Sardis, and to Philadelphia, and to Laodicea."note
12
Καὶ ἐπέστρεψα βλέπειν τὴν φωνὴν ἥτις ἐλάλει μετʼ ἐμοῦ· καὶ ἐπιστρέψας εἶδον ἑπτὰ λυχνίας χρυσᾶς,

and in the midst of the lampstands one like a son of man, clothed in a robe reaching to his feet, and girded across the chest with a golden sash.note

And in his right hand he held seven stars; and out of his mouth a sharp two-edged sword was going forth; and his face was like the sun shining in its strength.note

As for the secret of the seven stars that you saw on my right hand, and the seven golden lampstands: the seven stars are angels of the seven congregations, and the seven lampstands are seven congregations.note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. The Greek shown is the SBL Greek New Testament (ed. Michael W. Holmes, Society of Biblical Literature, 2010), a modern critical text in the Alexandrian tradition; quotations from the copyrighted English versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison, and the KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims, and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–3 · Ἀποκάλυψις Ἰησοῦ Χριστοῦ apokalypsis Iēsou Christou

"The revelation." The book is named from its own first word: apokalypsis — an unveiling, the pulling-back of a curtain ("Apocalypse" is simply this word left untranslated; the modern sense of "catastrophe" is a later accretion — the word means disclosure). The opening verse hands down a chain of custody: God → Jesus Christ → his angel → his slave John → the slaves of God; and it states the book's method in one verb — "he made it known in signs" (esēmanen, from sēmeion, "sign": the shelf's "signified," KJV ASV Douay). A book that tells you on its first line that it will speak in symbols has already given you its reading instructions. On "slaves" (douloi): the shelf softens to "servants" (KJV, NIV; ASV margins "bondservants") while the NWT reads "slaves" plainly — as does this translation, on the same plain-word principle as "slave-girl" for Hagar (Genesis 16).

"What must happen soon… the time is near." Said honestly: this is the book's hardest word, and it stands in the first paragraph. En tachei means "soon, quickly" — not "someday" — and readers have wrestled with the nearness ever since (the main proposals: fulfilled in the first-century crisis; "soon" on God's clock, "one day as a thousand years"; or inaugurated then and still unwinding). This translation prints "soon" and leaves the wrestling in the notes, where it belongs. Then the book's first beatitude (there will be seven): "blessed is the one who reads aloud" — ho anaginōskōn is the public lector, one reader before a listening assembly; a window straight into how this book was first experienced: performed whole, in one sitting, out loud.

Verses 4–6 · ἀπὸ ὁ ὢν καὶ ὁ ἦν καὶ ὁ ἐρχόμενος apo ho ōn kai ho ēn kai ho erchomenos

The Name that refuses to decline. Verse 4 contains the most famous deliberate grammatical error in the Greek Bible. After apo ("from"), Greek requires the genitive case — and John writes the nominative, three times: "from THE ONE WHO IS, AND WHO WAS, AND WHO IS COMING." Every Greek schoolboy would wince; John knows exactly what he is doing. Ho ōn, "the One Who Is," is the Greek Bible's rendering of the divine name's own explanation at the burning bush (Exodus 3:14) — and John treats the title as indeclinable, refusing to bend the Name to fit his sentence, grammar yielding to God rather than God to grammar. This translation sets the phrase in small capitals to mark the standing stone in the syntax. It is the divine name unfolded into three tenses — and where a Hebrew chapter would print Jehovah, this Greek circumlocution is the closest the New Testament comes to spelling it (see the Ask Mr. Librarian page on the Name).

One Greek letter: washed, or freed? Verse 5 carries a textual crux you can hear in English: the manuscripts split between lysanti ("freed/loosed us from our sins") and lousanti ("washed us from our sins") — one letter apart, and pronounced almost identically. The older witnesses read "freed" (so ASV "loosed," NIV "has freed," NWT "loosed"); the later text behind the Reformation Bibles read "washed" (KJV Geneva, and Douay from the Vulgate's lavit). This translation follows the critical text: freed. The same seam runs through verse 6: the older text reads "made us a kingdom, priests" (Exodus 19:6's "a kingdom of priests," echoed); the later text behind KJV reads "kings and priests." And note the titles of verse 5 — "the faithful witness" (martys, the word already migrating toward English "martyr"), "the firstborn of the dead" (on "firstborn" as rank, not manufacture, see the Is Jesus God? page), "the ruler of the kings of the earth" — a threefold claim staked, under a Roman emperor, from a penal island.

Verses 7–8 · τὸ Ἄλφα καὶ τὸ Ὦ … ὁ παντοκράτωρ to Alpha kai to Ō … ho pantokratōr

"Look — he is coming with the clouds." Idou — the Greek heir of the Hebrew hineh, rendered "Look —" throughout this translation. The verse is a fusion of two prophets: the cloud-rider who approaches the Ancient of Days in Daniel 7:13, and Zechariah's "they will look on me, whom they pierced, and mourn" (Zechariah 12:10) — the same Zechariah text John's Gospel quotes at the cross. "Beat their breasts" is the mourning gesture the Greek actually names (kopsontai); the shelf's "wail" and "mourn" soften it to sound alone.

"I am the Alpha and the Omega… the Almighty." The first letter and the last letter of the Greek alphabet — A to Z, with everything between implied. (KJV adds "the beginning and the ending" here — a phrase the later manuscripts imported from 21:6; the older text says it with the letters alone.) Then a word this project has met before, from the other side: pantokratōr, "the Almighty" — the very Greek word the old translators reached for when they guessed at El Shaddai in Genesis (see the note at 17:1, where this translation refused "Almighty" because the Hebrew's meaning is uncertain). Here there is no guess: the Greek itself says pantokratōr, "the all-holding" — the title is native, and this translation prints it gladly. Revelation uses it nine times; the rest of the New Testament, once. One more shelf note, factually: the NWT prints "says Jehovah God" in this verse — restoring the name where the Greek has kyrios ho theos, on its argument that the phrase quotes the Old Testament's naming formulas; the Greek text itself reads "Lord God," and that is what stands in the verse above.

Verses 9–11 · ἐν τῇ νήσῳ τῇ καλουμένῃ Πάτμῳ en tē nēsō tē kaloumenē Patmō

Which John? The book says only "John" — no "apostle," no "son of Zebedee," just "your brother, and companion in the affliction." Tradition from the second century (Justin Martyr, Irenaeus) identifies him with the apostle, author of the Gospel; but the question is not modern — Dionysius of Alexandria, in the third century, already argued from style that the Gospel and the Apocalypse cannot come from the same hand (the Gospel's Greek is smooth; Revelation's is wild, Hebrew-boned, and — as verse 4 just showed — deliberately lawless), and proposed a second John, "John of Patmos." The congregation kept the book either way. This translation reports the question and translates the text, which claims only the name.

Patmos, and the Lord's day. A small rocky island off the Asian coast — Rome used such islands for banishment, and "because of the word of God" reads most naturally as exile for preaching. "I came to be in the Spirit on the Lord's day" (kyriakē hēmera) — the phrase's first appearance in all literature: almost certainly Sunday, the resurrection's weekday, which soon bore this name everywhere Greek was spoken (a quiet counter-coinage, too — "the Emperor's day" was already a phrase in the Roman east). The voice commands a scroll for seven congregations, named in exact geographic order: Ephesus north to Smyrna and Pergamum, then inland and south through Thyatira, Sardis, Philadelphia, Laodicea — a courier's circuit; the book is a circular letter, and the list is its postal route. Their seven letters are the next two chapters.

Verses 12–16 · ὅμοιον υἱὸν ἀνθρώπου homoion huion anthrōpou

"One like a son of man." The vision is built almost entirely from Daniel 7 — but with one staggering transfer. In Daniel, "one like a son of man" comes with the clouds to the Ancient of Days, whose "hair is like pure wool" and whose clothing is "white as snow" (Daniel 7:9, 13). Here the son of man wears the Ancient of Days' own description: "his head and his hair were white like wool, white as snow." The two figures of Daniel's throne-room have become one — the chapter's boldest sentence, spoken entirely in imagery (the deity-of-Christ argument built on such texts is catalogued on the Is Jesus God? page). On the capitalization crux: KJV prints "like unto the Son of man" — a confessing capital — where ASV prints "like unto a son of man," indefinite, as the Greek is; this translation reads "one like a son of man" and lets the vision make the argument.

The rest of the figure is a mosaic of the prophets: the long robe and golden sash are priestly vesture (Exodus 28); eyes "like a flame of fire" and feet "like fine bronze… glowing in a furnace" come from the great visions of Daniel 10 and Ezekiel 1 (chalkolibanon, rendered "fine bronze," is a word found nowhere else in Greek literature — a guess by every translator since, honestly made); the voice "like the sound of many waters" is Ezekiel's for the glory of God (Ezekiel 43:2); and "out of his mouth a sharp two-edged sword" is Isaiah's servant, whose mouth is "like a sharp sword" (Isaiah 49:2) — the figure's only weapon is what he says. Seven stars in his right hand; a face like the sun in its strength. John is shown nothing that a prophet had not seen — and everything at once.

Verses 17–20 · Μὴ φοβοῦ mē phobou

"Do not be afraid." John falls "like a dead man" — and the hand that holds seven stars is laid on him, with the vision-word spoken to every terrified seer: mē phobou. It is the word Abram heard in the Bible's first prophetic vision — "Do not be afraid, Abram" (Genesis 15:1) — spoken now at the last book's first vision; the canon opens and closes its visions with the same reassurance. Then a self-naming that out-claims every title so far: "I am the first and the last" — which is not a new coinage but a quotation: it is Jehovah's own self-description in Isaiah ("I am the first and I am the last; besides me there is no god," Isaiah 44:6) — on the lips of the one who says, in the next breath, "and I was dead." The two claims sit in one sentence, and every reading of who Jesus is must pass through it (both readings, at full strength, on the Is Jesus God? page).

"The keys of Death and of Hades." Hadēs is the Greek Bible's rendering of the Hebrew Sheol — the grave-realm, the place of the dead, not the fiery hell of later imagery; KJV prints "the keys of hell and of death" (its text also reverses the order), where ASV and NIV keep "Hades" — as here. Keys mean custody: the door locks from his side now. Then the chapter closes with a gift rare in apocalyptic — the vision interprets itself: "the seven stars are angels of the seven congregations, and the seven lampstands are seven congregations." The book has shown its method twice now — it speaks in signs (v1), and its signs can be read. The "angels" of the congregations (guardian spirits? human messengers? the congregations' heavenly doubles?) are argued over to this day — the seven letters that follow are addressed to them, whoever they are.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "slaves" for douloi (with NWT, against the softening "servants"), "Hades" not "hell" (with ASV/NIV), "a son of man" indefinite (with ASV, against KJV's confessing capital), and the critical text's "freed us" and "a kingdom, priests" (with ASV/NIV, against the later text behind KJV's "washed" and "kings and priests").

Where it goes its own way: "made it known in signs" (keeping esēmanen's sign-language audible as the book's method statement), small capitals for THE ONE WHO IS AND WHO WAS AND WHO IS COMING (marking the deliberate grammar-break that refuses to decline the Name), "beat their breasts" for the mourning gesture the Greek names, and "the Almighty" printed gladly for pantokratōr — the word Genesis 17's note refused for El Shaddai, native at last.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: pantokratōr answers the "Almighty" question left open at Genesis 17:1; "the one who is" unfolds the Name whose story began at Genesis 2:4 (and on the Jehovah page); "the word of God" carries John 1:1 forward; and "do not be afraid," spoken to a man flat on the ground before God, was first heard by Abram in vision (15:1). Planted: the seven letters to the seven congregations (chapters 2–3, next in this book); the seven beatitudes of Revelation (one down); "I am coming" as the book's drumbeat; and Daniel 7's throne-room, waiting for the day Daniel itself joins this translation.

Next installment in this book: Revelation 2 — the letters begin: Ephesus, Smyrna, Pergamum, Thyatira.

Revelation 2 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
Τῷ ἀγγέλῳ τῆς ἐν Ἐφέσῳ ἐκκλησίας γράψον· Τάδε λέγει ὁ κρατῶν τοὺς ἑπτὰ ἀστέρας ἐν τῇ δεξιᾷ αὐτοῦ, ὁ περιπατῶν ἐν μέσῳ τῶν ἑπτὰ λυχνιῶν τῶν χρυσῶν·
