Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. The Greek shown is the SBL Greek New Testament (ed. Michael W. Holmes, Society of Biblical Literature, 2010), a modern critical text in the Alexandrian tradition; quotations from the copyrighted English versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison, and the KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims, and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–7 · Ephesus · τὴν ἀγάπην σου τὴν πρώτην ἀφῆκες tēn agapēn sou tēn prōtēn aphēkes

The anatomy of a letter. All seven letters share one form, worth learning on the first: an address ("to the angel of the congregation in…"); a title of the speaker, drawn from chapter 1's vision (here: the stars and lampstands of 1:13-16 — each city gets the facet of the vision it needs); "I know…" — praise first, wherever praise is possible; the complaint ("but I have this against you"); a call — "the one who has an ear, let him hear"; and a promise "to the one who overcomes" (ho nikōn, the victor), every one of which is paid out in the book's last two chapters. A word about the word: "congregation" here renders ekklēsia — an assembly of people, with no building and no institution in it (Acts 19 uses the same word for the Ephesus riot). It was Tyndale's rendering, and King James's translation rules expressly forbade it — "the word Church not to be translated Congregation"; the dictionary entry tells the whole story. Ephesus — the province's great harbor city, home of the temple of Artemis and, by tradition, of John himself — gets the fullest praise in the set: toil, endurance, doctrinal vigilance (they "tested those who call themselves apostles… and found them false"). And then the sentence that has stung readers for nineteen centuries: "you have left your first love" — orthodoxy intact, the fire gone; and the threat is not persecution but removal: "I will remove your lampstand." (The Nicolaitans, hated here and taught at Pergamum: honestly unknown — Irenaeus guessed followers of Nicolaus of Acts 6:5; the letters themselves pair them with "Balaam's teaching," idol-food and immorality, which is likely all we can say.)

The tree, again. The first overcomer-promise reaches all the way back to this project's first pages: "to eat from the tree of life, which is in the paradise of God." Paradeisos — a Persian loan-word for a walled garden — is the very word the Greek Bible chose for the garden of Eden; and the tree of life is the one whose way home has been under guard since "the cherubim… and a flaming sword… to guard the way to the tree of life" (Genesis 3:24). The Bible's last book begins its promises by unbarring its first book's gate.

Verses 8–11 · Smyrna · γίνου πιστὸς ἄχρι θανάτου ginou pistos achri thanatou

The letter with no complaint. Smyrna — the beautiful harbor city whose ringed acropolis ancient writers praised as "the crown of Smyrna" — receives, with Philadelphia, one of only two letters containing no rebuke at all. Its speaker-title is the one a persecuted congregation needs: "the first and the last, who was dead and came to life" (1:17-18's own words). "I know your… poverty — but you are rich": the city was wealthy; the congregation was poor, likely because confessing Christ cost trade and standing. "Affliction ten days" — a bounded number: short, counted, survivable; the point is the limit, not the calendar. And the charge that follows became one of the most famous in martyrdom's history: "Be faithful until death, and I will give you the crown of life" — the stephanos, the victor's laurel, not a monarch's diadem. Two generations later Smyrna's own bishop, Polycarp — who tradition says knew John — was burned in this city's stadium at 86, refusing to recant ("eighty-six years I have served him, and he has done me no wrong"): the letter, kept to the letter.

A hard verse, handled honestly. "The slander of those who say they are Jews, and are not, but are a synagogue of Satan." Two things are true and must both be said. First: in its own century this is an intra-Jewish polemic — John is Jewish, much of Smyrna's congregation was Jewish, and the charge is that the accusers (likely denouncing Christians to the Roman authorities, as Polycarp's martyrdom-account describes) have forfeited the name they claim; "synagogue" here is simply "assembly," the ordinary word. Second: torn from that setting, the phrase has been used for centuries to license Christian hatred of Jews as such — a use the text does not support and this project names as the abuse it is, exactly as it did with the "curse of Ham" (Genesis 9). The second death of the promise (v11) is defined by the book itself much later — the lake of fire (20:14) — and the promise is an exact mirror: those faithful until death are untouchable by the death that matters.

Verses 12–17 · Pergamum · ὅπου ὁ θρόνος τοῦ Σατανᾶ hopou ho thronos tou Satana

"Where Satan's throne is." Pergamum, the old royal capital of Asia, terraced up its mountain like a crown — and something up there is being called Satan's throne (KJV "Satan's seat" undersells the Greek: thronos is a throne). The candidates, each with a pedigree: the Great Altar of Zeus, whose sculpted frieze of gods battling serpent-legged giants dominated the acropolis (the altar itself was excavated and carried off to Berlin — it stands today in the Pergamon Museum, one of the strangest afterlives any biblical landmark has had); the temple of Rome and Augustus — Pergamum was the first city of Asia licensed for the imperial cult, and emperor-worship is Revelation's great enemy; or the healing shrine of Asclepius with its serpent emblem. Perhaps all of them at once: the whole skyline. Here Antipas was killed — "my witness, my faithful one" (martys, the word already crossing into "martyr," and the exact title of Jesus himself in 1:5) — the book's only named Christian casualty.

Balaam's program, and the white stone. The complaint reaches back to Numbers: Balaam, who could not curse Israel, taught Balak to seduce it instead — "idol-sacrifices and sexual immorality" — the Bible's shorthand for assimilation by dinner-party (in these trade cities, the guild feasts in the gods' honor were where business got done; the pressure was economic, and the "teaching" said go along). The promises answer the menu: instead of idol-meat, hidden manna; and "a white stone, and on the stone a new name written." The psēphos (NWT reads "white pebble," which is exactly what the word means) had many uses — a juror's pebble of acquittal, an admission-token to a feast, an amulet bearing a secret name — and the letter may intend the whole cluster: verdict, invitation, identity. As for the new name: readers of this site have watched God rename people since "your name shall be Abraham" (Genesis 17:5); the last book makes the renaming personal, private, and permanent.

Verses 18–23 · Thyatira · ὁ ἐραυνῶν νεφροὺς καὶ καρδίας ho eraunōn nephrous kai kardias

The longest letter goes to the smallest city. Thyatira — no acropolis, no famous temple, a workshop town of trade guilds on the inland road (its one other New Testament appearance is a businesswoman: Lydia, "a seller of purple from Thyatira," Acts 16:14) — receives the longest of the seven letters, from "the Son of God" (the title's only occurrence in all Revelation), with the furnace-eyes and bronze feet of the vision. The praise is real and rising — "your last works are more than the first," the exact inverse of Ephesus. The complaint is a prophetess the letter code-names Jezebel — as Pergamum's problem was code-named Balaam: Old Testament villains as ciphers for local teachers — whose program is the same guild-feast accommodation ("eat idol-sacrifices"), taught from inside the congregation, with time already given to repent and refused. (A textual note the KJV tradition preserves: some manuscripts read "your wife Jezebel" — one Greek pronoun, and the town's whole drama becomes the angel's own household; the older text reads simply "the woman.")

"The one who searches kidneys and hearts." Kept literal, and worth its strangeness: Hebrew anatomy located the inmost self in the kidneys (kelayot; KJV "reins," from the Latin) — and the sentence is Jehovah's own self-description at Jeremiah 17:10, "I, Jehovah, search the heart and test the kidneys… to give each according to his ways," here spoken by the Son of God in the first person. The same claim closed John 2 a chapter shelf away: "he knew what was in the human." The sickbed sentence is grim, and its idiom is Hebrew to the bone — "I will kill with death" (apoktenō en thanatō), which in the Greek Bible's usage likely means by pestilence; this translation keeps the doubled strangeness and files the reading here.

Verses 24–29 · Thyatira, continued · ποιμανεῖ αὐτοὺς ἐν ῥάβδῳ σιδηρᾷ poimanei autous en rhabdō sidēra

"No other burden." To the rest of Thyatira — those who never learned what the sect grandly called "the deep things of Satan" (the letter quotes their slogan back with visible irony: "as they say") — the demand is almost startlingly light: "I lay on you no other burden; only hold fast what you have, until I come." No new program, no heroics: endurance as the whole assignment.

The iron rod, and the letter of the LXX. The overcomer's promise quotes Psalm 2 — "authority over the nations… he will shepherd them with an iron rod" — and inside that verb sits one of the tidiest text-critical stories on this site. The Hebrew of Psalm 2:9 reads "you will break them" (tero'em); the Greek translators read the same consonants from a different root and wrote "you will shepherd them" (poimaneis) — and Revelation quotes the Greek, then keeps the Hebrew's shattering in the very next clause ("as clay pots are shattered"): both readings of one ancient word, side by side in a single sentence. KJV and Douay flatten to "rule"; ASV margins the truth ("as a shepherd"); this translation prints "shepherd" because that is the word John wrote. Last, the strangest gift in the seven letters: "I will give him the morning star." The book decodes it only on its final page — "I am the root and the offspring of David, the bright morning star" (22:16). The gift is the giver.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "Satan's throne" (thronos — against KJV's "seat"), "white stone" with NWT's "pebble" saluted as exactly right, "slaves" for douloi again, and "shepherd them with an iron rod" (the verb John actually wrote, against the shelf's "rule").

Where it goes its own way: "searches kidneys and hearts" kept anatomically literal (KJV's "reins" honored in the note), "I will kill with death" kept as the Hebraism it is (with the pestilence reading filed), and the synagogue-of-Satan sentence translated plainly and then handled in full — first-century meaning and later abuse both named.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: the tree of life, under guard since Eden's east gate (3:24), promised open in the paradise of God; the new name on the white stone from the God who renamed Abram (17:5); "the first and the last, who was dead" — Smyrna addressed by the Patmos vision's own title (1:17); and the searcher of hearts, who a chapter-shelf away "knew what was in the human" (John 2:25). Planted: the second death, defined at 20:14; the hidden manna (Exodus's jar, waiting for that book); the morning star, decoded at 22:16; and three letters still to deliver — Sardis, Philadelphia, Laodicea, next in this book.

Next installment in this book: Revelation 3 — the letters conclude: Sardis ("you have a name that you live, and you are dead"), Philadelphia's open door, and lukewarm Laodicea.

Daniel 1 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
בִּשְׁנַת שָׁלוֹשׁ, לְמַלְכוּת יְהוֹיָקִים מֶלֶךְ-יְהוּדָה--בָּא נְבוּכַדְנֶאצַּר מֶלֶךְ-בָּבֶל יְרוּשָׁלִַם, וַיָּצַר עָלֶיהָ.
In the third year of the reign of Jehoiakim king of Judah, Nebuchadnezzar king of Babylon came to Jerusalem and laid siege to it.note
2
וַיִּתֵּן אֲדֹנָי בְּיָדוֹ אֶת-יְהוֹיָקִים מֶלֶךְ-יְהוּדָה, וּמִקְצָת כְּלֵי בֵית-הָאֱלֹהִים, וַיְבִיאֵם אֶרֶץ-שִׁנְעָר, בֵּית אֱלֹהָיו; וְאֶת-הַכֵּלִים הֵבִיא, בֵּית אוֹצַר אֱלֹהָיו.
And the Lord gave Jehoiakim king of Judah into his hand, with some of the vessels of the house of God; and he brought them to the land of Shinar, to the house of his god — and the vessels he brought into the treasure-house of his god.note
3
וַיֹּאמֶר הַמֶּלֶךְ, לְאַשְׁפְּנַז רַב סָרִיסָיו: לְהָבִיא מִבְּנֵי יִשְׂרָאֵל, וּמִזֶּרַע הַמְּלוּכָה--וּמִן-הַפַּרְתְּמִים.
And the king told Ashpenaz, the chief of his court officials, to bring some of the sons of Israel — of the royal seed, and of the nobles —note
4
יְלָדִים אֲשֶׁר אֵין-בָּהֶם כָּל-מאוּם וְטוֹבֵי מַרְאֶה וּמַשְׂכִּלִים בְּכָל-חָכְמָה, וְיֹדְעֵי דַעַת וּמְבִינֵי מַדָּע, וַאֲשֶׁר כֹּחַ בָּהֶם, לַעֲמֹד בְּהֵיכַל הַמֶּלֶךְ; וּלְלַמְּדָם סֵפֶר, וּלְשׁוֹן כַּשְׂדִּים.
youths in whom was no blemish — good-looking, skilled in all wisdom, knowing knowledge and understanding learning, with strength in them to stand in the king's palace — to teach them the writing and the tongue of the Chaldeans.note
5
וַיְמַן לָהֶם הַמֶּלֶךְ דְּבַר-יוֹם בְּיוֹמוֹ, מִפַּת-בַּג הַמֶּלֶךְ וּמִיֵּין מִשְׁתָּיו, וּלְגַדְּלָם, שָׁנִים שָׁלוֹשׁ; וּמִקְצָתָם--יַעַמְדוּ, לִפְנֵי הַמֶּלֶךְ.
And the king appointed them a daily portion from the king's food and from the wine he drank — to raise them three years, and at the end of them, they would stand before the king.note
6
וַיְהִי בָהֶם, מִבְּנֵי יְהוּדָה--דָּנִיֵּאל חֲנַנְיָה, מִישָׁאֵל וַעֲזַרְיָה.
Among them, from the sons of Judah, were Daniel, Hananiah, Mishael, and Azariah;note
7
וַיָּשֶׂם לָהֶם שַׂר הַסָּרִיסִים, שֵׁמוֹת; וַיָּשֶׂם לְדָנִיֵּאל בֵּלְטְשַׁאצַּר, וְלַחֲנַנְיָה שַׁדְרַךְ, וּלְמִישָׁאֵל מֵישַׁךְ, וְלַעֲזַרְיָה עֲבֵד נְגוֹ.
and the chief of the officials set names on them: to Daniel he gave Belteshazzar, to Hananiah Shadrach, to Mishael Meshach, and to Azariah Abednego.note
8
וַיָּשֶׂם דָּנִיֵּאל עַל-לִבּוֹ, אֲשֶׁר לֹא-יִתְגָּאַל בְּפַת-בַּג הַמֶּלֶךְ וּבְיֵין מִשְׁתָּיו; וַיְבַקֵּשׁ מִשַּׂר הַסָּרִיסִים, אֲשֶׁר לֹא יִתְגָּאָל.
But Daniel set on his heart that he would not defile himself with the king's food or with the wine he drank; and he asked the chief of the officials that he might not defile himself.note
9
וַיִּתֵּן הָאֱלֹהִים אֶת-דָּנִיֵּאל, לְחֶסֶד וּלְרַחֲמִים, לִפְנֵי, שַׂר הַסָּרִיסִים.
And God gave Daniel kindness and compassion before the chief of the officials.note
10
וַיֹּאמֶר שַׂר הַסָּרִיסִים, לְדָנִיֵּאל--יָרֵא אֲנִי אֶת-אֲדֹנִי הַמֶּלֶךְ, אֲשֶׁר מִנָּה אֶת-מַאֲכַלְכֶם וְאֶת-מִשְׁתֵּיכֶם: אֲשֶׁר לָמָּה יִרְאֶה אֶת-פְּנֵיכֶם זֹעֲפִים, מִן-הַיְלָדִים אֲשֶׁר כְּגִילְכֶם, וְחִיַּבְתֶּם אֶת-רֹאשִׁי, לַמֶּלֶךְ.
And the chief of the officials said to Daniel, "I fear my lord the king, who has appointed your food and your drink. Why should he see your faces more haggard than the youths your own age? You would forfeit my head to the king."note
11
וַיֹּאמֶר דָּנִיֵּאל, אֶל-הַמֶּלְצַר--אֲשֶׁר מִנָּה, שַׂר הַסָּרִיסִים, עַל-דָּנִיֵּאל חֲנַנְיָה, מִישָׁאֵל וַעֲזַרְיָה.
So Daniel said to the steward whom the chief of the officials had appointed over Daniel, Hananiah, Mishael, and Azariah:note
12
נַס-נָא אֶת-עֲבָדֶיךָ, יָמִים עֲשָׂרָה; וְיִתְּנוּ-לָנוּ מִן-הַזֵּרֹעִים, וְנֹאכְלָה--וּמַיִם וְנִשְׁתֶּה.
"Test your servants, please, ten days: let them give us some of the vegetables to eat, and water to drink;note
13
וְיֵרָאוּ לְפָנֶיךָ, מַרְאֵינוּ, וּמַרְאֵה הַיְלָדִים, הָאֹכְלִים אֵת פַּת-בַּג הַמֶּלֶךְ; וְכַאֲשֶׁר תִּרְאֵה, עֲשֵׂה עִם-עֲבָדֶיךָ.
then let our appearance be seen before you, and the appearance of the youths who eat the king's food — and as you see, deal with your servants."note
14
וַיִּשְׁמַע לָהֶם, לַדָּבָר הַזֶּה; וַיְנַסֵּם, יָמִים עֲשָׂרָה.
And he listened to them in this matter, and tested them ten days.note
15
וּמִקְצָת, יָמִים עֲשָׂרָה, נִרְאָה מַרְאֵיהֶם טוֹב, וּבְרִיאֵי בָּשָׂר: מִן-כָּל-הַיְלָדִים--הָאֹכְלִים, אֵת פַּת-בַּג הַמֶּלֶךְ.
And at the end of ten days their appearance was seen to be better, and fuller in flesh, than all the youths who ate the king's food.note
16
וַיְהִי הַמֶּלְצַר, נֹשֵׂא אֶת-פַּת-בָּגָם, וְיֵין, מִשְׁתֵּיהֶם; וְנֹתֵן לָהֶם, זֵרְעֹנִים.
So the steward carried away their food-portion and the wine they were to drink, and kept giving them vegetables.note
17
וְהַיְלָדִים הָאֵלֶּה אַרְבַּעְתָּם, נָתַן לָהֶם הָאֱלֹהִים מַדָּע וְהַשְׂכֵּל בְּכָל-סֵפֶר וְחָכְמָה; וְדָנִיֵּאל הֵבִין, בְּכָל-חָזוֹן וַחֲלֹמוֹת.
And these four youths — God gave them knowledge and skill in all writing and wisdom; and Daniel had understanding in every vision and dream.note
18
וּלְמִקְצָת, הַיָּמִים, אֲשֶׁר-אָמַר הַמֶּלֶךְ, לַהֲבִיאָם; וַיְבִיאֵם שַׂר הַסָּרִיסִים, לִפְנֵי נְבֻכַדְנֶצַּר.
And at the end of the days the king had set for bringing them in, the chief of the officials brought them before Nebuchadnezzar.note
19
וַיְדַבֵּר אִתָּם, הַמֶּלֶךְ, וְלֹא נִמְצָא מִכֻּלָּם, כְּדָנִיֵּאל חֲנַנְיָה מִישָׁאֵל וַעֲזַרְיָה; וַיַּעַמְדוּ, לִפְנֵי הַמֶּלֶךְ.
And the king spoke with them, and among them all none was found like Daniel, Hananiah, Mishael, and Azariah; and they stood before the king.note
20
וְכֹל, דְּבַר חָכְמַת בִּינָה, אֲשֶׁר-בִּקֵּשׁ מֵהֶם, הַמֶּלֶךְ--וַיִּמְצָאֵם עֶשֶׂר יָדוֹת, עַל כָּל-הַחַרְטֻמִּים הָאַשָּׁפִים, אֲשֶׁר, בְּכָל-מַלְכוּתוֹ.
And in every matter of wisdom and understanding that the king sought from them, he found them ten hands better than all the magicians and enchanters in his whole kingdom.note
21
וַיְהִי, דָּנִיֵּאל, עַד-שְׁנַת אַחַת, לְכוֹרֶשׁ הַמֶּלֶךְ. {פ}
And Daniel continued until the first year of King Cyrus.note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–2 · וַיְבִיאֵם אֶרֶץ-שִׁנְעָר vayvi'em erets-Shinar

A fourth book opens on this shelf — and it is the strangest one yet: half court-tales (chapters 1–6), half apocalyptic visions (7–12), and written in two languages — Hebrew here, but at 2:4 the book will switch to Aramaic in mid-verse and stay there through chapter 7: the Bible's longest stretch of its second tongue, the diplomatic language of the empires Daniel served. The Hebrew Bible shelves Daniel among the Writings, between Esther and Ezra; the Greek tradition behind the English Bibles moved him to the Prophets, after Ezekiel. And the book carries the sharpest dating debate on this site so far, reported per the house rule: its setting is the sixth-century exile; much modern scholarship dates the finished book to the second century BC (Greek loanwords in chapter 3's orchestra; visions whose detail tracks history precisely down to Antiochus IV and blurs after 164 BC), while the traditional reading holds the sixth-century claim the book itself makes. Both cases have real arguments; this translation renders the text and files the dispute where it belongs — here.

"The third year of Jehoiakim" — and a date you can check. 605 BC: the year Nebuchadnezzar crushed Egypt at Carchemish and swept south — a campaign fixed by the Babylonian Chronicle tablets, which makes this the first verse on this site pinned by a document outside the Bible (the Chronology page's "third clock," engaged at last). Jeremiah 25:1 calls the same year Jehoiakim's fourth — Babylonian counting starts a reign's "year one" at the first New Year, Judean counting at the accession itself; both point at 605. The theology is in the verb: "the Lord GAVE Jehoiakim into his hand" — natan: Babylon did not win, God handed over; and the same verb will structure the chapter (God gave the king, v2; gave kindness, v9; gave knowledge, v17 — every power in this story is on loan). The vessels go "to the land of Shinar" — not the ordinary word for Babylonia but the archaic name of the tower (Genesis 10:10; 11:2; NIV flattens it to "Babylonia," KJV keeps Shinar, as here): the narrator files this empire, from its first verse, under Babel. One more quiet fact: the divine name never appears in this chapter — "the Lord" (Adonai) and "God" (Elohim) only; Jehovah enters Daniel only in the prayer of chapter 9.

Verses 3–7 · וַיָּשֶׂם לָהֶם … שֵׁמוֹת vayasem lahem … shemot

The empire harvests elites. Royal seed, nobles, "youths in whom was no blemish" (KJV "children in whom was no blemish" — the vocabulary of sacrificial flawlessness, applied to boys), handsome, brilliant — and enrolled in a three-year course in "the writing and tongue of the Chaldeans": cuneiform — Akkadian, plus the scholars' dead Sumerian — the hardest scribal curriculum on earth, all tablet-lists, omen-series and mathematics. Babylon absorbed conquered nations by educating their best (and feeding them: the daily pat-bag, "the king's food" — a Persian loanword sitting in the Hebrew, one of this book's tell-tale foreign words; KJV "the king's meat" — meat = food, the archaism flagged since Genesis 1 — ASV, charmingly, "the king's dainties").

The renamings. Each of the four names carries God: Dani-El, "El is my judge"; Chanan-Yah, "Yah has been gracious"; Misha-El, "who is what El is?"; Azar-Yah, "Yah has helped." Each replacement smuggles in a Babylonian god: Belteshazzar (Bel, protect his life!), Abednego — "servant of Nebo," with the god's name apparently garbled to Nego (scribes misspelling idols on purpose is a real and delicious habit), Shadrach and Meshach obscure. Readers of this site have watched renaming since "your name shall be Abraham" (Genesis 17:5) — and this is that thread, inverted: God renames to bless; the empire renames to erase. Watch the narrator's quiet resistance: the book goes right on calling them Daniel, Hananiah, Mishael, and Azariah.

Verses 8–14 · וַיָּשֶׂם דָּנִיֵּאל עַל-לִבּוֹ vayasem Daniel al-libbo

"Daniel set on his heart." (KJV "purposed in his heart.") The book's hinge sentence — and its puzzle: why would the food defile (yitga'al)? Kosher law does not ban wine, and vegetables were not the only clean option on a royal menu. The readings: meat from idol-sacrifices; unclean species; or — perhaps likeliest — the politics of the table: to eat the king's portion is to be the king's man, and Daniel draws the line at dependence, not diet. Whatever the reason, watch the manner: no protest, no bid for martyrdom — a polite request, and "God gave Daniel kindness and compassion" — chesed (the covenant word of Genesis 19:19) and rachamim, womb-feeling, granted this time in a foreign court.

Ten days of vegetables — history's first controlled trial. A test group, a control group ("the youths who eat the king's food"), a fixed duration, an observable endpoint: "as you see, deal with your servants." The design is so clean that medical historians regularly cite it as the earliest recorded comparative dietary experiment. The word for the diet, zero'im — seed-food — occurs only here in the Bible, and the KJV gave English one of its best archaisms for it: "give us pulse to eat." And "ten days… that you may be tested" has a long echo: when Smyrna is promised "affliction ten days, that you may be tested" (Revelation 2:10), it is speaking Daniel's dialect — the bounded trial, counted and survivable.

Verses 15–17 · נָתַן לָהֶם הָאֱלֹהִים natan lahem ha-Elohim

The verdict, with a straight face. Ten days on, the resistance diet has out-fattened the imperial table — "their appearance was seen to be better, and fuller in flesh" — the chapter's dry humor, and its point: the outcome was never nutritional. Then natan sounds its third beat: "God gave them knowledge and skill in all writing and wisdom" — the curriculum was Babylon's, the aptitude was God's — "and Daniel had understanding in every vision and dream": half a verse, and it is the job description for the entire book (the dream of the statue, the tree, the writing on the wall, and the visions of chapters 7–12 all hang from this line).

Verses 18–21 · עֶשֶׂר יָדוֹת eser yadot

"Ten hands better." The idiom is kept as the Hebrew has it (the shelf smooths to "ten times better" — KJV NIV alike; the hands stay here): in every matter of wisdom the king finds the four exiles ten hands above "all the magicians and enchanters" — and "magicians" is chartummim, an Egyptian loanword: the very court-magician title of Pharaoh's men in the Joseph story. The narrator is quietly filing Babylon's court under Egypt's — Israel has stood before a foreign king's diviners before, and out-dreamed them. (Douay, following the Greek and Latin, calls the king "Nabuchodonosor" throughout — same man, longer journey through the alphabets.)

One verse, one lifetime. "And Daniel continued until the first year of King Cyrus" — from 605 to 539 BC in a single sentence: the boy deported before the temple fell outlives the empire that took him. Babylon's own fall (chapter 5) and Cyrus's first year are pinned by the Nabonidus Chronicle and the Cyrus Cylinder — the exile's end-date is posted at its beginning, and the whole captivity is bracketed inside one chapter. The open break {פ} closes the overture; the curtain rises next on a dream the king refuses to tell.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "youths in whom was no blemish" (the sacrificial vocabulary kept), "set on his heart" (with KJV's "purposed"), and "Shinar" kept archaic with KJV, against the shelf's "Babylonia" glosses.

Where it goes its own way: "ten hands better" kept literal against the whole shelf's "ten times"; "the king's food" for the Persian loanword pat-bag (KJV's "meat" and ASV's "dainties" both saluted); and the book-level apparatus — the mid-verse language switch ahead at 2:4, the canon-placement split, the dating debate — filed openly in the first note.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: Shinar — the temple vessels carried to the tower's own land (11:2); the renaming thread inverted — the God who renamed to bless (17:5), an empire renaming to erase; ten days of testing, the bounded trial Smyrna's letter will speak again (Rev 2:10); and chesed in a foreign court (19:19). Planted: the Aramaic switch (2:4); the temple vessels, waiting for Belshazzar's feast (ch. 5); "every vision and dream," the whole book's warrant; the chartummim, Pharaoh's word, waiting for Joseph (Genesis 41); and Cyrus, named before his story — the exile's terminus posted at its start.

Next installment in this book: Daniel 2 — the dream the king refuses to tell, the statue of four metals — and the Bible switches to Aramaic in the middle of a verse.

Daniel 11 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
וַאֲנִי בִּשְׁנַת אַחַת, לְדָרְיָוֶשׁ הַמָּדִי--עָמְדִי לְמַחֲזִיק וּלְמָעוֹז, לוֹ.
"And I — in the first year of Darius the Mede, I stood up to strengthen and protect him.note
2
וְעַתָּה, אֱמֶת אַגִּיד לָךְ; הִנֵּה-עוֹד שְׁלֹשָׁה מְלָכִים עֹמְדִים לְפָרַס, וְהָרְבִיעִי יַעֲשִׁיר עֹשֶׁר-גָּדוֹל מִכֹּל, וּכְחֶזְקָתוֹ בְעָשְׁרוֹ, יָעִיר הַכֹּל אֵת מַלְכוּת יָוָן.
And now I will tell you the truth. Look — three more kings shall arise for Persia, and the fourth shall grow richer than all of them; and when he is strong through his riches, he shall stir up everything against the kingdom of Greece.note
3
וְעָמַד, מֶלֶךְ גִּבּוֹר; וּמָשַׁל מִמְשָׁל רַב, וְעָשָׂה כִּרְצוֹנוֹ.
Then a mighty king shall arise, and rule a great dominion, and do as he pleases.note
4
וּכְעָמְדוֹ, תִּשָּׁבֵר מַלְכוּתוֹ, וְתֵחָץ, לְאַרְבַּע רוּחוֹת הַשָּׁמָיִם; וְלֹא לְאַחֲרִיתוֹ, וְלֹא כְמָשְׁלוֹ אֲשֶׁר מָשָׁל--כִּי תִנָּתֵשׁ מַלְכוּתוֹ, וְלַאֲחֵרִים מִלְּבַד-אֵלֶּה.
And as he arises, his kingdom shall be broken, and divided toward the four winds of the heavens — not to his posterity, and not like the dominion he ruled; for his kingdom shall be plucked up, and go to others besides these.note
5
וְיֶחֱזַק מֶלֶךְ-הַנֶּגֶב, וּמִן-שָׂרָיו; וְיֶחֱזַק עָלָיו וּמָשָׁל, מִמְשָׁל רַב מֶמְשַׁלְתּוֹ.
And the king of the south shall grow strong — yet one of his commanders shall grow stronger still, and rule; his dominion shall be a great dominion.note
6
וּלְקֵץ שָׁנִים, יִתְחַבָּרוּ, וּבַת מֶלֶךְ-הַנֶּגֶב תָּבוֹא אֶל-מֶלֶךְ הַצָּפוֹן, לַעֲשׂוֹת מֵישָׁרִים; וְלֹא-תַעְצֹר כּוֹחַ הַזְּרוֹעַ, וְלֹא יַעֲמֹד וּזְרֹעוֹ, וְתִנָּתֵן הִיא וּמְבִיאֶיהָ וְהַיֹּלְדָהּ, וּמַחֲזִקָהּ בָּעִתִּים.
And at the end of some years they shall ally themselves, and the daughter of the king of the south shall come to the king of the north to seal fair terms. But she shall not retain the strength of her arm, and he shall not stand, nor his arm; and she shall be given up — she, and those who brought her, and he who fathered her, and he who supported her in those times.note
7
וְעָמַד מִנֵּצֶר שָׁרָשֶׁיהָ, כַּנּוֹ; וְיָבֹא אֶל-הַחַיִל, וְיָבֹא בְּמָעוֹז מֶלֶךְ הַצָּפוֹן, וְעָשָׂה בָהֶם, וְהֶחֱזִיק.
But from a branch of her own roots one shall arise in his place; and he shall come against the army, and enter the fortress of the king of the north, and deal with them, and prevail.note
8
וְגַם אֱלֹהֵיהֶם עִם-נְסִכֵיהֶם עִם-כְּלֵי חֶמְדָּתָם כֶּסֶף וְזָהָב, בַּשְּׁבִי--יָבִא מִצְרָיִם; וְהוּא שָׁנִים יַעֲמֹד, מִמֶּלֶךְ הַצָּפוֹן.
Even their gods, with their cast images and their precious vessels of silver and gold, he shall carry captive into Egypt; then for some years he shall stand off from the king of the north.note
9
וּבָא, בְּמַלְכוּת מֶלֶךְ הַנֶּגֶב, וְשָׁב, אֶל-אַדְמָתוֹ.
And he shall come into the kingdom of the king of the south, but shall return to his own land.note
10
וּבָנָו יִתְגָּרוּ, וְאָסְפוּ הֲמוֹן חֲיָלִים רַבִּים, וּבָא בוֹא, וְשָׁטַף וְעָבָר; וְיָשֹׁב ויתגרו וְיִתְגָּרֶה, עַד-מָעֻזֹּה.
And his sons shall stir themselves for war and gather a multitude of great forces; and one shall surely come, and flood through, and pass over — and he shall return, and wage war, up to his fortress.note
11
וְיִתְמַרְמַר, מֶלֶךְ הַנֶּגֶב, וְיָצָא, וְנִלְחַם עִמּוֹ עִם-מֶלֶךְ הַצָּפוֹן; וְהֶעֱמִיד הָמוֹן רָב, וְנִתַּן הֶהָמוֹן בְּיָדוֹ.
Then the king of the south, embittered, shall go out and fight with him — with the king of the north; and he shall raise a great multitude, and the multitude shall be given into his hand.note
12
וְנִשָּׂא הֶהָמוֹן, ירום וְרָם לְבָבוֹ; וְהִפִּיל רִבֹּאוֹת, וְלֹא יָעוֹז.
And when the multitude is carried off, his heart shall be lifted high; he shall bring down tens of thousands — but he shall not prevail.note
13
וְשָׁב, מֶלֶךְ הַצָּפוֹן, וְהֶעֱמִיד הָמוֹן, רַב מִן-הָרִאשׁוֹן; וּלְקֵץ הָעִתִּים שָׁנִים יָבוֹא בוֹא, בְּחַיִל גָּדוֹל וּבִרְכוּשׁ רָב.
For the king of the north shall return and raise a multitude greater than the first; and at the end of the times — years — he shall surely come, with a great army and abundant supplies.note
14
וּבָעִתִּים הָהֵם, רַבִּים יַעַמְדוּ עַל-מֶלֶךְ הַנֶּגֶב; וּבְנֵי פָּרִיצֵי עַמְּךָ, יִנַּשְּׂאוּ לְהַעֲמִיד חָזוֹן--וְנִכְשָׁלוּ.
And in those times many shall rise against the king of the south; and the sons of the violent among your own people shall lift themselves up, to establish the vision — and they shall stumble.note
15
וְיָבֹא, מֶלֶךְ הַצָּפוֹן, וְיִשְׁפֹּךְ סוֹלְלָה, וְלָכַד עִיר מִבְצָרוֹת; וּזְרֹעוֹת הַנֶּגֶב, לֹא יַעֲמֹדוּ, וְעַם מִבְחָרָיו, וְאֵין כֹּחַ לַעֲמֹד.
And the king of the north shall come and pour out a siege-ramp and capture a fortified city; and the arms of the south shall not stand — not even his picked troops; there shall be no strength to stand.note
16
וְיַעַשׂ הַבָּא אֵלָיו כִּרְצוֹנוֹ, וְאֵין עוֹמֵד לְפָנָיו; וְיַעֲמֹד בְּאֶרֶץ-הַצְּבִי, וְכָלָה בְיָדוֹ.
And he who comes against him shall do as he pleases, and none shall stand before him; and he shall stand in the Beautiful Land, with destruction in his hand.note
17
וְיָשֵׂם פָּנָיו לָבוֹא בְּתֹקֶף כָּל-מַלְכוּתוֹ, וִישָׁרִים עִמּוֹ--וְעָשָׂה; וּבַת הַנָּשִׁים יִתֶּן-לוֹ לְהַשְׁחִיתָהּ, וְלֹא תַעֲמֹד וְלֹא-לוֹ תִהְיֶה.
And he shall set his face to come with the strength of his whole kingdom, bringing fair terms — and he shall carry them out: he shall give him the daughter of women, to ruin it; but she shall not stand for him, and shall not be his.note
18
וישב וְיָשֵׂם פָּנָיו לְאִיִּים, וְלָכַד רַבִּים; וְהִשְׁבִּית קָצִין חֶרְפָּתוֹ לוֹ, בִּלְתִּי חֶרְפָּתוֹ יָשִׁיב לוֹ.
Then he shall turn his face to the coastlands, and capture many; but a commander shall put a stop to his insolence — more, he shall turn his insolence back upon him.note
19
וְיָשֵׁב פָּנָיו, לְמָעוּזֵּי אַרְצוֹ; וְנִכְשַׁל וְנָפַל, וְלֹא יִמָּצֵא.
So he shall turn his face back to the fortresses of his own land — and he shall stumble, and fall, and be found no more.note
20
וְעָמַד עַל-כַּנּוֹ מַעֲבִיר נוֹגֵשׂ, הֶדֶר מַלְכוּת; וּבְיָמִים אֲחָדִים יִשָּׁבֵר, וְלֹא בְאַפַּיִם וְלֹא בְמִלְחָמָה.
Then in his place shall arise one who sends an exactor of tribute through the glory of the kingdom; but within a few days he shall be broken — not in anger, and not in battle.note
21
וְעָמַד עַל-כַּנּוֹ נִבְזֶה, וְלֹא-נָתְנוּ עָלָיו הוֹד מַלְכוּת; וּבָא בְשַׁלְוָה, וְהֶחֱזִיק מַלְכוּת בַּחֲלַקְלַקּוֹת.
And in his place shall arise a contemptible man, on whom royal majesty was never conferred; he shall come in quietly, and seize the kingdom by smooth intrigues.note
22
וּזְרֹעוֹת הַשֶּׁטֶף יִשָּׁטְפוּ מִלְּפָנָיו, וְיִשָּׁבֵרוּ; וְגַם, נְגִיד בְּרִית.
And the arms of the flood shall be flooded away before him, and broken — and also the prince of the covenant.note
23
וּמִן-הִתְחַבְּרוּת אֵלָיו, יַעֲשֶׂה מִרְמָה; וְעָלָה וְעָצַם, בִּמְעַט-גּוֹי.
And from the time an alliance is made with him, he shall work deceit; and he shall rise, and grow strong, with a small nation.note
24
בְּשַׁלְוָה וּבְמִשְׁמַנֵּי מְדִינָה, יָבוֹא, וְעָשָׂה אֲשֶׁר לֹא-עָשׂוּ אֲבֹתָיו וַאֲבוֹת אֲבֹתָיו, בִּזָּה וְשָׁלָל וּרְכוּשׁ לָהֶם יִבְזוֹר; וְעַל מִבְצָרִים יְחַשֵּׁב מַחְשְׁבֹתָיו, וְעַד-עֵת.
Quietly he shall come into the richest parts of the province, and do what his fathers never did, nor his fathers' fathers: plunder and spoil and goods he shall scatter among his followers; and against the strongholds he shall devise his schemes — but only for a time.note
25
וְיָעֵר כֹּחוֹ וּלְבָבוֹ עַל-מֶלֶךְ הַנֶּגֶב, בְּחַיִל גָּדוֹל, וּמֶלֶךְ הַנֶּגֶב יִתְגָּרֶה לַמִּלְחָמָה, בְּחַיִל-גָּדוֹל וְעָצוּם עַד-מְאֹד; וְלֹא יַעֲמֹד, כִּי-יַחְשְׁבוּ עָלָיו מַחֲשָׁבוֹת.
And he shall stir up his power and his heart against the king of the south with a great army; and the king of the south shall be roused for war with a very great and mighty army — but he shall not stand, for schemes shall be devised against him.note
26
וְאֹכְלֵי פַת-בָּגוֹ יִשְׁבְּרוּהוּ, וְחֵילוֹ יִשְׁטוֹף; וְנָפְלוּ, חֲלָלִים רַבִּים.
The eaters of his own food-portion shall break him; his army shall be flooded away, and many shall fall slain.note
27
וּשְׁנֵיהֶם הַמְּלָכִים לְבָבָם לְמֵרָע, וְעַל-שֻׁלְחָן אֶחָד כָּזָב יְדַבֵּרוּ; וְלֹא תִצְלָח, כִּי-עוֹד קֵץ לַמּוֹעֵד.
And the two kings — their hearts set on evil, at one table they shall speak lies; but it shall not succeed, for the end is still for the appointed time.note
28
וְיָשֹׁב אַרְצוֹ בִּרְכוּשׁ גָּדוֹל, וּלְבָבוֹ עַל-בְּרִית קֹדֶשׁ; וְעָשָׂה, וְשָׁב לְאַרְצוֹ.
Then he shall return to his land with great goods, and his heart set against the holy covenant; and he shall act — and return to his own land.note
29
לַמּוֹעֵד יָשׁוּב, וּבָא בַנֶּגֶב; וְלֹא-תִהְיֶה כָרִאשֹׁנָה, וְכָאַחֲרוֹנָה.
At the appointed time he shall return, and come into the south — but this last time shall not be as the first.note
30
וּבָאוּ בוֹ צִיִּים כִּתִּים, וְנִכְאָה, וְשָׁב וְזָעַם עַל-בְּרִית-קוֹדֶשׁ, וְעָשָׂה; וְשָׁב וְיָבֵן, עַל-עֹזְבֵי בְּרִית קֹדֶשׁ.
For ships of Kittim shall come against him, and he shall lose heart; and he shall turn back, and rage against the holy covenant, and act — he shall turn back, and give heed to those who forsake the holy covenant.note
31
וּזְרֹעִים, מִמֶּנּוּ יַעֲמֹדוּ; וְחִלְּלוּ הַמִּקְדָּשׁ הַמָּעוֹז, וְהֵסִירוּ הַתָּמִיד, וְנָתְנוּ, הַשִּׁקּוּץ מְשֹׁמֵם.
And forces from him shall arise and profane the sanctuary, the fortress; and they shall remove the daily offering, and set up the abomination that desolates.note
32
וּמַרְשִׁיעֵי בְרִית, יַחֲנִיף בַּחֲלַקּוֹת; וְעַם יֹדְעֵי אֱלֹהָיו, יַחֲזִקוּ וְעָשׂוּ.
Those who act wickedly against the covenant he shall corrupt with smooth words — but the people who know their God shall stand firm, and act.note
33
וּמַשְׂכִּילֵי עָם, יָבִינוּ לָרַבִּים; וְנִכְשְׁלוּ בְּחֶרֶב וּבְלֶהָבָה, בִּשְׁבִי וּבְבִזָּה--יָמִים.
And the wise among the people shall give understanding to many; yet they shall stumble by sword and by flame, by captivity and by plunder — for days.note
34
וּבְהִכָּשְׁלָם, יֵעָזְרוּ עֵזֶר מְעָט; וְנִלְווּ עֲלֵיהֶם רַבִּים, בַּחֲלַקְלַקּוֹת.
And when they stumble, they shall be helped with a little help; and many shall join them with smooth talk.note
35
וּמִן-הַמַּשְׂכִּילִים יִכָּשְׁלוּ, לִצְרוֹף בָּהֶם וּלְבָרֵר וְלַלְבֵּן--עַד-עֵת קֵץ: כִּי-עוֹד, לַמּוֹעֵד.
And some of the wise shall stumble — to refine among them, and to purify, and to make white, until the time of the end; for it is still for the appointed time.note
36
וְעָשָׂה כִרְצֹנוֹ הַמֶּלֶךְ, וְיִתְרוֹמֵם וְיִתְגַּדֵּל עַל-כָּל-אֵל, וְעַל אֵל אֵלִים, יְדַבֵּר נִפְלָאוֹת; וְהִצְלִיחַ עַד-כָּלָה זַעַם, כִּי נֶחֱרָצָה נֶעֱשָׂתָה.
And the king shall do as he pleases; he shall exalt himself and magnify himself above every god, and against the God of gods he shall speak astonishing things; and he shall prosper — until the indignation is finished, for what is decreed shall be done.note
37
וְעַל-אֱלֹהֵי אֲבֹתָיו לֹא יָבִין, וְעַל-חֶמְדַּת נָשִׁים וְעַל-כָּל-אֱלוֹהַּ לֹא יָבִין: כִּי עַל-כֹּל, יִתְגַּדָּל.
To the gods of his fathers he shall give no heed — nor to the one beloved of women, nor to any god at all — for above them all he shall magnify himself.note
38
וְלֶאֱלֹהַּ, מָעֻזִּים, עַל-כַּנּוֹ, יְכַבֵּד; וְלֶאֱלוֹהַּ אֲשֶׁר לֹא-יְדָעֻהוּ אֲבֹתָיו, יְכַבֵּד בְּזָהָב וּבְכֶסֶף וּבְאֶבֶן יְקָרָה--וּבַחֲמֻדוֹת.
But in their place he shall honor a god of fortresses — a god his fathers never knew he shall honor with gold and silver, with precious stone and costly things.note
39
וְעָשָׂה לְמִבְצְרֵי מָעֻזִּים, עִם-אֱלוֹהַּ נֵכָר, אֲשֶׁר הכיר יַכִּיר, יַרְבֶּה כָבוֹד; וְהִמְשִׁילָם, בָּרַבִּים, וַאֲדָמָה, יְחַלֵּק בִּמְחִיר.
And he shall deal with the strongest fortresses by the help of a foreign god; whoever acknowledges him he shall load with honor — he shall make them rulers over many, and parcel out land for a price.note
40
וּבְעֵת קֵץ, יִתְנַגַּח עִמּוֹ מֶלֶךְ הַנֶּגֶב, וְיִשְׂתָּעֵר עָלָיו מֶלֶךְ הַצָּפוֹן בְּרֶכֶב וּבְפָרָשִׁים, וּבָאֳנִיּוֹת רַבּוֹת; וּבָא בַאֲרָצוֹת, וְשָׁטַף וְעָבָר.
And at the time of the end, the king of the south shall lock horns with him; and the king of the north shall storm against him with chariots and horsemen and many ships — and he shall come into the lands, and flood through, and pass over.note
41
וּבָא בְּאֶרֶץ הַצְּבִי, וְרַבּוֹת יִכָּשֵׁלוּ; וְאֵלֶּה, יִמָּלְטוּ מִיָּדוֹ--אֱדוֹם וּמוֹאָב, וְרֵאשִׁית בְּנֵי עַמּוֹן.
And he shall come into the Beautiful Land, and many shall stumble; but these shall escape his hand: Edom, and Moab, and the chief part of the sons of Ammon.note
42
וְיִשְׁלַח יָדוֹ, בַּאֲרָצוֹת; וְאֶרֶץ מִצְרַיִם, לֹא תִהְיֶה לִפְלֵיטָה.
And he shall stretch out his hand against the lands, and the land of Egypt shall not escape.note
43
וּמָשַׁל, בְּמִכְמַנֵּי הַזָּהָב וְהַכֶּסֶף, וּבְכֹל, חֲמֻדוֹת מִצְרָיִם--וְלֻבִים וְכֻשִׁים, בְּמִצְעָדָיו.
And he shall rule the hidden stores of gold and silver and all the precious things of Egypt, with Libyans and Cushites at his steps.note
44
וּשְׁמֻעוֹת יְבַהֲלֻהוּ, מִמִּזְרָח וּמִצָּפוֹן; וְיָצָא בְּחֵמָא גְדֹלָה, לְהַשְׁמִיד וּלְהַחֲרִים רַבִּים.
But reports out of the east and the north shall terrify him, and he shall go out in great fury, to destroy and devote many to destruction.note
45
וְיִטַּע אָהֳלֵי אַפַּדְנוֹ, בֵּין יַמִּים לְהַר-צְבִי-קֹדֶשׁ; וּבָא, עַד-קִצּוֹ, וְאֵין עוֹזֵר, לוֹ.
And he shall plant his palace-tents between the sea and the beautiful holy mountain — and he shall come to his end, with none to help him.note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–4 · מַלְכוּת יָוָן malkhut Yavan

You are entering a vision in progress. Chapters 10–12 of Daniel are a single scene, and this page opens at its middle: back in chapter 10 (not yet on this site), Daniel — in "the third year of Cyrus" — has fasted three weeks; a shining figure has arrived, apologizing for the delay ("the prince of Persia withstood me twenty-one days, and Michael… came to help me"); and it is that angel who speaks every verse of this chapter. His opening "I stood up to strengthen him" (v1) reaches back into that frame — and "the first year of Darius the Mede" names this book's famous puzzle: no such king appears in the Babylonian or Persian records between Belshazzar and Cyrus. The proposals: a throne-name of Cyrus himself; Gubaru, the governor who took Babylon the night it fell; or an error of the tradition — reported, not adjudicated. (A quibble readers catch: 1:21 said Daniel continued "until the first year of Cyrus," yet chapter 10 has him active in Cyrus's third — 1:21 marks the exile's end-point he lived to see, not his last day at work.)

Persia in one verse, Alexander in two. "Three more kings… and the fourth, richer than all, shall stir up everything against the kingdom of Greece" — Yavan, Javan, the Table of Nations' own name for the Greek world (Genesis 10:2; KJV "Grecia") — the fourth being Xerxes, whose invasion of Greece (480 BC) bought a grudge Greece never dropped. Then "a mighty king… and as he arises, his kingdom shall be broken toward the four winds — not to his posterity": Alexander, dead at 32 in Babylon, his empire quartered among his generals, his heirs murdered — the chapter's method in miniature: no one after Darius is ever named; a century and a half of history proceeds entirely in pronouns.

Verses 5–20 · מֶלֶךְ-הַנֶּגֶב … מֶלֶךְ הַצָּפוֹן melekh-hanegev … melekh hatsafon

The chess match — and where the reader lives. "The king of the south" is the house of Ptolemy in Egypt; "the king of the north," the house of Seleucus in Syria — and the Beautiful Land lies exactly between, so every march in this chapter goes past Jerusalem's door. The correspondences here are of a density unmatched anywhere in prophecy — a partial register, in order: the strong commander who outgrows his king (v5: Seleucus I, once Ptolemy I's officer); the daughter sent north to seal terms and "given up — she, and those who brought her, and he who fathered her" (v6: Berenice, married to Antiochus II, murdered with her child and household — some read "he who fathered her" as "her child"; the historical fit is grimly good either way); the "branch of her roots" who storms north in revenge and hauls home "even their gods… captive into Egypt" (vv 7-8: Ptolemy III, whose own inscriptions boast of bringing the gods back — the trophy-theology of Daniel 1:2, replayed between pagans); the raging counter-multitude "given into his hand" (v11: Raphia, 217 BC, the great elephant battle); the return "with a great army" that the south cannot stand (vv 13-16: Antiochus III, Panium, 200 BC — the year Judea passed from south to north); the "daughter of women" given "to ruin it" who "shall not stand for him" (v17: Cleopatra I, planted as a Trojan bride — who sided with her husband); the turn to the coastlands, stopped by "a commander" who turned his insolence home (v18: Rome's Scipio, Magnesia, 190 BC); the ignominious stumble (v19: Antiochus III, killed plundering a temple for tribute money); and the successor who "sends an exactor of tribute" and is broken "not in anger, and not in battle" (v20: Seleucus IV, whose tax-man Heliodorus raided Jerusalem's temple — the story of 2 Maccabees 3 — and then, it seems, poisoned his king). The dating debate planted at Daniel 1 lives exactly here: this density is either prophecy of stupefying precision, or history told as prophecy up to the writer's own day — hold the question; verse 40 will sharpen it.

The scribes' double track. Several verses in this stretch carry a ketiv/qere — the Masoretes' "written / read" apparatus: where tradition read a word differently than the consonants write it, they left the written letters standing and put the reading beside them (vv 10, 12, 18 here, 39 below — visible in the Hebrew column as an unpointed word beside its pointed twin). Printed as received: the manuscript's own honesty, kept.

Verses 21–28 · וְעָמַד עַל-כַּנּוֹ נִבְזֶה ve'amad al-kanno nivzeh

"A contemptible man." Enter the chapter's center of gravity — never named, like everyone here, but wearing one king's biography: Antiochus IV, who styled himself Epiphanes, "God Manifest" (contemporaries punned Epimanes, "the madman"). Not the heir — "royal majesty was never conferred on him" — he slid onto the throne "by smooth intrigues" while the true heir sat hostage in Rome. "The prince of the covenant" broken (v22) is read as Onias III, the legitimate high priest, deposed and later murdered (171 BC; 2 Maccabees 4 tells it) — the empire now selling Israel's priesthood to the highest bidder. The first Egyptian war (vv 25-27) comes off exactly as written: the south's army vast and useless, broken from inside — "the eaters of his own food-portion shall break him": pat-bag, the king's-table word of Daniel 1:5 — the table Daniel refused becomes the table that devours a king. "Two kings at one table, speaking lies" (v27): Antiochus and his puppet-nephew Ptolemy, uncle and ward toasting each other's ruin. And coming home heavy with Egyptian plunder, "his heart set against the holy covenant… and he shall act" (v28) — the temple treasury stripped, 169 BC.

Verses 29–35 · צִיִּים כִּתִּים … הַשִּׁקּוּץ מְשֹׁמֵם tsiyyim Kittim … hashiqquts meshomem

The ships of Kittim, and a circle in the sand. Kittim is Javan's son from the Table of Nations (Genesis 10:4) — first Cyprus, then the Greek west, and by now the newest western power: Rome (the Dead Sea Scrolls read Kittim as the Romans too; KJV "Chittim," while NIV translates the name away as "western coastlands"). The scene behind the verse is one of ancient history's greatest: Antiochus, mid-conquest of Egypt, 168 BC, met at Eleusis by the Roman envoy Popillius Laenas, who handed him the Senate's ultimatum, drew a circle around him in the sand with his stick, and told him to answer before stepping out of it. He stepped out a client, "and he shall lose heart" — and turned his humiliation on Jerusalem.

The abomination that desolates. December 167 BC: the garrison in the temple-fortress, the daily offering halted, and on the altar of burnt offering "the abomination that desolates" (KJV "the abomination that maketh desolate") — an altar to Olympian Zeus; swine offered, Torah scrolls burned, the covenant made a capital crime. When 1 Maccabees 1:54 records the day, it reaches for this verse's own phrase — "they erected a desolating abomination upon the altar" — the earliest reader of Daniel we can watch reading Daniel. (Jesus will lift the phrase again toward a horizon of his own: "the abomination of desolation, spoken of by Daniel the prophet… let the reader understand," Matthew 24:15.) Against the terror the chapter sets not warriors but "the people who know their God" and the maskilim, "the wise," who "give understanding to many" and fall by sword and flame — the martyrs of the persecution; the armed Maccabean revolt itself appears, on the common reading, only as "a little help" (v34) — the book's cool, deliberate distance from the sword. Their falling "refines, purifies, makes white" — the metallurgy of martyrdom, until the appointed time.

Verses 36–39 · וְעַל אֵל אֵלִים יְדַבֵּר נִפְלָאוֹת ve'al El elim yedabber niflaot

The king above every god. Self-exaltation "above every god," "astonishing things against the God of gods" — Antiochus's own coins escalate to THEOU EPIPHANOUS, "of God Manifest," and his program made him, for the tradition, the very type of the final blasphemer. The details keep their Hellenistic fit: "no heed… to the one beloved of women" — Tammuz-Adonis, the dying god the women of the Levant wept for (Ezekiel 8:14 shows the weeping at Jerusalem's own gate); honor instead to "a god of fortresses" (KJV "the god of forces," ASV "the god of fortresses") — read as Zeus Olympios of the citadel cult, or Jupiter Capitolinus whose temple he lavished, or, unsettlingly, simply war itself, honored "with gold and silver" — garrisons endowed like shrines, loyalty bought with parceled land "for a price" (v39): the Akra citadel planted in Jerusalem, and confiscated Judean land in its gift.

Verses 40–45 · וּבְעֵת קֵץ uve'et qets

The seam — said plainly. "At the time of the end" a last campaign unrolls: a whirlwind victory through the lands, Egypt looted, "Libyans and Cushites at his steps" (KJV "the Libyans and the Ethiopians"), alarms from east and north, and the king's end "between the sea and the beautiful holy mountain… with none to help him." And here the astonishing historical fit stops: Antiochus IV mounted no such final conquest of Egypt, and he died at Tabae in Persia, late 164 BC, campaigning eastward — not encamped in the Land at all. This seam is the oldest argument about the book. The pagan philosopher Porphyry (3rd century AD) read it exactly as a modern critic does: accurate "prophecy" up to the author's own moment (~166 BC), genuine — and unfulfilled — expectation beyond it; we know his argument because Jerome preserved it in his Daniel commentary in order to fight it, answering that from verse 36 (or here at 40) the subject quietly shifts from Antiochus to a final enemy at the end of days — the "gap" reading held by traditional interpreters ever since, with Antiochus as the near figure and type. One reading hears a fingerprint; the other, a telescope. Both are stated here at full strength, per this project's standing rule — and the verse itself is translated as it stands, matching neither side's convenience.

Old neighbors on a late map. Who escapes the last king's hand? "Edom, and Moab, and the chief part of the sons of Ammon" — Esau's nation and the two born in Lot's cave (Genesis 19:37-38), still on the map, still next door, four centuries after Genesis closed. "He shall plant his palace-tents" — appadno, a Persian loanword so unfamiliar that the Vulgate took it for a place-name, and Douay duly prints "he shall fix his tabernacle Apadno between the seas" — a phantom town born of one hard word, sitting on the shelf to this day. "And he shall come to his end, with none to help him" — on either reading of the seam, the chapter's verdict on every empire in it. The next page of the book is the reason all this was worth telling: "at that time Michael shall stand up… and many who sleep in the dust of the earth shall awake."

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "the abomination that desolates" (with the KJV family), "a god of fortresses" (with ASV, against KJV's vaguer "forces"), "Kittim" kept as the name it is (against NIV's "western coastlands"), and the pronouns left unnamed — the chapter's own method — with the identifications confined to the notes.

Where it goes its own way: "the Beautiful Land" for erets ha-tsvi (KJV "the glorious land"; NWT, memorably, "the land of the Decoration"), "palace-tents" for the loanword that became Douay's phantom town Apadno, "lock horns" for the goring-verb of verse 40, and the seam of verses 40-45 reported with both readings at full strength rather than smoothed toward either.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: Kittim and Javan, sons of the Table of Nations (10:4, 10:2), grown into Rome and Greece; the Beautiful Land — the everlasting possession of (17:8) as a chessboard square between empires; gods and vessels carried off as trophies, the theology of (1:2); the pat-bag — Daniel's refused portion (1:8) breaking a king at his own table; and Edom, Moab, and Ammon — Lot's cave-born kin (19:37) — still next door at history's end. Planted: "Michael shall stand up," the dust that wakes, and the sealed book — Daniel 12, this book's next page; and the abomination-phrase, waiting on the Mount of Olives (Matthew 24:15).

Next installment in this book: Daniel 12 — "many who sleep in the dust of the earth shall awake": the Bible's clearest early resurrection text, the sealed scroll, and the numbered days.

Daniel 12 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
וּבָעֵת הַהִיא יַעֲמֹד מִיכָאֵל הַשַּׂר הַגָּדוֹל, הָעֹמֵד עַל-בְּנֵי עַמֶּךָ, וְהָיְתָה עֵת צָרָה, אֲשֶׁר לֹא-נִהְיְתָה מִהְיוֹת גּוֹי עַד הָעֵת הַהִיא; וּבָעֵת הַהִיא יִמָּלֵט עַמְּךָ, כָּל-הַנִּמְצָא כָּתוּב בַּסֵּפֶר.
"And at that time Michael shall stand up, the great prince who stands over the sons of your people; and there shall be a time of distress such as has never been since there was a nation until that time. And at that time your people shall escape — everyone found written in the book.note
2
וְרַבִּים, מִיְּשֵׁנֵי אַדְמַת-עָפָר יָקִיצוּ; אֵלֶּה לְחַיֵּי עוֹלָם, וְאֵלֶּה לַחֲרָפוֹת לְדִרְאוֹן עוֹלָם. {ס}
And many of those sleeping in the ground of dust shall wake — these to everlasting life, and these to reproaches, to everlasting abhorrence.note
3
וְהַמַּשְׂכִּלִים--יַזְהִרוּ, כְּזֹהַר הָרָקִיעַ; וּמַצְדִּיקֵי, הָרַבִּים, כַּכּוֹכָבִים, לְעוֹלָם וָעֶד. {פ}
And the wise shall shine like the shining of the vault; and those who bring the many to righteousness, like the stars, forever and ever.note
4
וְאַתָּה דָנִיֵּאל, סְתֹם הַדְּבָרִים וַחֲתֹם הַסֵּפֶר--עַד-עֵת קֵץ; יְשֹׁטְטוּ רַבִּים, וְתִרְבֶּה הַדָּעַת.
And you, Daniel — shut up the words and seal the book until the time of the end. Many shall rove about, and knowledge shall multiply."note
5
וְרָאִיתִי אֲנִי דָנִיֵּאל, וְהִנֵּה שְׁנַיִם אֲחֵרִים עֹמְדִים: אֶחָד הֵנָּה לִשְׂפַת הַיְאֹר, וְאֶחָד הֵנָּה לִשְׂפַת הַיְאֹר.
Then I, Daniel, looked — and look: two others standing, one here on the bank of the river, and one here on the bank of the river.note
6
וַיֹּאמֶר, לָאִישׁ לְבוּשׁ הַבַּדִּים, אֲשֶׁר מִמַּעַל, לְמֵימֵי הַיְאֹר: עַד-מָתַי, קֵץ הַפְּלָאוֹת.
And he said to the man clothed in linen, who was above the waters of the river: "How long until the end of the wonders?"note
7
וָאֶשְׁמַע אֶת-הָאִישׁ לְבוּשׁ הַבַּדִּים, אֲשֶׁר מִמַּעַל לְמֵימֵי הַיְאֹר, וַיָּרֶם יְמִינוֹ וּשְׂמֹאלוֹ אֶל-הַשָּׁמַיִם, וַיִּשָּׁבַע בְּחֵי הָעוֹלָם: כִּי לְמוֹעֵד מוֹעֲדִים וָחֵצִי, וּכְכַלּוֹת נַפֵּץ יַד-עַם-קֹדֶשׁ--תִּכְלֶינָה כָל-אֵלֶּה.
And I heard the man clothed in linen, who was above the waters of the river; and he raised his right hand and his left hand to the heavens, and swore by the One who lives forever: that it is for an appointed time, appointed times, and a half — and when the shattering of the hand of the holy people is finished, all these things shall be finished.note
8
וַאֲנִי שָׁמַעְתִּי, וְלֹא אָבִין; וָאֹמְרָה--אֲדֹנִי, מָה אַחֲרִית אֵלֶּה. {פ}
And I heard, but I did not understand. And I said: "My lord, what shall come after these things?"note
9
וַיֹּאמֶר, לֵךְ דָּנִיֵּאל: כִּי-סְתֻמִים וַחֲתֻמִים הַדְּבָרִים, עַד-עֵת קֵץ.
And he said: "Go, Daniel — for the words are shut up and sealed until the time of the end.note
10
יִתְבָּרְרוּ וְיִתְלַבְּנוּ וְיִצָּרְפוּ, רַבִּים, וְהִרְשִׁיעוּ רְשָׁעִים, וְלֹא יָבִינוּ כָּל-רְשָׁעִים; וְהַמַּשְׂכִּלִים, יָבִינוּ.
Many shall purify themselves, and make themselves white, and be refined; and the wicked shall act wickedly — and none of the wicked shall understand, but the wise shall understand.note

Happy the one who waits, and reaches one thousand three hundred and thirty-five days.note
13
וְאַתָּה, לֵךְ לַקֵּץ; וְתָנוּחַ וְתַעֲמֹד לְגֹרָלְךָ, לְקֵץ הַיָּמִין. {ש}
And you — go on to the end. And you shall rest, and you shall stand up to your lot at the end of the days."note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verse 1 · מִיכָאֵל הַשַּׂר הַגָּדוֹל Mikha'el hassar haggadol

Michael stands up. The verb is amad — the word chapter 11 marched on, king after king "arising" and "standing" — handed at last to someone worth it: Michael, "the great prince who stands over the sons of your people" — the guardian-advocate introduced back in the vision's opening frame (10:13, 21) as "one of the chief princes," fighting the unseen "princes" of Persia and Greece. His name is itself a rhetorical question — mi-kha-El, "Who is like God?" — an angel named after the answer no one. The "time of distress such as has never been" (KJV "a time of trouble, such as never was"; NIV "a time of distress") is the phrase Jesus reaches for on the Mount of Olives (Matthew 24:21) — one breath after quoting this book's "abomination that desolates."

The escape-verb, paid. "Your people shall escape" — yimmalet, the very verb of 11:41, where Edom, Moab, and Ammon slipped the last king's hand. The old neighbors escaped by geography; here the measure is different — "everyone found written in the book." A ledger with names in it runs through scripture: Moses offers to be blotted out of "your book" (Exodus 32:32), Malachi's "book of remembrance," and Revelation's book of life. Deliverance, this verse says, is by enrollment, not by borders.

Verse 2 · אַדְמַת-עָפָר admat-afar

The Hebrew Bible's clearest resurrection sentence. Hints came earlier — but this verse is the first to say it flat out, both directions: sleepers waking, "these to everlasting life, and these to reproaches, to everlasting abhorrence" (the Hebrew really does point twice — elleh… ve'elleh, "these… and these"; KJV "some… and some"). Where they sleep is chosen with a jeweler's care: admat-afar, "the ground of dust" — adamah and afar, the two words of Eden's making and Eden's sentence ("dust from the ground," Genesis 2:7; "dust you are, and to dust you shall return," 3:19). The oldest sentence in the book is answered with an alarm clock.

Two phrases with long futures. "Everlasting life" — chayyei olam — occurs exactly once in the entire Hebrew Bible: here. John's Gospel will make the Greek of it (zōē aiōnios) its heartbeat, and John 5:28-29 is very nearly this verse translated — "those in the tombs… shall come out, to a resurrection of life… of judgment." At the other pole, dir'on, "abhorrence," appears only twice in scripture: here, and in Isaiah 66:24's closing image of corpses "whose worm shall not die" — the picture Jesus borrows for Gehenna. (NWT 1984 renders both poles with its signature clock: "indefinitely lasting life… indefinitely lasting abhorrence.") And the verse says "many… shall wake," not "all" — readers ancient and modern have argued whether that is selection or simply Hebrew's way of saying "the multitudes"; the honest report is that the text says many, and its notes should too.

Verses 3–4 · כְּזֹהַר הָרָקִיעַ ke-zohar haraqia

The teachers' reward. "The wise" — the maskilim of 11:33-35, who gave understanding to many and fell by sword and flame — "shall shine like the shining of the vault": the cognate kept (yazhiru ke-zohar), and raqia rendered as this project has rendered it since day two of creation — the hammered vault of Genesis 1:6 (KJV "the brightness of the firmament"). The martyrs' brightness is measured against the sky God beat out over Eden; "those who bring the many to righteousness, like the stars" — the very currency of the promise to Abram, out under the night sky of Genesis 15:5. Eight centuries later, the masterwork of Jewish mysticism took its title from this verse's word: the Zohar, "Radiance." And quietly, a thread: many wake (v2), the many made righteous (v3), many rove (v4), many refined (v10) — rabbim is this chapter's drumbeat.

Seal the book — and the famous half-verse. "Shut up the words and seal the book" — setom… chatom, a rhyme in the Hebrew — is apocalyptic notary practice: a witnessed document closed until its date (and the exact order Revelation will countermand for its own scroll — "do not seal… for the time is near," Revelation 1:3 announcing the unsealing, 22:10 giving the order). Then the half-verse every generation reads its own age into: "many shall rove about, and knowledge shall multiply" (KJV "many shall run to and fro, and knowledge shall be increased"; Douay, from the Vulgate, goes its own way — "Many shall pass over, and knowledge shall be manifold"). Moderns have heard jets and the internet in it. The roving-verb (shut) elsewhere describes searching — eyes ranging the earth, seekers roving for the word of God and not finding it (Amos 8:12, its mirror image) — so the likelier sense is readers roving through the sealed book itself, understanding multiplying as the end approaches (v10 agrees: "the wise shall understand"). Both readings are on the table; the sentence has earned its fame either way.

Verses 5–7 · לְמוֹעֵד מוֹעֲדִים וָחֵצִי le-mo'ed mo'adim va-chetsi

A courtroom on a riverbank. Two more figures appear, "one here on the bank of the river, and one here on the bank of the river" — the sentence repeated whole, as the scroll repeats it: a witness stationed on each bank, the Torah's arithmetic for establishing a matter. The river-word is a small treasure: ye'or — Egypt's own word for the Nile, the river of Pharaoh's dreams — borrowed here, uniquely, for the great river of this vision (10:4 names it: the Tigris). "And he said" — the Hebrew never says who (KJV supplies "And one said"): one of the two witnesses, presumably, asking the question the whole book has been holding — "How long?"

The oath with both hands. The Bible's oath posture is one raised hand — Abram: "I have raised my hand to Jehovah" (Genesis 14:22). The man in linen above the waters raises "his right hand and his left hand to the heavens" — the oath doubled, sworn "by the One who lives forever," to answer a question about time by the only party outside it. The answer: "an appointed time, appointed times, and a half" — mo'ed, the word of Genesis 1:14, where the lights were hung "for appointed times"; time itself is measured out in the calendar God installed on day four. (KJV "a time, times, and an half"; NWT, for once beside this translation, "an appointed time, appointed times and a half.") The shape is 1 + 2 + ½: three and a half — the broken seven, persecution's signature number, the same span the book's Aramaic half calls "a time, times and half a time" (7:25) and Revelation will count out as 1,260 days and 42 months. And the hinge: "when the shattering of the hand of the holy people is finished" — hand meaning power (KJV "to scatter the power") — "all these things shall be finished": the finishing-verb doubled, kalah answering kalah. Rock bottom, the oath swears, is on the schedule — and so is the end of it.

Verses 8–10 · וְלֹא אָבִין velo avin

The canon's most comforting admission. "I heard, but I did not understand." The seer himself — shown the entire scroll, angel at his side — doesn't get it, says so, asks a follow-up ("what shall come after these things?" — acharit, the after-part, the outcome), and is not rebuked. He is dismissed with his question unanswered: "Go, Daniel — for the words are shut up and sealed" (v4's rhyming pair, paid). Every honest reader of this book has been in good company since its first reader.

The furnace, finished. "Many shall be purified, made white, refined" — the same three-verb metallurgy the martyrs' falling was said to work in 11:35, now promised its completion. Meanwhile "the wicked shall act wickedly" — the cognate kept (hirshi'u resha'im) — "and none of the wicked shall understand": in this book understanding is a moral capacity, not an intellectual one; the sealed words stay sealed from a certain kind of reader. "But the wise shall understand" — the maskilim's final appearance, holding the book's last classroom.

Verses 11–13 · אַשְׁרֵי הַמְחַכֶּה ashrei hamechakkeh

1,290 and 1,335 — the numbers nobody has decoded. From the halted offering and the abomination that desolates (11:31's date: 15 Kislev, 167 BC), 1,290 days; then a beatitude on reaching 1,335 — 45 days more. The arithmetic teases: three and a half years of 360-day months is 1,260; 1,290 adds a month; 1,335 another 45 — and no event we can name lands on either (the temple's rededication — Hanukkah, 25 Kislev 164 — comes at about three years, short of both). Some readers hear successive recalculations as a first figure passed — the book's own honesty about waiting; traditional readers assign the numbers to the end of days; this page reports that after twenty-two centuries the count is still open. The text's practical counsel is the beatitude itself: "Happy the one who waits" — ashrei, the word the book of Psalms opens with (KJV "Blessed is he that waiteth").

A pension and a resurrection. The book's last sentence is addressed to one tired old man: "And you — go on to the end. And you shall rest" — nuach, the verb hiding inside Noah's name — "and you shall stand up to your lot at the end of the days" (Geneva "stand up in thy lot"; Douay "stand in thy lot unto the end of the days"). Goral is the allotted portion — the pebble-drawn share of land with your name on it — and amad sounds one last time: Michael stands (v1), the sleepers of verse 2 wake, and Daniel, dead now these many centuries, is promised he will stand in his allotment — the resurrection of verse 2 made personal, to a named man. The final word of the book is "the days" spelled the Aramaic way (ha-yamin) — a book that switched languages midstream, signing off with an accent. After it the text carries the closing mark Mechon-Mamre's edition prints at the end of a book: {ש} — the same sign that stands at the end of Genesis 50.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "everlasting life" with the whole shelf (the phrase is exact, and unique in the Hebrew Bible); "shut up the words and seal the book" with the KJV family's doubled verbs; "Happy" for ashrei, with the Psalms tradition.

Where it goes its own way: "the ground of dust" for admat-afar (KJV "the dust of the earth") — keeping both Eden words audible; "shine like the shining of the vault" — the cognate kept, and raqia rendered as Genesis 1 rendered it (KJV "the brightness of the firmament"); "rove about" for the searching-verb (KJV "run to and fro"); "an appointed time, appointed times, and a half" — mo'ed kept as Genesis 1:14's word; and "escape" for yimmalet (KJV "delivered"), to keep 11:41's echo audible.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: the escape-verb (11:41 → 12:1); the furnace triple (11:35 → 12:10); the maskilim's reward (11:33 → 12:3); the vault of day two (Genesis 1:6); the appointed times of day four (1:14); the dust of Eden (2:7, 3:19) given back its sleepers; Abram's stars (15:5) and Abram's raised hand (14:22), doubled by the man in linen; and the sealed book answered across the canon by Revelation's unsealed one (Revelation 1:3). Planted: Michael's war in heaven (Revelation 12:7, when this site gets there); the abomination-phrase still waiting on the Mount of Olives (Matthew 24:15); and "everlasting life," waiting for John 3:16.

Next installment in this book: Daniel 2 — Nebuchadnezzar's statue-dream, the four kingdoms, and the moment the book itself switches into Aramaic mid-verse.

nor do they light a lamp and put it under the measuring-basket, but on the lampstand — and it shines for all who are in the house.note

Μὴ νομίσητε ὅτι ἦλθον καταλῦσαι τὸν νόμον ἢ τοὺς προφήτας· οὐκ ἦλθον καταλῦσαι ἀλλὰ πληρῶσαι·

21
Ἠκούσατε ὅτι ἐρρέθη τοῖς ἀρχαίοις· Οὐ φονεύσεις· ὃς δʼ ἂν φονεύσῃ, ἔνοχος ἔσται τῇ κρίσει.
You have heard that it was said to the ancients: You shall not murder, and whoever murders shall be liable to the judgment.note
22
ἐγὼ δὲ λέγω ὑμῖν ὅτι πᾶς ὁ ὀργιζόμενος τῷ ἀδελφῷ αὐτοῦ ἔνοχος ἔσται τῇ κρίσει· ὃς δʼ ἂν εἴπῃ τῷ ἀδελφῷ αὐτοῦ· Ῥακά, ἔνοχος ἔσται τῷ συνεδρίῳ· ὃς δʼ ἂν εἴπῃ· Μωρέ, ἔνοχος ἔσται εἰς τὴν γέενναν τοῦ πυρός.

leave your gift there, before the altar, and go — first be reconciled to your brother, and then come and offer your gift.note
