Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–2 · ἀνέβη εἰς τὸ ὄρος anebē eis to oros

You are entering Matthew mid-book — and Matthew itself. The first Gospel arrives on this site at chapter 5: behind us (not yet on these pages) lie the genealogy, Bethlehem, the magi, Egypt, the baptism, and the wilderness temptation; immediately behind, 4:23-25's great crowds from Galilee, the Decapolis, Jerusalem, and beyond the Jordan. Seeing them, "he went up the mountain" and — the ancient teacher's posture — sat down (KJV "when he was set," an archaism worth a smile). Matthew's architecture is famous: the teaching gathered into five great discourses (chapters 5–7, 10, 13, 18, 24–25), and many readers since the church fathers have heard a deliberate echo — a new teaching delivered from a mountain, five blocks of instruction answering five books of Moses. Reported as a reading, not voted on; the chapter works either way. "And opening his mouth, he taught them" — the Semitic idiom for beginning solemn, deliberate speech; the scroll clears its throat.

🔴 A note on the red ink. Beginning with this chapter, this site prints the recorded words of Jesus in red — the convention promised on the library's roadmap "so it would be ready the day Matthew begins." John 1–2 and the risen Christ's words in Revelation 1–2 have been set in red to match. From verse 3 to the chapter's last word, everything on this page is one unbroken speech — and the Sermon runs on for two chapters more.

Verses 3–12 · Μακάριοι makarioi

"Happy" — and why not "Blessed." Makarios is the Greek Bible's rendering of the Hebrew ashrei — the word Psalms opens with, the word this site met one page ago in Daniel 12:12, "Happy the one who waits." It declares a fortunate condition, an enviable state — not a blessing pronounced (Greek has a separate praise-word, eulogētos, for that). So this translation reads Happy, with the NWT ("Happy are…") and against the grand tradition — KJV, Geneva, and Douay all "Blessed"; TLB paraphrases the state itself ("Humble men are very fortunate…"). And no "are": the Greek writes none — Μακάριοι οἱ πτωχοί, "Happy the poor," is a verbless exclamation in the ashrei mold, a pronouncement rather than a sentence, and every version on the shelf quietly supplies the verb. This translation waits for the Greek to write it: at v 11 it finally does (μακάριοί ἐστε, "Happy are you"), and the English follows in the same breath. Eight beatitudes (vv 3–10), framed by the same present-tense promise — "theirs is the kingdom" opens and closes the set, while everything between is future tense: the kingdom is the now that holds the not-yet. Then the camera turns: "Happy are you…" (vv 11–12).

The furniture of the promises. "The kingdom of the heavens" — the plural is real (tōn ouranōn), and it is Matthew's signature: alone among the evangelists he says "kingdom of the heavens" (a reverent Jewish sidestep of the divine name; Mark and Luke say "kingdom of God"). KJV flattens to "of heaven"; the NWT keeps the plural with us. "The meek shall inherit the earth" is Psalm 37:11 nearly verbatim — and tēn gēn is also simply "the land": the promise-currency of Genesis ("to give you this land, to inherit it," 15:7), widened to the world. The poor "in spirit" — ptōchoi means beggars, the destitute, not the modestly poor; Luke's parallel (6:20) has flatly "Happy the poor," and the two Gospels' forms are left to light each other rather than be harmonized. The set ends where the prophets ended: persecuted, lied about, rejoicing — "for so they persecuted the prophets before you."

Verses 13–16 · ἐὰν τὸ ἅλας μωρανθῇ ean to halas mōranthē

Salt that goes foolish. The Greek of "loses its taste" is a small joke with teeth: mōranthē — literally "becomes foolish," the very root (mōros) of the insult "Fool!" that verse 22 will make actionable. Behind both may stand one Aramaic word (tafel, insipid/foolish): salt gone stupid. Can salt actually lose its savor? Dead Sea salt could — impure, mixed with gypsum, the sodium chloride leaching out until what remained was literally good for nothing but road grit, "trampled underfoot." The city on a mountain that cannot be hidden; the lamp under the modios (a grain-measuring basket, about nine liters — KJV "under a bushel," which gave English an idiom) — and on the lampstand instead: lychnia, the word Revelation's opening vision reaches for when it makes the congregations themselves the lampstands. "Let your light shine before men" — the chapter's only command aimed at being seen, and it routes the credit elsewhere: "that they may glorify your Father" — the Sermon's great word for God, arriving here first.

Verses 17–20 · ἰῶτα ἓν ἢ μία κεραία iōta hen ē mia keraia

Not one iota, not one little hook. "Do not suppose that I came to tear down the Law or the Prophets" — katalysai, a demolition word, used of buildings — "but to fulfill." Then the most famous small-print clause in scripture: a Greek sentence pointing at Hebrew letters. The iōta stands for yod (י), the smallest letter of the Hebrew alphabet; the keraia ("little horn") is the serif — the tiny hook of ink that distinguishes one letter from its near-twin. KJV "one jot or one tittle" (jot IS iota, worn down by English mouths); NIV "the smallest letter, not the least stroke of a pen." "Until the heaven and the earth pass away" — Genesis 1:1's merism for everything, given an expiry clause. And verse 20 plants the chapter's quiet thread-word: unless your righteousness overflows (perisseusē) beyond the scribes and Pharisees — watch that word; it returns at verses 37 and 47, and the six cases that follow (anger, lust, divorce, oaths, retaliation, enemies) are all portraits of what overflow looks like.

Verses 21–26 · Ῥακά … γέεννα Raca … geenna

The first "You have heard… but I say." Six times this chapter quotes the tradition and then goes under it, to the root. The quote is the sixth commandment; the root is anger — and this site's own shelf holds the proof text: the first murder began as anger in a face ("Why are you angry, and why has your face fallen?", Genesis 4:6). The escalation is a courtroom ladder: anger → "the judgment" (the local court); 'Raca' (Aramaic rēqa, "empty-head" — Matthew transliterated it and nearly every version since has left it standing: KJV "Raca") → "the council" (synedrion — the Sanhedrin word); 'Fool!' (mōre — the salt-word of v13) → "the Gehenna of fire."

Gehenna, kept as the name it is. Geenna is the Greek shape of gei-hinnom, the Valley of Hinnom below Jerusalem's walls — the child-sacrifice valley of the kings (Josiah defiled its burning-place, 2 Kings 23:10), which by Jesus' day had become the standing name for the fiery end of the wicked; its furniture — unquenched fire, the undying worm — comes from Isaiah 66:24, the same closing verse this site met beside Daniel 12:2's "abhorrence" one page ago. KJV translates it away as "hell fire"; the NWT keeps "Gehenna" — as does this translation: a real valley you can stand in, doing figurative work, and the reader deserves to see the geography. Also here, this page's first New Testament textual variant: the later manuscript mass reads "angry with his brother without a cause" (KJV, from the Byzantine text) — but the two earliest great codices (Vaticanus, and Sinaiticus's first hand) lack the word eikē. A one-word escape hatch, added early; the critical text this translation follows does not offer it. The section closes in small change: settle on the road, or pay "the last quadrans" — Rome's smallest coin (KJV "the uttermost farthing"; Douay "the last farthing"; NIV "the last penny").

Verses 27–32 · πρὸς τὸ ἐπιθυμῆσαι pros to epithymēsai

The look with a purpose. The Greek of verse 28 is precise in a way sermons often are not: pros to epithymēsai — looking in order to desire, the cultivated look, the look as project — not the glance that happens to a person. The grammar aims at what the will does with the eyes, and the translation keeps it: "looks at a woman in order to desire her." Then the famous surgery: eye and hand, right eye and right hand (the favored ones), with skandalizei — "trips you up," from skandalon, the trigger-stick of a trap. Hyperbole with a straight face: no reader in the first century reached for a knife, and none was meant to; the point is that amputation is a bargain next to Gehenna.

The divorce sentence. "It was said: whoever divorces his wife, let him give her a certificate" — Deuteronomy 24:1's get, the paper that protected the woman's right to remarry. The debate alive in Jesus' day ran between the school of Hillel ("for any cause," even a spoiled dinner) and the school of Shammai ("only for indecency") — and Matthew's exception, "apart from a matter of sexual immorality" (logou porneias), even echoes the shape of Deuteronomy's disputed phrase (ervat davar, "indecency of a matter"). Honest reporting: Mark 10:11 and Luke 16:18 carry this saying with no exception clause; Matthew alone has it, twice (here and 19:9). The versions differ only in how bluntly they name the exception (KJV "saving for the cause of fornication"; NIV "except for sexual immorality").

Verses 33–37 · ναὶ ναί, οὒ οὔ nai nai, ou ou

Closing the oath loopholes. The tradition said: don't swear falsely, and pay what you vow to the Lord. Around that rule had grown a casuistry of substitutes — swear "by heaven," "by the earth," "toward Jerusalem," "by my head," and the divine name stays technically unspent, the oath technically deniable. Jesus closes every exit with scripture's own furniture: heaven is "God's throne," the earth "the footstool of his feet" (Isaiah 66:1 — the third visit to Isaiah 66 in this site's last two chapters), and Jerusalem "the city of the great King" (Psalm 48:2) — note the preposition, swearing toward (eis) Jerusalem, the direction a vow faced. Even your head won't serve: you cannot make one hair white or black. So: "let your word be 'Yes, yes — no, no'" — and here the thread-word of verse 20 returns: what overflows beyond these (to perisson toutōn) is ek tou ponērou — "from the evil one," or "from evil": the Greek genitive is genuinely ambiguous, and the same ambiguity waits in the Lord's Prayer one chapter ahead (KJV "cometh of evil"; NIV "from the evil one"). James 5:12 quotes this saying nearly verbatim — the Sermon's earliest audible echo in another book.

Verses 38–42 · ὅστις σε ἀγγαρεύσει hostis se angareusei

An eye for an eye — what it was for. The quoted law (Exodus 21:24) was itself a limit: one eye for an eye, not a feud for an eye — a ceiling on vengeance, administered by courts, not a license for it. Jesus lowers the ceiling to the floor: "do not stand against the one who is evil" (antistēnai — a battle-line word). The three cases that follow are drawn with a reporter's eye for detail. The slap lands on the right cheek — from a right-handed striker, that is the back of the hand: an insult, a put-down, not a mugging; turning the other cheek offers the striker only an equal's blow. The lawsuit takes the tunic (chitōn, the shirt); handing over the cloak as well (himation) hands over the one garment Exodus 22:26-27 says even a creditor must return by sundown — the defendant walks out of court wearing the verdict. And "whoever presses you into service for one mile" — angareuō, a Persian word from the royal courier-post (Herodotus' angaroi), by Roman times the soldier's legal right to commandeer a civilian's back or beast for one mile — milion, a Latin loanword sitting in the Greek like a boot-print (KJV "whosoever shall compel thee to go a mile"). Matthew will use this rare verb once more: on the Cyrenian pressed into carrying a cross (27:32).

Verses 43–48 · ἀγαπᾶτε τοὺς ἐχθροὺς ὑμῶν agapate tous echthrous hymōn

The quote with an addition. "You shall love your neighbor" is Leviticus 19:18. "And hate your enemy" is — nowhere. It is the only one of the six quotations that adds words the Law never says; Jesus is quoting the street version. And the street version had real addresses: the Qumran community's own rule commanded members to "love all the sons of light… and hate all the sons of darkness" (1QS 1:9-10) — a documented contemporary reading, canceled here at its root: "love your enemies, and pray for those who persecute you." A second textual variant sits in verse 44: the KJV's beloved long form — "bless them that curse you, do good to them that hate you… despitefully use you" — entered Matthew from Luke 6:27-28 by scribal harmonizing; the earliest witnesses carry the short, harder sentence, and the critical text this translation follows keeps it short.

The Father's weather, and the last word. The argument for enemy-love is meteorological: "he makes his sun rise on evil and good, and sends rain on just and unjust" — the sun that Genesis 1 hung "to shine on the earth," no address list attached. Even "the tax collectors" love their own (telōnai — remember the word: in chapter 9 this Gospel's own narrator will be found sitting in a tax booth); even "the Gentiles" greet their brothers — "what more are you doing?": the overflow-word of verses 20 and 37, third and last time. And the close: "You, then, shall be whole, as your heavenly Father is whole." Teleios — traditionally "perfect" (KJV "Be ye therefore perfect") — means complete, undivided, whole-grown: the word the Greek Bible uses where the Hebrew says tamim, the charge El Shaddai laid on Abram — "walk before me, and be blameless" (Genesis 17:1). Luke's parallel reads "be merciful, as your Father is merciful" (6:36) — the two versions light each other: the wholeness in question is love without a fence around it. And the grammar of the last sentence is the Decalogue's own: Ἔσεσθε, future indicative — "You shall be" — the Sermon closes its first chapter speaking the way the Law speaks.

Patterns worth carrying forward

🔴 The red letters begin. A promise on the library's roadmap since before the Gospels arrived — "the words of Jesus will be set in red… so the convention is ready the day Matthew begins" — kept today: this chapter debuts it, and John 1–2 and Revelation 1–2 were set in red to match.

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "Raca" left standing, with everyone since Matthew transliterated it; the six quotation-formulas rendered flat ("You have heard that it was said"); "an eye for an eye" exactly as the shelf has always had it.

Where it goes its own way: "Happy" for makarioi (with NWT, against KJV/Geneva/Douay's "Blessed") — the same call this site made for ashrei in Daniel 12:12, one page ago; "the kingdom of the heavens" — Matthew's literal plural, kept; "not one iota, not one little hook" (KJV "jot… tittle"); "Gehenna" kept as the place-name it is (KJV "hell fire"); "the last quadrans" (KJV "farthing"); "presses you into service" for the Persian courier-word angareuō; and "whole" for teleios (KJV "perfect") — completeness, not flawlessness.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: ashrei → makarios, "Happy" (Daniel 12:12); the meek inheriting in the promise-grammar of (15:7); anger as the root of the first murder (4:6); the sun hung to shine on the earth (1:17); "walk before me, and be blameless" — tamim, now teleios (17:1); the light of the world (John 1:9) and the lamp on Revelation's own lampstands (Revelation 1:20); and Gehenna furnished from Isaiah 66:24, the verse Daniel 12:2 shares. Planted: the overflow-word (vv 20, 37, 47 — the Sermon's measure, carrying into chapters 6–7); "from the evil one," waiting for the Lord's Prayer; angareuō, waiting for Simon of Cyrene (27:32); and the tax collectors, waiting for a man named Matthew at his booth (9:9).

Next installment in this book: Matthew 6 — the Lord's Prayer, treasure in the heavens, and the birds and the lilies.

Matthew 6 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
Προσέχετε δὲ τὴν δικαιοσύνην ὑμῶν μὴ ποιεῖν ἔμπροσθεν τῶν ἀνθρώπων πρὸς τὸ θεαθῆναι αὐτοῖς· εἰ δὲ μή γε, μισθὸν οὐκ ἔχετε παρὰ τῷ πατρὶ ὑμῶν τῷ ἐν τοῖς οὐρανοῖς.
"Take care not to do your righteousness before men, to be gazed at by them — otherwise you have no reward with your Father who is in the heavens.note
2
Ὅταν οὖν ποιῇς ἐλεημοσύνην, μὴ σαλπίσῃς ἔμπροσθέν σου, ὥσπερ οἱ ὑποκριταὶ ποιοῦσιν ἐν ταῖς συναγωγαῖς καὶ ἐν ταῖς ῥύμαις, ὅπως δοξασθῶσιν ὑπὸ τῶν ἀνθρώπων· ἀμὴν λέγω ὑμῖν, ἀπέχουσιν τὸν μισθὸν αὐτῶν.
So when you give alms, do not sound a trumpet before you, as the hypocrites do in the synagogues and in the alleys, so that they may be glorified by men. Amen I tell you: they have their reward in full.note
3
σοῦ δὲ ποιοῦντος ἐλεημοσύνην μὴ γνώτω ἡ ἀριστερά σου τί ποιεῖ ἡ δεξιά σου,
But you, when you give alms — do not let your left hand know what your right hand is doing,note
4
ὅπως ᾖ σου ἡ ἐλεημοσύνη ἐν τῷ κρυπτῷ· καὶ ὁ πατήρ σου ὁ βλέπων ἐν τῷ κρυπτῷ ἀποδώσει σοι.
so that your alms may be in secret — and your Father, who sees in secret, will repay you.note
5
Καὶ ὅταν προσεύχησθε, οὐκ ἔσεσθε ὡς οἱ ὑποκριταί· ὅτι φιλοῦσιν ἐν ταῖς συναγωγαῖς καὶ ἐν ταῖς γωνίαις τῶν πλατειῶν ἑστῶτες προσεύχεσθαι, ὅπως φανῶσιν τοῖς ἀνθρώποις· ἀμὴν λέγω ὑμῖν, ἀπέχουσι τὸν μισθὸν αὐτῶν.
And when you pray, you shall not be like the hypocrites — for they love to pray standing in the synagogues and on the corners of the main streets, so that they may appear to men. Amen I tell you: they have their reward in full.note
6
σὺ δὲ ὅταν προσεύχῃ, εἴσελθε εἰς τὸ ταμεῖόν σου καὶ κλείσας τὴν θύραν σου πρόσευξαι τῷ πατρί σου τῷ ἐν τῷ κρυπτῷ· καὶ ὁ πατήρ σου ὁ βλέπων ἐν τῷ κρυπτῷ ἀποδώσει σοι.
But you, when you pray, go into your inner room, and shut your door, and pray to your Father who is in secret — and your Father, who sees in secret, will repay you.note
7
Προσευχόμενοι δὲ μὴ βατταλογήσητε ὥσπερ οἱ ἐθνικοί, δοκοῦσιν γὰρ ὅτι ἐν τῇ πολυλογίᾳ αὐτῶν εἰσακουσθήσονται·
And in praying, do not babble on like the Gentiles — for they suppose that by their many words they will be heard.note
8
μὴ οὖν ὁμοιωθῆτε αὐτοῖς, οἶδεν γὰρ ὁ πατὴρ ὑμῶν ὧν χρείαν ἔχετε πρὸ τοῦ ὑμᾶς αἰτῆσαι αὐτόν.
So do not be like them — for your Father knows what you need before you ask him.note
9
Οὕτως οὖν προσεύχεσθε ὑμεῖς· Πάτερ ἡμῶν ὁ ἐν τοῖς οὐρανοῖς· ἁγιασθήτω τὸ ὄνομά σου,
Pray, then, like this: Our Father, who is in the heavens, let your name be sanctified;note
10
ἐλθέτω ἡ βασιλεία σου, γενηθήτω τὸ θέλημά σου, ὡς ἐν οὐρανῷ καὶ ἐπὶ γῆς·
let your kingdom come; let your will come to be — as in heaven, so also on earth.note
11
τὸν ἄρτον ἡμῶν τὸν ἐπιούσιον δὸς ἡμῖν σήμερον·
Give us today our bread for the day ahead;note
12
καὶ ἄφες ἡμῖν τὰ ὀφειλήματα ἡμῶν, ὡς καὶ ἡμεῖς ἀφήκαμεν τοῖς ὀφειλέταις ἡμῶν·
and forgive us our debts, as we also have forgiven our debtors;note
13
καὶ μὴ εἰσενέγκῃς ἡμᾶς εἰς πειρασμόν, ἀλλὰ ῥῦσαι ἡμᾶς ἀπὸ τοῦ πονηροῦ.
and do not bring us into testing, but rescue us from the evil one.note
14
ἐὰν γὰρ ἀφῆτε τοῖς ἀνθρώποις τὰ παραπτώματα αὐτῶν, ἀφήσει καὶ ὑμῖν ὁ πατὴρ ὑμῶν ὁ οὐράνιος·
For if you forgive men their missteps, your heavenly Father will also forgive you;note
15
ἐὰν δὲ μὴ ἀφῆτε τοῖς ἀνθρώποις, οὐδὲ ὁ πατὴρ ὑμῶν ἀφήσει τὰ παραπτώματα ὑμῶν.
but if you do not forgive men, neither will your Father forgive your missteps.note
16
Ὅταν δὲ νηστεύητε, μὴ γίνεσθε ὡς οἱ ὑποκριταὶ σκυθρωποί, ἀφανίζουσιν γὰρ τὰ πρόσωπα αὐτῶν ὅπως φανῶσιν τοῖς ἀνθρώποις νηστεύοντες· ἀμὴν λέγω ὑμῖν, ἀπέχουσιν τὸν μισθὸν αὐτῶν.
And when you fast, do not become gloomy-faced like the hypocrites — for they make their faces disappear, so that they may appear to men as fasting. Amen I tell you: they have their reward in full.note
17
σὺ δὲ νηστεύων ἄλειψαί σου τὴν κεφαλὴν καὶ τὸ πρόσωπόν σου νίψαι,
But you, when you fast, oil your head and wash your face,note
18
ὅπως μὴ φανῇς τοῖς ἀνθρώποις νηστεύων ἀλλὰ τῷ πατρί σου τῷ ἐν τῷ κρυφαίῳ· καὶ ὁ πατήρ σου ὁ βλέπων ἐν τῷ κρυφαίῳ ἀποδώσει σοι.
so that you may not appear to men as fasting, but to your Father who is in hiding — and your Father, who sees in hiding, will repay you.note
19
Μὴ θησαυρίζετε ὑμῖν θησαυροὺς ἐπὶ τῆς γῆς, ὅπου σὴς καὶ βρῶσις ἀφανίζει, καὶ ὅπου κλέπται διορύσσουσιν καὶ κλέπτουσιν·
Do not store up for yourselves treasures on the earth, where moth and devouring make them disappear, and where thieves dig through and steal.note
20
θησαυρίζετε δὲ ὑμῖν θησαυροὺς ἐν οὐρανῷ, ὅπου οὔτε σὴς οὔτε βρῶσις ἀφανίζει, καὶ ὅπου κλέπται οὐ διορύσσουσιν οὐδὲ κλέπτουσιν·
But store up for yourselves treasures in heaven, where neither moth nor devouring makes them disappear, and where thieves do not dig through, nor steal.note
21
ὅπου γάρ ἐστιν ὁ θησαυρός σου, ἐκεῖ ἔσται καὶ ἡ καρδία σου.
For where your treasure is, there your heart will be also.note
22
Ὁ λύχνος τοῦ σώματός ἐστιν ὁ ὀφθαλμός. ἐὰν οὖν ᾖ ὁ ὀφθαλμός σου ἁπλοῦς, ὅλον τὸ σῶμά σου φωτεινὸν ἔσται·
The lamp of the body is the eye. So if your eye is undivided, your whole body will be full of light;note
23
ἐὰν δὲ ὁ ὀφθαλμός σου πονηρὸς ᾖ, ὅλον τὸ σῶμά σου σκοτεινὸν ἔσται. εἰ οὖν τὸ φῶς τὸ ἐν σοὶ σκότος ἐστίν, τὸ σκότος πόσον.
but if your eye is evil, your whole body will be dark. If, then, the light in you is darkness — how great the darkness!note
24
Οὐδεὶς δύναται δυσὶ κυρίοις δουλεύειν· ἢ γὰρ τὸν ἕνα μισήσει καὶ τὸν ἕτερον ἀγαπήσει, ἢ ἑνὸς ἀνθέξεται καὶ τοῦ ἑτέρου καταφρονήσει. οὐ δύνασθε θεῷ δουλεύειν καὶ μαμωνᾷ.
No one can be a slave to two masters: for either he will hate the one and love the other, or he will hold to the one and despise the other. You cannot be slaves to God and to Mammon.note
25
Διὰ τοῦτο λέγω ὑμῖν· μὴ μεριμνᾶτε τῇ ψυχῇ ὑμῶν τί φάγητε, μηδὲ τῷ σώματι ὑμῶν τί ἐνδύσησθε· οὐχὶ ἡ ψυχὴ πλεῖόν ἐστι τῆς τροφῆς καὶ τὸ σῶμα τοῦ ἐνδύματος;
Because of this I tell you: do not be anxious for your life, what you will eat — nor for your body, what you will put on. Is not life more than food, and the body more than clothing?note
26
ἐμβλέψατε εἰς τὰ πετεινὰ τοῦ οὐρανοῦ, ὅτι οὐ σπείρουσιν οὐδὲ θερίζουσιν οὐδὲ συνάγουσιν εἰς ἀποθήκας, καὶ ὁ πατὴρ ὑμῶν ὁ οὐράνιος τρέφει αὐτά· οὐχ ὑμεῖς μᾶλλον διαφέρετε αὐτῶν;
Look closely at the birds of the heaven: they do not sow, nor reap, nor gather into barns — and your heavenly Father feeds them. Are you not worth more than they?note
27
τίς δὲ ἐξ ὑμῶν μεριμνῶν δύναται προσθεῖναι ἐπὶ τὴν ἡλικίαν αὐτοῦ πῆχυν ἕνα;
And who among you, by being anxious, can add one cubit to his span of life?note
28
καὶ περὶ ἐνδύματος τί μεριμνᾶτε; καταμάθετε τὰ κρίνα τοῦ ἀγροῦ πῶς αὐξάνουσιν· οὐ κοπιῶσιν οὐδὲ νήθουσιν·
And why are you anxious about clothing? Study the lilies of the field, how they grow: they do not toil, nor spin —note
29
λέγω δὲ ὑμῖν ὅτι οὐδὲ Σολομὼν ἐν πάσῃ τῇ δόξῃ αὐτοῦ περιεβάλετο ὡς ἓν τούτων.
but I tell you that not even Solomon, in all his glory, was clothed like one of these.note
30
εἰ δὲ τὸν χόρτον τοῦ ἀγροῦ σήμερον ὄντα καὶ αὔριον εἰς κλίβανον βαλλόμενον ὁ θεὸς οὕτως ἀμφιέννυσιν, οὐ πολλῷ μᾶλλον ὑμᾶς, ὀλιγόπιστοι;
Now if God so clothes the grass of the field — here today, and tomorrow thrown into the oven — will he not much more clothe you, you little-faiths?note
31
μὴ οὖν μεριμνήσητε λέγοντες· Τί φάγωμεν; ἤ· Τί πίωμεν; ἤ· Τί περιβαλώμεθα;
So do not be anxious, saying: What shall we eat? or: What shall we drink? or: What shall we wear?note
32
πάντα γὰρ ταῦτα τὰ ἔθνη ἐπιζητοῦσιν· οἶδεν γὰρ ὁ πατὴρ ὑμῶν ὁ οὐράνιος ὅτι χρῄζετε τούτων ἁπάντων.
For all these things the nations chase after — and your heavenly Father knows that you need them all.note
33
ζητεῖτε δὲ πρῶτον τὴν βασιλείαν καὶ τὴν δικαιοσύνην αὐτοῦ, καὶ ταῦτα πάντα προστεθήσεται ὑμῖν.
But seek first the kingdom, and his righteousness — and all these things will be added to you.note
34
μὴ οὖν μεριμνήσητε εἰς τὴν αὔριον, ἡ γὰρ αὔριον μεριμνήσει αὑτῆς· ἀρκετὸν τῇ ἡμέρᾳ ἡ κακία αὐτῆς.
So do not be anxious for tomorrow, for tomorrow will be anxious for itself. Sufficient for the day is its own trouble.note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–4 · πρὸς τὸ θεαθῆναι pros to theathēnai

Piety as theater. The Sermon's middle chapter opens with a header for its first three sections: do not do your righteousness "to be gazed at" — and the verb is theathēnai, the root that gave Greek its theater. The audience-word is no accident, because the performers arrive one verse later: hypokritai — in ordinary Greek, stage actors, the masked professionals (Galilee knew them: the theater at Sepphoris stood an hour's walk from Nazareth). A textual note sits in verse 1: the later manuscripts read "do not your alms" (KJV), narrowing the header to the first example; the earliest read "your righteousness" — the umbrella over all three practices — and the critical text this translation follows keeps the umbrella. No trumpet before you (no evidence anyone literally trumpeted their giving — this is satire, drawn with one stroke); the left hand kept ignorant of the right; and the section's refrain: "your Father, who sees in secret, will repay you." This site's shelf already holds the name for that: El Roi, "the God who sees" — Hagar's coinage in the desert (Genesis 16:13). One more scribal gift declined: the KJV adds "shall reward thee openly" in all three refrains — the word is absent from the earliest witnesses, and its addition quietly ruins the point: the older text lets the secret stay secret.

Paid in full. "They have their reward" — apechousin, the standing formula on Greek commercial receipts: paid in full, account closed. The applause was the payment; nothing further is owed. The word recurs like a stamp at verses 2, 5, and 16 — three receipts, three closed accounts.

Verses 5–8 · μὴ βατταλογήσητε mē battalogēsēte

The inner room, and the babble-word. Against praying on the corners of the main streets ("so that they may appear to men" — watch that verb; verse 16 will play a whole pun on it), the instruction is architectural: the tameion — the storeroom, the one room in a Galilean house with a door that shut and locked. And against the pagan style: "do not babble on" — battalogēsēte, a word found almost nowhere before this verse and quite possibly built to sound like what it means (batta-batta…), glossed by the very next phrase, "their many words" (KJV "use not vain repetitions"; NIV "do not keep on babbling"; NWT "do not say the same things over and over again"). The theology underneath is the chapter's quiet engine: "your Father knows what you need before you ask" — prayer, in this Sermon, is not information transfer.

Verses 9–13 · Πάτερ ἡμῶν Pater hēmōn

The most-recited text in human history. Its architecture is a hinge: an address, then three your-petitions (your name, your kingdom, your will), then three us-petitions (bread, debts, rescue) — heaven's agenda before ours. "Our Father, who is in the heavens" — Matthew's plural, kept as everywhere in this book. "Let your name be sanctified" (hagiasthētō — set apart, treated as holy): the KJV's "hallowed be thy name" is the same word wearing four centuries of polish (the NWT reads "sanctified" with us). "Let your will come to be" — genēthētō, the LXX's own verb from creation's "let there be." Then the hardest word in the prayer: ton arton hēmōn ton epiousion. This adjective appears nowhere else in Greek literature — Origen, the third century's best philologist, said plainly that the evangelists appear to have coined it. From epienai, "the coming (day)," it most likely means bread for the day ahead — asked for today: tomorrow's ration requested one sunrise at a time, the rhythm of the manna. The shelf tells the whole uncertainty: KJV "our daily bread"; Douay, translating the Vulgate of Matthew literally, prints the strangest word on any shelf — "our supersubstantial bread." And "forgive us our debts" (opheilēmata — Aramaic choba means both debt and sin; KJV "debts" with us; Luke's version says "sins"; the liturgy's "trespasses" comes not from the prayer but from verse 14, via Tyndale). The last petition pays this site's freshest echo: "rescue us apo tou ponērou" — "from the evil one," or "from evil": the same ambiguous genitive flagged at 5:37, unresolvable by grammar alone (KJV "deliver us from evil"; NIV "the evil one").

The famous missing doxology. "For thine is the kingdom, and the power, and the glory, for ever. Amen" (KJV, and Geneva before it) — is not in the earliest manuscripts. Vaticanus, Sinaiticus, and Bezae all end the prayer at "the evil one"; the early commentators who walk through the prayer line by line (Origen, Tertullian, Cyprian) show no knowledge of it in Matthew. Its pedigree is liturgical: the Didache — a church manual within living memory of the apostles — already appends a shorter form ("for yours is the power and the glory forever") when it prescribes this prayer three times a day, and the full triplet echoes David's blessing in 1 Chronicles 29:11. Worship wrote it; scribes, hearing it every week, eventually copied what their ears expected. The critical text omits it, and so does this translation — in the company, delightfully, of Douay: the Vulgate never had it, so on this most Protestant of lines, Rome and the critical editors agree against the King James.

Verses 14–15 · τὰ παραπτώματα ta paraptōmata

Where "trespasses" came from. The prayer said debts; this two-verse coda — the only petition Jesus stops to underline — switches to paraptōmata, "missteps" (a stumble off the path, a false step). Tyndale rendered it "trespasses" here, the Book of Common Prayer carried his word up into the prayer itself, and English-speaking congregations have prayed a verse-14 word in a verse-12 slot ever since. The logic of the coda is a closed loop, stated without cushioning: forgiveness withheld below interrupts forgiveness from above — the one petition of the prayer whose terms the pray-er sets personally.

Verses 16–18 · ἀφανίζουσιν … ὅπως φανῶσιν aphanizousin … hopōs phanōsin

The Greek's own pun, kept. The gloomy fasters "make their faces disappear" — ash-smeared, unwashed, theatrically wrecked — "so that they may appear to men as fasting": aphanizousin… phanōsin, vanish to be visible, the wordplay sitting right in the Greek and kept in the English. The counter-instruction is festive camouflage: oil your head and wash your face — groom yourself like a man going to a banquet, so the fast belongs to God alone. And a small fidelity: verse 18 twice shifts to a different secret-word (en tō kryphaiō, "in hiding") than verses 4 and 6's en tō kryptō ("in secret") — the variation is the text's, so it is this translation's too.

Verses 19–24 · θεῷ … καὶ μαμωνᾷ theō … kai mamōna

Treasure that disappears — the pun's second act. The verb that vanished the fasters' faces returns for the treasure: moth and devouring (brōsis, "eating" — rust, worm, rot; KJV "rust") "make them disappear" (aphanizei — the same word as verse 16). And "thieves dig through" (dioryssousin) — through, not in: a Galilean burglar went through the mud-brick wall itself. Then the needle: "where your treasure is, there your heart will be also" — the Sermon's compass; the heart follows the deposit.

The undivided eye, and the Aramaic ledger-word. "If your eye is undivided" — haplous: single, simple, sound (KJV "single"; NWT "simple"; NIV "healthy") — against the evil eye, which in Hebrew idiom is the stingy eye (Deuteronomy 15:9 warns against an "evil eye" toward a poor brother): the whole pair is about undivided generosity as much as optics, which is exactly why the next sentence is about divided service. "No one can be a slave to two masters" (douleuein — not moonlighting; ownership). "You cannot be slaves to God and to Mammon" — mamōnas, Aramaic for wealth/property (from the same root as amen: that in which one trusts), left untranslated by Matthew and by nearly everyone since (KJV "mammon"; NIV breaks ranks: "money") — not a Canaanite deity, but capital personified by the grammar of the sentence itself: only masters have slaves.

Verses 25–34 · μὴ μεριμνᾶτε … ὀλιγόπιστοι mē merimnate … oligopistoi

The anxiety drumbeat. Six times in ten verses: merimnaō — "be anxious, be pulled apart by care" (vv 25, 27, 28, 31, 34 twice). The KJV's "take no thought" is 1611 English in which "thought" meant anxious brooding — four centuries later it reads like a ban on planning, one of the shelf's best examples of a translation aging out from under its readers (ASV already corrects to "be not anxious"; TLB "don't worry"). The arguments are two creatures and a flower: "the birds of the heaven" — the Greek Bible's exact phrase from the creation account, where the Creator hands "every green plant for food" to everything that lives (Genesis 1:30): the feeding of the unemployed sparrow was on the record from page one. A textual footnote: the earliest witnesses ask only "what you will eat" in verse 25 — the "or what you will drink" of the later mass (KJV) drifts in from verse 31, where the question belongs. The cubit of verse 27 measures a puzzle: added to one's hēlikia — height, or lifespan? A cubit of height would be grotesque; a cubit of time is a mixed metaphor most readers hear as the point — worry cannot add the smallest unit to either (NIV "a single hour").

Solomon loses a beauty contest, and the pet word. "Study the lilies of the field" — likely the scarlet anemones that still carpet Galilee each spring — "not even Solomon in all his glory" (the gold-shielded, ivory-throned zenith of 1 Kings 10) "was clothed like one of these." Grass — oven fuel by tomorrow, in the klibanos — outdressing the richest king on the shelf; "will he not much more clothe you, you little-faiths?" One Greek word, oligopistoi — Matthew's pet name for disciples (it returns in the storm, 8:26, and on the water, 14:31): not faithless, just chronically under-supplied — a diagnosis with affection in it. Verse 33 carries one last variant: the earliest text says "seek first the kingdom, and his righteousness" — later scribes supplied "of God" (KJV), giving Matthew a phrase he almost never uses (his idiom, thirty-plus times, is "of the heavens" — the addition is well-meant and un-Matthean). And the Sermon's driest proverb closes the chapter: "Sufficient for the day is its own trouble" — tomorrow is a bad creditor; do not borrow from it.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "forgive us our debts" with the KJV (the liturgy's "trespasses" belongs to verse 14); "Mammon" kept, with everyone but the NIV; and the doxology omitted with the earliest manuscripts — and with Douay, whose Vulgate never had it.

Where it goes its own way: "let your name be sanctified" (KJV "hallowed" — same word, four centuries of polish); "our bread for the day ahead" for the hapax epiousios (KJV "daily"; Douay, unforgettably, "supersubstantial"); "to be gazed at" for the theater-verb; the disappear/appear pun kept at verse 16 and paid again at verse 19; "your eye is undivided" (KJV "single"); "do not babble on" (KJV "use not vain repetitions"); and "you little-faiths" for Matthew's one-word pet name oligopistoi.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: "from the evil one" — the ambiguity flagged at 5:37, home in the prayer; the Father "who sees in secret" beside Hagar's El Roi, "the God who sees" (16:13); the birds fed by the Creator who handed out food on the record's first page (1:30); bread by sweat in Eden's sentence (3:19), asked back as a gift one day at a time; and "seek first… righteousness" answering the fourth Beatitude's hunger (5:6). Planted: "little-faiths," waiting for the storm (8:26); peirasmos, "testing," waiting for Gethsemane ("pray that you not enter into testing," 26:41); the overflow-word thread of 5:20 still running; and the Sermon's last chapter ahead — judging, the narrow gate, and a house on rock.

Next installment in this book: Matthew 7 — do not judge, the narrow gate, wolves in sheep's clothing, and the house on the rock: the Sermon's finale.

Matthew 7 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
Μὴ κρίνετε, ἵνα μὴ κριθῆτε·
"Do not judge, so that you may not be judged;note
2
ἐν ᾧ γὰρ κρίματι κρίνετε κριθήσεσθε, καὶ ἐν ᾧ μέτρῳ μετρεῖτε μετρηθήσεται ὑμῖν.
for with the judgment you judge, you will be judged — and with the measure you measure, it will be measured out to you.note
3
τί δὲ βλέπεις τὸ κάρφος τὸ ἐν τῷ ὀφθαλμῷ τοῦ ἀδελφοῦ σου, τὴν δὲ ἐν τῷ σῷ ὀφθαλμῷ δοκὸν οὐ κατανοεῖς;
And why do you look at the splinter in your brother's eye, but the beam in your own eye you do not notice?note
4
ἢ πῶς ἐρεῖς τῷ ἀδελφῷ σου· Ἄφες ἐκβάλω τὸ κάρφος ἐκ τοῦ ὀφθαλμοῦ σου, καὶ ἰδοὺ ἡ δοκὸς ἐν τῷ ὀφθαλμῷ σοῦ;
Or how will you say to your brother: Let me take the splinter out of your eye — and look, the beam is in your own eye?note
5
ὑποκριτά, ἔκβαλε πρῶτον ἐκ τοῦ ὀφθαλμοῦ σοῦ τὴν δοκόν, καὶ τότε διαβλέψεις ἐκβαλεῖν τὸ κάρφος ἐκ τοῦ ὀφθαλμοῦ τοῦ ἀδελφοῦ σου.
Hypocrite! First take the beam out of your own eye, and then you will see clearly to take the splinter out of your brother's eye.note
6
Μὴ δῶτε τὸ ἅγιον τοῖς κυσίν, μηδὲ βάλητε τοὺς μαργαρίτας ὑμῶν ἔμπροσθεν τῶν χοίρων, μήποτε καταπατήσουσιν αὐτοὺς ἐν τοῖς ποσὶν αὐτῶν καὶ στραφέντες ῥήξωσιν ὑμᾶς.
Do not give what is holy to the dogs, nor throw your pearls before the swine — lest they trample them under their feet, and turn and tear you to pieces.note
7
Αἰτεῖτε, καὶ δοθήσεται ὑμῖν· ζητεῖτε, καὶ εὑρήσετε· κρούετε, καὶ ἀνοιγήσεται ὑμῖν.
Ask, and it will be given to you; seek, and you will find; knock, and it will be opened to you.note
8
πᾶς γὰρ ὁ αἰτῶν λαμβάνει καὶ ὁ ζητῶν εὑρίσκει καὶ τῷ κρούοντι ἀνοιγήσεται.
For everyone who asks receives, and the one who seeks finds, and to the one who knocks it will be opened.note
9
ἢ τίς ἐστιν ἐξ ὑμῶν ἄνθρωπος, ὃν αἰτήσει ὁ υἱὸς αὐτοῦ ἄρτον — μὴ λίθον ἐπιδώσει αὐτῷ;
Or what man is there among you who, when his son asks him for bread — will hand him a stone?note
10
ἢ καὶ ἰχθὺν αἰτήσει — μὴ ὄφιν ἐπιδώσει αὐτῷ;
Or asks for a fish — will hand him a serpent?note
11
εἰ οὖν ὑμεῖς πονηροὶ ὄντες οἴδατε δόματα ἀγαθὰ διδόναι τοῖς τέκνοις ὑμῶν, πόσῳ μᾶλλον ὁ πατὴρ ὑμῶν ὁ ἐν τοῖς οὐρανοῖς δώσει ἀγαθὰ τοῖς αἰτοῦσιν αὐτόν.
If you, then, being evil, know how to give good gifts to your children — how much more will your Father who is in the heavens give good things to those who ask him!note
12
Πάντα οὖν ὅσα ἐὰν θέλητε ἵνα ποιῶσιν ὑμῖν οἱ ἄνθρωποι, οὕτως καὶ ὑμεῖς ποιεῖτε αὐτοῖς· οὗτος γάρ ἐστιν ὁ νόμος καὶ οἱ προφῆται.
So everything that you want men to do for you — do the same for them as well; for this is the Law and the Prophets.note
13
Εἰσέλθατε διὰ τῆς στενῆς πύλης· ὅτι πλατεῖα ἡ πύλη καὶ εὐρύχωρος ἡ ὁδὸς ἡ ἀπάγουσα εἰς τὴν ἀπώλειαν, καὶ πολλοί εἰσιν οἱ εἰσερχόμενοι διʼ αὐτῆς·
Enter through the narrow gate. For wide is the gate and roomy is the way that leads away into destruction, and many are those entering through it;note
14
ὅτι στενὴ ἡ πύλη καὶ τεθλιμμένη ἡ ὁδὸς ἡ ἀπάγουσα εἰς τὴν ζωήν, καὶ ὀλίγοι εἰσὶν οἱ εὑρίσκοντες αὐτήν.
but narrow is the gate and pressed is the way that leads away into life, and few are those finding it.note
15
Προσέχετε ἀπὸ τῶν ψευδοπροφητῶν, οἵτινες ἔρχονται πρὸς ὑμᾶς ἐν ἐνδύμασι προβάτων, ἔσωθεν δέ εἰσιν λύκοι ἅρπαγες.
Beware of the false prophets, who come to you in the clothing of sheep, but inside are ravenous wolves.note
16
ἀπὸ τῶν καρπῶν αὐτῶν ἐπιγνώσεσθε αὐτούς. μήτι συλλέγουσιν ἀπὸ ἀκανθῶν σταφυλὰς ἢ ἀπὸ τριβόλων σῦκα;
From their fruits you will recognize them. Are grapes gathered from thorns, or figs from thistles?note
17
οὕτως πᾶν δένδρον ἀγαθὸν καρποὺς καλοὺς ποιεῖ, τὸ δὲ σαπρὸν δένδρον καρποὺς πονηροὺς ποιεῖ·
So every good tree produces good fruit, but the rotten tree produces evil fruit;note
18
οὐ δύναται δένδρον ἀγαθὸν καρποὺς πονηροὺς ποιεῖν, οὐδὲ δένδρον σαπρὸν καρποὺς καλοὺς ποιεῖν.
a good tree cannot produce evil fruit, nor can a rotten tree produce good fruit.note
19
πᾶν δένδρον μὴ ποιοῦν καρπὸν καλὸν ἐκκόπτεται καὶ εἰς πῦρ βάλλεται.
Every tree not producing good fruit is cut down and thrown into fire.note
20
ἄρα γε ἀπὸ τῶν καρπῶν αὐτῶν ἐπιγνώσεσθε αὐτούς.
So then — from their fruits you will recognize them.note

Οὐ πᾶς ὁ λέγων μοι· Κύριε κύριε, εἰσελεύσεται εἰς τὴν βασιλείαν τῶν οὐρανῶν, ἀλλʼ ὁ ποιῶν τὸ θέλημα τοῦ πατρός μου τοῦ ἐν τοῖς οὐρανοῖς.
Not everyone who says to me 'Lord, Lord' will enter the kingdom of the heavens — but the one who does the will of my Father who is in the heavens.note
22
πολλοὶ ἐροῦσίν μοι ἐν ἐκείνῃ τῇ ἡμέρᾳ· Κύριε κύριε, οὐ τῷ σῷ ὀνόματι ἐπροφητεύσαμεν, καὶ τῷ σῷ ὀνόματι δαιμόνια ἐξεβάλομεν, καὶ τῷ σῷ ὀνόματι δυνάμεις πολλὰς ἐποιήσαμεν;
Many will say to me on that day: Lord, Lord — did we not prophesy in your name, and in your name cast out demons, and in your name do many works of power?note
23
καὶ τότε ὁμολογήσω αὐτοῖς ὅτι Οὐδέποτε ἔγνων ὑμᾶς· ἀποχωρεῖτε ἀπʼ ἐμοῦ οἱ ἐργαζόμενοι τὴν ἀνομίαν.
And then I will declare to them: I never knew you. Depart from me, you workers of lawlessness.note
24
Πᾶς οὖν ὅστις ἀκούει μου τοὺς λόγους τούτους καὶ ποιεῖ αὐτούς, ὁμοιωθήσεται ἀνδρὶ φρονίμῳ, ὅστις ᾠκοδόμησεν αὐτοῦ τὴν οἰκίαν ἐπὶ τὴν πέτραν.
So everyone who hears these words of mine, and does them, will be like a prudent man who built his house on the rock.note
25
καὶ κατέβη ἡ βροχὴ καὶ ἦλθον οἱ ποταμοὶ καὶ ἔπνευσαν οἱ ἄνεμοι καὶ προσέπεσαν τῇ οἰκίᾳ ἐκείνῃ, καὶ οὐκ ἔπεσεν, τεθεμελίωτο γὰρ ἐπὶ τὴν πέτραν.
And the rain came down, and the rivers came, and the winds blew, and they fell upon that house — and it did not fall, for it had been founded on the rock.note
26
καὶ πᾶς ὁ ἀκούων μου τοὺς λόγους τούτους καὶ μὴ ποιῶν αὐτοὺς ὁμοιωθήσεται ἀνδρὶ μωρῷ, ὅστις ᾠκοδόμησεν αὐτοῦ τὴν οἰκίαν ἐπὶ τὴν ἄμμον.
And everyone who hears these words of mine, and does not do them, will be like a foolish man who built his house on the sand.note
27
καὶ κατέβη ἡ βροχὴ καὶ ἦλθον οἱ ποταμοὶ καὶ ἔπνευσαν οἱ ἄνεμοι καὶ προσέκοψαν τῇ οἰκίᾳ ἐκείνῃ, καὶ ἔπεσεν, καὶ ἦν ἡ πτῶσις αὐτῆς μεγάλη.
And the rain came down, and the rivers came, and the winds blew, and they struck against that house — and it fell. And its fall was great."note
28
Καὶ ἐγένετο ὅτε ἐτέλεσεν ὁ Ἰησοῦς τοὺς λόγους τούτους, ἐξεπλήσσοντο οἱ ὄχλοι ἐπὶ τῇ διδαχῇ αὐτοῦ·
And it came to be, when Jesus finished these words, that the crowds were astounded at his teaching;note
29
ἦν γὰρ διδάσκων αὐτοὺς ὡς ἐξουσίαν ἔχων καὶ οὐχ ὡς οἱ γραμματεῖς αὐτῶν.
for he was teaching them as one having authority — and not as their scribes.note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–6 · τὸ κάρφος … ἡ δοκός to karphos … hē dokos

The most-quoted — and most half-quoted — verse in the Sermon. "Do not judge" (Geneva "Iudge not, that ye be not iudged") forbids the censor's chair: the appetite for verdicts, measured back "with the measure you measure" — the rabbis' own principle, middah keneged middah, measure for measure. But the chapter refuses to be quoted in half: verse 6 requires telling dogs from children, and verses 15–20 command fruit inspection. The Sermon bans the gavel, not the eyes. Then the workshop comedy: the splinter (karphos — a chip, a speck of straw; KJV's famous "mote") versus the beam (dokos — a roof-timber, the piece a builder lays across a house) — a joke drawn to scale by, tradition remembers, a builder's household (13:55 will call him "the carpenter's son"). "Hypocrite!" — the stage-actor word of 6:2, now aimed at the eye-surgeon playing to the gallery.

Pearls before swine. Verse 6 folds back on itself: dogs… swine… swine trample, dogs turn and tear — an A-B-B-A envelope, the sentence's own teeth arranged in a ring. "What is holy" is sacrificial language (consecrated meat, not for scavengers); the pearls are the Gospel's first — one more will come, a pearl worth everything (13:45-46). The instruction sits deliberately beside "do not judge": discernment about audiences is not the censoriousness verse 1 forbids.

Verses 7–12 · Αἰτεῖτε … ζητεῖτε … κρούετε aiteite … zēteite … krouete

Keep asking. The three imperatives are present tense — Greek's continuous aspect: keep on asking, keep on seeking, keep on knocking. For once the NWT's signature habit of spelling out verbal aspect ("Keep on asking… keep on seeking… keep on knocking") is exactly what the grammar says (KJV "Ask, and it shall be given you"). The two absurd gifts are lookalikes chosen by a Galilean eye: the round flat loaf that could pass for a stone (the tempter had already noticed the resemblance, 4:3), and the eel-bodied catfish of the lake — snake-shaped, and unclean besides — for a serpent. "If you, then, being evil" — the Sermon's bleakest line of anthropology, dropped in passing inside its warmest promise, in the rabbis' own argument-form (qal va-chomer, "how much more").

The Golden Rule — signed. "Everything that you want men to do for you — do the same for them as well." The negative form was already ancient wisdom: Hillel, one generation earlier — "what is hateful to you, do not do to your fellow; that is the whole Torah, the rest is commentary"; Tobit 4:15; Confucius, five centuries before. The Sermon states it positive — not a fence against harm but a commission to initiate — and then signs it with the Sermon's own bookend: "for this is the Law and the Prophets," the phrase that opened the body of the discourse at 5:17, now closing it. Everything between the bookends is what fulfillment looks like.

Verses 13–14 · τεθλιμμένη ἡ ὁδός tethlimmenē hē hodos

Two gates, two roads — and a squeezed one. The wide gate opens onto a roomy road (eurychōros — broad country, plenty of company); the narrow gate onto a road that is "pressed" — tethlimmenē, from thlibō, the root English meets as "tribulation": the way itself is squeezed. The KJV's "strait is the gate" has spent four centuries being misread as straight — strait as in straitjacket and the Bering Strait, narrow, not unbent (ASV keeps "straitened"; NIV "small is the gate"). The Two Ways is scripture's oldest fork — "I have set before you life and death" (Deuteronomy 30:19), Psalm 1's two paths — and it became the early church's first catechism form: the Didache (met two pages ago growing a doxology) opens with the words "There are two ways, one of life and one of death."

Verses 15–20 · ἀπὸ τῶν καρπῶν αὐτῶν apo tōn karpōn autōn

Fruit inspection, commanded. One page after "do not judge," a direct order to evaluate: the chapter polices its own balance. The false prophets arrive costumed — "in the clothing of sheep, but inside are ravenous wolves" (the shelf built this English idiom; Tyndale's coinage runs through KJV to every playground since) — and the test is agricultural, not cosmetic: "from their fruits you will recognize them," said twice, an envelope around the orchard (vv 16, 20). The counter-crop is this site's oldest pair: "thorns… thistles" — the exact two words the Greek Bible uses for the curse on the ground in Eden (Genesis 3:18): no grapes grow on the curse's own vegetation. And verse 19 is a quotation the first readers would have caught: "every tree not producing good fruit is cut down and thrown into fire" — the Baptist's sentence, word for word (3:10); the Sermon countersigns John.

Verses 21–23 · Οὐδέποτε ἔγνων ὑμᾶς oudepote egnōn hymas

The Sermon's most sobering sentence. "Not everyone who says to me 'Lord, Lord'" — the doubled address of urgent devotion — "but the one who does": the doing-verb (poieō) now takes over the chapter (vv 21, 24, 26). The rejected résumé is the startling part: prophecy, exorcism, works of power, all "in your name," and the verdict does not dispute a line of it. What it says instead is relational, and final: "I never knew you" — religious performance, even successful religious performance, without acquaintance. "Depart from me, you workers of lawlessness" (anomia — KJV "ye that work iniquity") is Psalm 6:8 with the psalmist's seat taken: Jesus quotes David's dismissal of his enemies as words he himself will say "on that day" — the calm assumption of the Judge's chair by the preacher of this sermon is the chapter's quietest, largest claim.

Verses 24–27 · ἀνδρὶ φρονίμῳ … ἀνδρὶ μωρῷ andri phronimō … andri mōrō

The two builders — and the fool-word comes home. The prudent man (phronimos — sense, foresight; the word will pick wise virgins and faithful stewards later in this book) built on the rock; the other man is a mōros — and the Sermon's own thread completes: the salt that "goes foolish" (5:13), the insult too costly to throw at a brother (5:22), and now, earned honestly at last: the man who hears and does not do. The scenery is Galilee's actual geology — in summer, a smooth sand flat IS a streambed; the wadi decides in winter, when "the rain came down, and the rivers came." The two storms are told in matching triplets (TLB enjoys itself: "the rains came in torrents"), differing by one verb — the storm fell upon the first house, struck against the second — while the hinge verb is the simplest in the story: it did not fall… it fell. "For it had been founded on the rock" — pluperfect: the decisive work was already done, invisibly, before the weather arrived. And the Sermon's last red words are a warning that lands like thunder: "and its fall was great." No altar call; a collapsing house.

Verses 28–29 · ὡς ἐξουσίαν ἔχων hōs exousian echōn

The narrator returns — the first black ink in three chapters. "And it came to be, when Jesus finished these words" — this is a formula, and Matthew will lay it down five times (7:28; 11:1; 13:53; 19:1; 26:1), once at the end of each great discourse: the visible joints of the five-block architecture proposed back at 5:1, promissory note paid. The crowds were "astounded" (exeplēssonto — struck out of themselves; KJV "astonished"), and the reason is professional: "he was teaching them as one having authority (exousia), and not as their scribes." The scribal method was citation — rulings handed down in the name of earlier teachers; this preacher's only footnote, six times on this hillside, was "but I say to you." The word exousia is planted where the book can reach it: its very last sentence will claim "all authority, in heaven and on earth" (28:18). One small thing more: in all of chapter 7's sermon-text there is not a single proper name — the first name on the page belongs to the narrator, and it is Jesus, named here for the first time on this site's Matthew.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "Ask, and it will be given to you" with everyone; "wolves in sheep's clothing" and "pearls before swine" — English idioms the shelf itself created, kept; "Lord, Lord" doubled as the Greek doubles it.

Where it goes its own way: "splinter" and "beam" (KJV "mote and beam" — the workshop scale kept); "pressed is the way" for tethlimmenē (KJV "narrow"; ASV "straitened") — the tribulation-root left audible; "roomy" for the broad road; "workers of lawlessness" (KJV "ye that work iniquity"); "prudent" for phronimos (KJV "wise"); "astounded" for the crowds (KJV "astonished").

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: "the Law and the Prophets" — the Sermon's bookend, opened at 5:17 and closed at 7:12; the mōros thread — forbidden as an insult (5:22), embodied by the salt (5:13), earned at 7:26 by the man who hears and does not do; the prayer's bread-petition (6:11) argued from fatherhood — no father hands his child a stone; "thorns and thistles" — Eden's curse-crop (3:18), where no grapes grow; rain and rising water as the test of what stands — the flood's grammar (7:17), scaled to a single house; and the wolves met again two generations on — Ephesus, praised for testing self-styled apostles and finding them false (Revelation 2:2). Planted: the pearl worth everything (13:45); exousia, waiting for "all authority" (28:18); the four remaining discourse-seams (11:1, 13:53, 19:1, 26:1); and the little-faiths' first storm, one chapter ahead (8:26).

Next installment in this book: Matthew 8 — down the mountain: the leper, the centurion of Capernaum ("only say a word"), and the little-faiths' first storm on the lake.

Jeremiah 20 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
וַיִּשְׁמַע פַּשְׁחוּר בֶּן-אִמֵּר הַכֹּהֵן, וְהוּא-פָקִיד נָגִיד בְּבֵית יְהוָה, אֶת-יִרְמְיָהוּ, נִבָּא אֶת-הַדְּבָרִים הָאֵלֶּה.
Now Pashhur son of Immer, the priest — and he was chief overseer in the house of Jehovah — heard Jeremiah prophesying these things.note
2
וַיַּכֶּה פַשְׁחוּר, אֵת יִרְמְיָהוּ הַנָּבִיא; וַיִּתֵּן אֹתוֹ עַל-הַמַּהְפֶּכֶת, אֲשֶׁר בְּשַׁעַר בִּנְיָמִן הָעֶלְיוֹן, אֲשֶׁר, בְּבֵית יְהוָה.
And Pashhur struck Jeremiah the prophet, and put him in the stocks at the upper Benjamin Gate, which was by the house of Jehovah.note
3
וַיְהִי, מִמָּחֳרָת, וַיֹּצֵא פַשְׁחוּר אֶת-יִרְמְיָהוּ, מִן-הַמַּהְפָּכֶת; וַיֹּאמֶר אֵלָיו יִרְמְיָהוּ, לֹא פַשְׁחוּר קָרָא יְהוָה שְׁמֶךָ--כִּי, אִם-מָגוֹר מִסָּבִיב. {ס}
And so it was on the next day: Pashhur brought Jeremiah out of the stocks. And Jeremiah said to him: "Not Pashhur has Jehovah called your name — but Magor-Missaviv.note
4
כִּי כֹה אָמַר יְהוָה הִנְנִי נֹתֶנְךָ לְמָגוֹר לְךָ וּלְכָל-אֹהֲבֶיךָ, וְנָפְלוּ בְּחֶרֶב אֹיְבֵיהֶם--וְעֵינֶיךָ רֹאוֹת; וְאֶת-כָּל-יְהוּדָה, אֶתֵּן בְּיַד מֶלֶךְ-בָּבֶל, וְהִגְלָם בָּבֶלָה, וְהִכָּם בֶּחָרֶב.
For thus says Jehovah: Look — I am making you a terror to yourself and to all who love you; and they shall fall by the sword of their enemies, with your own eyes watching. And all Judah I will give into the hand of the king of Babylon, and he will exile them to Babylon, and strike them with the sword.note
5
וְנָתַתִּי, אֶת-כָּל-חֹסֶן הָעִיר הַזֹּאת, וְאֶת-כָּל-יְגִיעָהּ, וְאֶת-כָּל-יְקָרָהּ; וְאֵת כָּל-אוֹצְרוֹת מַלְכֵי יְהוּדָה, אֶתֵּן בְּיַד אֹיְבֵיהֶם, וּבְזָזוּם וּלְקָחוּם, וֶהֱבִיאוּם בָּבֶלָה.
And I will give all the wealth of this city, and all its produce, and all its precious things — and all the treasures of the kings of Judah I will give into the hand of their enemies; and they will plunder them, and take them, and carry them to Babylon.note
6
וְאַתָּה פַשְׁחוּר, וְכֹל יֹשְׁבֵי בֵיתֶךָ, תֵּלְכוּ, בַּשֶּׁבִי; וּבָבֶל תָּבוֹא, וְשָׁם תָּמוּת וְשָׁם תִּקָּבֵר--אַתָּה וְכָל-אֹהֲבֶיךָ, אֲשֶׁר-נִבֵּאתָ לָהֶם בַּשָּׁקֶר. {פ}
And you, Pashhur, and all who live in your house — you shall go into captivity. And to Babylon you shall come, and there you shall die, and there you shall be buried — you, and all who love you, to whom you have prophesied falsehood."note
7
פִּתִּיתַנִי יְהוָה וָאֶפָּת, חֲזַקְתַּנִי וַתּוּכָל; הָיִיתִי לִשְׂחוֹק כָּל-הַיּוֹם, כֻּלֹּה לֹעֵג לִי.
You enticed me, Jehovah, and I was enticed; you overpowered me, and you prevailed. I have become a laughingstock all day long — everyone mocks me.note
8
כִּי-מִדֵּי אֲדַבֵּר אֶזְעָק, חָמָס וָשֹׁד אֶקְרָא: כִּי-הָיָה דְבַר-יְהוָה לִי לְחֶרְפָּה וּלְקֶלֶס, כָּל-הַיּוֹם.
For as often as I speak, I cry out — "Violence and ruin!" I call; for the word of Jehovah has become for me a reproach and a derision all day long.note
9
וְאָמַרְתִּי לֹא-אֶזְכְּרֶנּוּ, וְלֹא-אֲדַבֵּר עוֹד בִּשְׁמוֹ, וְהָיָה בְלִבִּי כְּאֵשׁ בֹּעֶרֶת, עָצֻר בְּעַצְמֹתָי; וְנִלְאֵיתִי כַּלְכֵל, וְלֹא אוּכָל.
And I said: I will not remember him, and I will not speak anymore in his name — but it was in my heart like a burning fire, shut up in my bones; and I wore myself out holding it in — and I could not.note
10
כִּי שָׁמַעְתִּי דִּבַּת רַבִּים, מָגוֹר מִסָּבִיב, הַגִּידוּ וְנַגִּידֶנּוּ, כֹּל אֱנוֹשׁ שְׁלֹמִי שֹׁמְרֵי צַלְעִי; אוּלַי יְפֻתֶּה וְנוּכְלָה לוֹ, וְנִקְחָה נִקְמָתֵנוּ מִמֶּנּוּ.
For I heard the whispering of many — Terror all around! "Report him! Let us report him!" Every man of my peace — watchers of my stumble: "Perhaps he will be enticed, and we shall prevail over him, and take our revenge on him."note
11
וַיהוָה אוֹתִי כְּגִבּוֹר עָרִיץ, עַל-כֵּן רֹדְפַי יִכָּשְׁלוּ וְלֹא יֻכָלוּ; בֹּשׁוּ מְאֹד כִּי-לֹא הִשְׂכִּילוּ, כְּלִמַּת עוֹלָם לֹא תִשָּׁכֵחַ.
But Jehovah is with me like a dread warrior; therefore my pursuers will stumble, and will not prevail. They will be deeply ashamed, for they have not succeeded — a disgrace forever, that will not be forgotten.note
12
וַיהוָה צְבָאוֹת בֹּחֵן צַדִּיק, רֹאֶה כְלָיוֹת וָלֵב; אֶרְאֶה נִקְמָתְךָ מֵהֶם, כִּי אֵלֶיךָ גִּלִּיתִי אֶת-רִיבִי. {ס}
And Jehovah of hosts, who tests the righteous, who sees kidneys and heart — let me see your vengeance on them, for to you I have laid bare my case.note
13
שִׁירוּ, לַיהוָה--הַלְלוּ, אֶת-יְהוָה: כִּי הִצִּיל אֶת-נֶפֶשׁ אֶבְיוֹן, מִיַּד מְרֵעִים. {ס}
Sing to Jehovah! Praise Jehovah! For he has rescued the soul of the needy from the hand of evildoers.note
14
אָרוּר הַיּוֹם, אֲשֶׁר יֻלַּדְתִּי בּוֹ: יוֹם אֲשֶׁר-יְלָדַתְנִי אִמִּי, אַל-יְהִי בָרוּךְ.
Cursed be the day I was born on; the day my mother bore me — let it not be blessed.note
15

Cursed be the man who brought my father the news: "A son is born to you — a male!" — making him very glad.note
16
וְהָיָה הָאִישׁ הַהוּא, כֶּעָרִים אֲשֶׁר-הָפַךְ יְהוָה וְלֹא נִחָם; וְשָׁמַע זְעָקָה בַּבֹּקֶר, וּתְרוּעָה בְּעֵת צָהֳרָיִם.
And let that man be like the cities that Jehovah overthrew, and did not relent; and let him hear an outcry in the morning, and a war-shout at noon —note
17
