because he did not kill me from the womb — so my mother would have been my grave, and her womb pregnant forever.note
18
לָמָּה זֶּה מֵרֶחֶם יָצָאתִי, לִרְאוֹת עָמָל וְיָגוֹן; וַיִּכְלוּ בְּבֹשֶׁת, יָמָי. {פ}
Why is it that I came out of the womb — to see toil and sorrow, and my days consumed in shame?note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–3 · מָגוֹר מִסָּבִיב Magor-Missaviv

You are entering the sixth book of this site mid-story. Jeremiah of Anathoth — a priest's son drafted as a boy ("before I formed you in the womb, I knew you… and I said, Look — I do not know how to speak, for I am only a youth," chapter 1, not yet on these pages) — has by now spent years saying what no one in Jerusalem wants said. "These things" that Pashhur heard are chapter 19, just off this page: a clay flask smashed at the Potsherd Gate, and a sermon preached over the valley of Ben-Hinnom — the very valley whose Greek name this site met three chapters ago as Gehenna (Matthew 5:22). The sermon in Gehenna's valley earned the prophet the first recorded violence of his life: Pashhur son of Immer, paqid nagid — chief overseer in the house of Jehovah, the head of temple order — struck him and locked him overnight in the mahpekhet: "the twister," a frame that bent the prisoner's body (the word is built on haphakh, the overturning-verb — hold that root; the chapter will close with it).

The renaming. A night in the stocks produced not a retraction but a name. "Not Pashhur has Jehovah called your name — but Magor-Missaviv": Terror-All-Around (KJV runs it together as one grim word, "Magormissabib"; Douay translates it away — "fear on every side"). Prophets rename people as verdicts; this one is a forecast, and it is Jeremiah's own signature phrase of dread (6:25; 46:5; 49:29; Psalm 31 borrows it) — verse 10 will show the street throwing it back in his face.

Verses 4–6 · בְּיַד מֶלֶךְ-בָּבֶל beyad melekh-Bavel

Babylon, named at last. Until this oracle, the doom in Jeremiah's preaching comes from an unnamed "north." Here, for the first time in the book's oracles, the destination is said out loud — Babylon, five times in three verses, as if the word itself had been waiting in the stocks overnight. The naming dates the scene: after Carchemish (605 BC — the year this site's Daniel 1 opens), when the identity of the "north" stopped being a riddle. "All Judah I will give into the hand of the king of Babylon" — prophesied here, and recorded as done on Daniel's very first page. Pashhur's own sentence is measured to his office: exile, death, and burial in Babylon, "you, and all who love you, to whom you have prophesied falsehood" — the chief of temple order was also, it turns out, a comfortable false prophet; the man who policed the true one.

A librarian's aside on sealed clay. From the ash of the very destruction Jeremiah foretold, excavations in the City of David (2005, 2008) produced two clay seal-impressions found a few meters apart: "Gedaliah son of Pashhur" and "Jehucal son of Shelemiah" — two officials who appear side by side, as Jeremiah's enemies, in Jeremiah 38:1. Not this chapter's Pashhur (ours is the son of Immer) — but the prophet's enemies list, countersigned in fired clay.

Verses 7–9 · פִּתִּיתַנִי יְהוָה וָאֶפָּת pittitani … va'eppat

The boldest prayer in the prophets. The verb is patah, and its range is the whole scandal: to persuade; to entice (the lying spirit is sent to "entice" Ahab to his death, 1 Kings 22 — this verb); to seduce (the seduction law of Exodus 22:16 — this verb); to deceive. The shelf has spent four centuries deciding how much to let the verse say: KJV "O LORD, thou hast deceived me" — quoted here with its small-caps name set aside — NIV "you deceived me"; NWT "You have fooled me, O Jehovah"; TLB flatly "you deceived me"; the 1917 JPS, whose English runs beside the Hebrew in this site's own source edition, "enticed" — the choice this translation makes, leaving the darker readings in this note at full strength rather than in the verse's mouth. "You overpowered me, and you prevailed" — mark that last verb (yakhol, to be able, to prevail): the chapter is about to decline it through every party.

The fire in the bones. The resignation is tendered in verse 9 — "I will not remember him, and I will not speak anymore in his name" — and rejected by the prophet's own anatomy: "like a burning fire, shut up in my bones" (the KJV's line here — "as a burning fire shut up in my bones" — is nearly perfect and nearly ours); "I wore myself out holding it in — and I could not" (lo ukhal): the prevail-verb again, negated. God can; the prophet cannot. That asymmetry is the entire confession.

Verses 10–13 · כֹּל אֱנוֹשׁ שְׁלֹמִי kol enosh shelomi

His own oracle, thrown back — and the verb declined. The whispering crowd has adopted his nickname-verdict as a taunt: "Terror all around!" — Magor-Missaviv, now street mockery. "Every man of my peace" — the Hebrew idiom for the trusted circle, the table-friends (Psalm 41:9 runs on the same idiom, "the man of my peace, who ate my bread" — a verse with an upper-room future) — are now "watchers of my stumble" (a small shelf-wink: the letters of his "stumble," tsal'i, are the letters this project met in Eden as "side"). And their hope is verbatim: "perhaps he will be enticed, and we shall prevail over him" — the exact two verbs of verse 7. What the prophet said God did to him is precisely what his enemies dream of doing; the difference is the outcome. Verse 11 closes the ledger: "my pursuers… will not prevail… they have not succeeded" — and that last verb (lo hiskilu) is the very root whose participle is Daniel's maskilim, "the wise": his enemies are, by the letter, the un-wise. Four occurrences of one verb of power: God prevailed (v7); the prophet could not (v9); the enemies hope to (v10); they will not (v11).

The assayer, the kidneys, and the pivot. "Jehovah is with me like a dread warrior" (gibbor arits — gibbor, the mighty-man word from Genesis 6's strange page, now God's epithet); "who tests the righteous" — bochen, the assayer's verb, the refining furnace of Daniel 11:35 compressed to a participle; "who sees kidneys and heart" — the kidneys as Hebrew's seat of the hidden self (KJV, wonderfully, "the reins" — Latin renes, kidneys; four centuries of English readers picturing bridles). Revelation quotes this oath back across the canon: Thyatira's Christ — "I am the one who searches kidneys and hearts." And then, with no warning, verse 13: "Sing to Jehovah! Praise Jehovah!" — the lament-psalm's pivot (complaint → confidence → praise) compressed into a single line. Hold the praise carefully; the floor is about to give way.

Verses 14–18 · אָרוּר הַיּוֹם arur hayyom

The birth-curse — kept raw. From "Sing to Jehovah" to "Cursed be the day I was born," with no seam. The book's editors did not smooth the sequence, and this translation will not either: this is the rawest run of verses in the prophets, and its arrangement — praise, then the pit — is the honest shape of a real interior life. Job 3 is the twin ("Let the day perish wherein I was born"); which poet echoes which, scholars split, and this note reports the split. Notice where the curse does not land: not on God, not on himself — on the day, and on the poor messenger who brought the good news ("A son is born to you — a male!"), who is wished the fate of "the cities that Jehovah overthrew" — haphakh, Sodom's own verb from this site's Genesis 19 (ASV "the cities which Jehovah overthrew"), closing the bracket the chapter opened: the man twisted in the mahpekhet ends by wishing the overturning on the world. "And did not relent" — nacham, the comfort-and-regret root this site has tracked since Noah's name.

The womb, twice. "Why is it that I came out of the womb — to see toil and sorrow?" — asked, devastatingly, by the one man in scripture who was told why: "before I formed you in the womb, I knew you" (1:5). The call and the curse are aimed at the same womb. The chapter ends with the question unanswered — no seam, no comfort; the next verse of the book (21:1) is simply the next assignment. The resolution is the one verse 9 already gave: the fire in the bones wins by default, and he keeps speaking. Not an answer — a vocation.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "a burning fire shut up in my bones" — the KJV's line, nearly untouchable, nearly kept whole; Magor-Missaviv left standing as the name it is (with KJV, against Douay's "fear on every side").

Where it goes its own way: "You enticed me… and I was enticed" (KJV/NIV "deceived," NWT "fooled" — the darker readings carried in the note at full strength); "kidneys and heart" kept literal (KJV "the reins"); "every man of my peace" kept as the idiom is (KJV "my familiars"); "all who love you" for Pashhur's circle (KJV "thy friends"); "the twister" explained for the stocks.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: haphakh, Sodom's overthrow-verb (19:25) — bracketing the chapter from the "twister" stocks to the birth-curse; nacham, the relent-root, from Noah's page; gibbor (6:4) turned into God's own epithet; the valley of Ben-Hinnom — the sermon that caused it all, preached in the valley Greek wears as Gehenna (Matthew 5:22); "kidneys and heart," paid forward across the whole canon to Thyatira (Revelation 2:23); and "into the hand of the king of Babylon" — prophesied here, receipted on Daniel's first page (Daniel 1:2). Planted: "every man of my peace," waiting for Psalm 41:9 and an upper room; Babylon, now on this site's map with its own encyclopedia entry — and waiting, much further on, for Revelation to make the name a cipher; and Jeremiah's other confessions (chapters 11–18), of which this chapter is the last and rawest.

Next installment in this book: Jeremiah 21 — the king's own delegation arrives ("inquire of Jehovah for us…") and receives the bleakest answer in the book.

Jeremiah 21 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
הַדָּבָר אֲשֶׁר-הָיָה אֶל-יִרְמְיָהוּ, מֵאֵת יְהוָה--בִּשְׁלֹחַ אֵלָיו הַמֶּלֶךְ צִדְקִיָּהוּ, אֶת-פַּשְׁחוּר בֶּן-מַלְכִּיָּה, וְאֶת-צְפַנְיָה בֶן-מַעֲשֵׂיָה הַכֹּהֵן, לֵאמֹר.
The word that came to Jeremiah from Jehovah, when King Zedekiah sent to him Pashhur son of Malchiah, and Zephaniah son of Maaseiah, the priest, saying:note
2
דְּרָשׁ-נָא בַעֲדֵנוּ אֶת-יְהוָה, כִּי נְבוּכַדְרֶאצַּר מֶלֶךְ-בָּבֶל נִלְחָם עָלֵינוּ; אוּלַי יַעֲשֶׂה יְהוָה אוֹתָנוּ כְּכָל-נִפְלְאֹתָיו, וְיַעֲלֶה מֵעָלֵינוּ.
"Inquire of Jehovah for us, please — for Nebuchadrezzar king of Babylon is making war against us. Perhaps Jehovah will deal with us according to all his wondrous works, and he will go up from us."note
3
וַיֹּאמֶר יִרְמְיָהוּ, אֲלֵיהֶם: כֹּה תֹאמְרֻן, אֶל-צִדְקִיָּהוּ. {ס}
And Jeremiah said to them: "Thus you shall say to Zedekiah:note
4
כֹּה-אָמַר יְהוָה אֱלֹהֵי יִשְׂרָאֵל, הִנְנִי מֵסֵב אֶת-כְּלֵי הַמִּלְחָמָה אֲשֶׁר בְּיֶדְכֶם, אֲשֶׁר אַתֶּם נִלְחָמִים בָּם אֶת-מֶלֶךְ בָּבֶל וְאֶת-הַכַּשְׂדִּים, הַצָּרִים עֲלֵיכֶם מִחוּץ לַחוֹמָה; וְאָסַפְתִּי אוֹתָם, אֶל-תּוֹךְ הָעִיר הַזֹּאת.
Thus says Jehovah, the God of Israel: Look — I am turning back the weapons of war that are in your hands, with which you are fighting the king of Babylon and the Chaldeans besieging you outside the wall; and I will gather them into the middle of this city.note
5
וְנִלְחַמְתִּי אֲנִי אִתְּכֶם, בְּיָד נְטוּיָה וּבִזְרוֹעַ חֲזָקָה, וּבְאַף וּבְחֵמָה, וּבְקֶצֶף גָּדוֹל.
And I myself will fight against you — with an outstretched hand and with a strong arm, in anger, and in fury, and in great wrath.note
6
וְהִכֵּיתִי, אֶת-יוֹשְׁבֵי הָעִיר הַזֹּאת, וְאֶת-הָאָדָם, וְאֶת-הַבְּהֵמָה: בְּדֶבֶר גָּדוֹל, יָמֻתוּ.
And I will strike the inhabitants of this city, both human and beast; by a great pestilence they shall die.note
7
וְאַחֲרֵי-כֵן נְאֻם-יְהוָה אֶתֵּן אֶת-צִדְקִיָּהוּ מֶלֶךְ-יְהוּדָה וְאֶת-עֲבָדָיו וְאֶת-הָעָם וְאֶת-הַנִּשְׁאָרִים בָּעִיר הַזֹּאת מִן-הַדֶּבֶר מִן-הַחֶרֶב וּמִן-הָרָעָב, בְּיַד נְבוּכַדְרֶאצַּר מֶלֶךְ-בָּבֶל, וּבְיַד אֹיְבֵיהֶם, וּבְיַד מְבַקְשֵׁי נַפְשָׁם; וְהִכָּם לְפִי-חֶרֶב--לֹא-יָחוּס עֲלֵיהֶם, וְלֹא יַחְמֹל וְלֹא יְרַחֵם.
And after that — declares Jehovah — I will give Zedekiah king of Judah, and his servants, and the people, and those left in this city from the pestilence, from the sword, and from the famine, into the hand of Nebuchadrezzar king of Babylon, and into the hand of their enemies, and into the hand of those who seek their life. And he will strike them with the edge of the sword — he will not spare them, and he will not pity, and he will not have compassion.note
8
וְאֶל-הָעָם הַזֶּה תֹּאמַר, כֹּה אָמַר יְהוָה: הִנְנִי נֹתֵן לִפְנֵיכֶם, אֶת-דֶּרֶךְ הַחַיִּים וְאֶת-דֶּרֶךְ הַמָּוֶת.
And to this people you shall say: Thus says Jehovah: Look — I am setting before you the way of life and the way of death.note
9
הַיֹּשֵׁב, בָּעִיר הַזֹּאת, יָמוּת, בַּחֶרֶב וּבָרָעָב וּבַדָּבֶר; וְהַיּוֹצֵא וְנָפַל עַל-הַכַּשְׂדִּים הַצָּרִים עֲלֵיכֶם, יחיה וְחָיָה, וְהָיְתָה-לּוֹ נַפְשׁוֹ, לְשָׁלָל.
Whoever stays in this city shall die — by the sword, and by the famine, and by the pestilence. But whoever goes out and falls away to the Chaldeans besieging you — he shall live; and he shall have his life as spoil.note
10
כִּי שַׂמְתִּי פָנַי בָּעִיר הַזֹּאת לְרָעָה, וְלֹא לְטוֹבָה--נְאֻם-יְהוָה; בְּיַד-מֶלֶךְ בָּבֶל תִּנָּתֵן, וּשְׂרָפָהּ בָּאֵשׁ. {ס}
For I have set my face against this city for evil, and not for good — declares Jehovah. Into the hand of the king of Babylon it shall be given, and he shall burn it with fire.note
11
וּלְבֵית מֶלֶךְ יְהוּדָה, שִׁמְעוּ דְּבַר-יְהוָה.
And to the house of the king of Judah: Hear the word of Jehovah!note
12
בֵּית דָּוִד, כֹּה אָמַר יְהוָה, דִּינוּ לַבֹּקֶר מִשְׁפָּט, וְהַצִּילוּ גָזוּל מִיַּד עוֹשֵׁק: פֶּן-תֵּצֵא כָאֵשׁ חֲמָתִי, וּבָעֲרָה וְאֵין מְכַבֶּה, מִפְּנֵי, רֹעַ מעלליהם מַעַלְלֵיכֶם.
House of David, thus says Jehovah: Render justice in the morning; rescue the robbed from the hand of the oppressor — lest my fury go out like fire, and burn with none to quench it, because of the evil of your doings.note
13
הִנְנִי אֵלַיִךְ יֹשֶׁבֶת הָעֵמֶק, צוּר הַמִּישֹׁר--נְאֻם-יְהוָה; הָאֹמְרִים מִי-יֵחַת עָלֵינוּ, וּמִי יָבוֹא בִּמְעוֹנוֹתֵינוּ.
Look — I am against you, inhabitant of the valley, rock of the plain — declares Jehovah — you who say: "Who shall come down against us? And who shall enter our lairs?"note
14
וּפָקַדְתִּי עֲלֵיכֶם כִּפְרִי מַעַלְלֵיכֶם, נְאֻם-יְהוָה; וְהִצַּתִּי אֵשׁ בְּיַעְרָהּ, וְאָכְלָה כָּל-סְבִיבֶיהָ.
And I will visit upon you according to the fruit of your doings — declares Jehovah — and I will kindle a fire in her forest, and it shall devour everything around her.note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–2 · פַּשְׁחוּר בֶּן-מַלְכִּיָּה Pashchur ben-Malkiyyah

The other Pashhur — and the scroll's own filing system. Read carefully: this is not chapter 20's Pashhur (son of Immer, the stocks). This one is Pashhur son of Malchiah — and the book has jumped seventeen years: chapter 20 stood in Jehoiakim's Jerusalem around 605 BC; chapter 21 opens in the final siege under Zedekiah, 588. Jeremiah's chapters are filed by theme and catchword, not by calendar — and the likeliest reason these two scenes sit side by side is the shared name itself: the scroll's ancient editors shelved Pashhur next to Pashhur. This second Pashhur has a future in the book: he stands among the officials who drop Jeremiah into the cistern (38:1) — the same verse where a "Gedaliah son of Pashhur" appears, whose clay seal-impression came out of the City of David's ash (chapter 20's note carries that story). Note also the king's spelling: Nebuchadrezzar, with an r — Jeremiah's usual form, closest to the Akkadian Nabû-kudurri-uṣur (KJV and ASV keep the r here; Douay writes "Nabuchodonosor," a third spelling on one shelf; the familiar "Nebuchadnezzar" is the softened form Kings and Daniel use).

The request: a rerun of the wondrous works. "Perhaps Jehovah will deal with us according to all his wondrous works" — niflaot, the Exodus-wonder word. The delegation is asking, in effect, for the archives: another Red Sea, or another night like 701 BC, when Sennacherib's army melted away from these same walls. It is a real prayer, prayed at the last possible hour, by a court that has ignored the praying prophet for forty years.

Verses 3–7 · בְּיָד נְטוּיָה וּבִזְרוֹעַ חֲזָקָה beyad netuyah uvizroa chazaqah

The Exodus formula, aimed inward. The answer begins with the weapons turned around in the defenders' own hands — and then the most chilling sentence in the book's diplomacy: "I myself will fight against you — with an outstretched hand and with a strong arm." That is the redemption formula: "a mighty hand and an outstretched arm" is how Deuteronomy says brought you out of Egypt, over and over, like a creed. The delegation asked for the Exodus God; the answer is that he is already on the field — on the other side. The triad that will drum through the rest of the book follows — sword, famine, pestilence — and the sentence on the court ends with a triple negation as bleak as Hebrew can build: "he will not spare… and he will not pity… and he will not have compassion." Zedekiah's own end (not told here; 39:5-7 will tell it) matched the oracle's temperature.

Verses 8–10 · דֶּרֶךְ הַחַיִּים וְאֶת-דֶּרֶךְ הַמָּוֶת derekh hachayyim… derekh hammavet

The Two Ways, at their sharpest. "Look — I am setting before you the way of life and the way of death" is Deuteronomy 30:19 quoted back to its own people — "I have set before you life and death… therefore choose life" — but with the choice shrunk to one grim door: staying in the city is the way of death (sword, famine, pestilence, the triad again); the way of life is to walk out and surrender. This site met the Two Ways three chapters ago at the Sermon's narrow gate, and in the Didache's opening line; here the fork is drawn on a wartime map, and the life-road runs straight into the enemy camp. "He shall have his life as spoil" (KJV "his life shall be unto him for a prey"; NIV "will escape with their lives") — a soldier's grim idiom: the only plunder you will carry out of this war is yourself. Jeremiah will hand the phrase, later, to the two men who trusted him (38:2 repeats this oracle nearly verbatim — and it is exactly the preaching that gets him thrown into the cistern as a traitor, 38:4; Ebed-melech and Baruch each receive "your life as spoil" as a personal promise, 39:18; 45:5). A scroll-note: verse 9 carries a ketiv/qere — the written and read forms of "he shall live" standing side by side in the Hebrew column, the manuscript's double track this site first met in Daniel 11.

Verses 11–14 · דִּינוּ לַבֹּקֶר מִשְׁפָּט dinu laboqer mishpat

The throne's one job. The oracle pivots from the people to the dynasty: "House of David… render justice in the morning" (KJV "Execute judgment in the morning") — the king's court sat at the gate in the cool of the morning; daily justice, especially for "the robbed," was the Davidic job description, and this site's shelf holds the original posting: Abraham's household was chosen "to do righteousness and justice" (Genesis 18:19). The alternative is the chapter's fire-chain: wrath "like fire… with none to quench it" (v12), lit for real in verse 14 — "I will kindle a fire in her forest": Jerusalem's "forest" is Solomon's cedar palace, the House of the Forest of Lebanon — the royal complex itself as kindling (2 Kings 25:9 reports the fulfillment: "every great house he burned with fire"). Verse 12 carries the chapter's second ketiv/qere: the written "their doings" beside the read "your doings" — the scribes preserving even the wobble of a pronoun.

The smug topography. "Look — I am against you, inhabitant of the valley, rock of the plain" (KJV "O inhabitant of the valley, and rock of the plain") — Jerusalem, ringed by ravines on three sides, purring over its own geography: "Who shall come down against us? And who shall enter our lairs?" — me'onot, animal dens: the city as a predator confident in its cave. This site has heard a fortified boast answered from above before — at Babel, where the builders reached up and "Jehovah came down." The answer to "who shall come down against us?" was on file.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "Nebuchadrezzar" kept with the r, as Jeremiah spells him (with KJV and ASV, against Douay's "Nabuchodonosor" and the harmonized "Nebuchadnezzar" of Kings and Daniel); the sword–famine–pestilence triad in the scroll's own order.

Where it goes its own way: "he shall have his life as spoil" (KJV "for a prey") — the soldier's-booty idiom kept; "wondrous works" for niflaot, the Exodus-wonder word; "our lairs" for the dens of verse 13 (KJV "habitations" — the animal image kept); "render justice in the morning" (KJV "execute judgment").

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: the Two Ways — Deuteronomy's fork, the Sermon's two gates (Matthew 7:13), now drawn on a wartime map; Babylon, named from the stocks (20:4) and now at the walls — the book's two Pashhur scenes filed side by side, seventeen years apart; the house of David measured against Abraham's brief, "to do righteousness and justice" (18:19); the city that asks "who shall come down against us?" answered by Babel's page — "Jehovah came down" (11:5); and the same king on two pages — Daniel's opening (his first visit, 605) and this oracle (his last, 588) (Daniel 1:1). Planted: this oracle as the charge sheet for the cistern (38:1-4 — with this chapter's Pashhur among the accusers); "life as spoil" waiting for Ebed-melech and Baruch; the fire in the cedar "forest," waiting for 586 and 2 Kings 25:9; and Zedekiah's last sight, waiting at Riblah.

Next installment in this book: Jeremiah 22 — the tariff of kings: "weep not for the dead," the donkey's burial, and Coniah the discarded signet ring.

Genesis 21 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
וַיהוָה פָּקַד אֶת-שָׂרָה, כַּאֲשֶׁר אָמָר; וַיַּעַשׂ יְהוָה לְשָׂרָה, כַּאֲשֶׁר דִּבֵּר.
And Jehovah visited Sarah as he had said, and Jehovah did for Sarah as he had spoken.note
2
וַתַּהַר וַתֵּלֶד שָׂרָה לְאַבְרָהָם בֵּן, לִזְקֻנָיו, לַמּוֹעֵד, אֲשֶׁר-דִּבֶּר אֹתוֹ אֱלֹהִים.
And Sarah conceived, and bore Abraham a son in his old age — at the appointed time that God had spoken of with him.note
3
וַיִּקְרָא אַבְרָהָם אֶת-שֶׁם-בְּנוֹ הַנּוֹלַד-לוֹ, אֲשֶׁר-יָלְדָה-לּוֹ שָׂרָה--יִצְחָק.
And Abraham called the name of his son who was born to him, whom Sarah bore to him — Isaac.note
4
וַיָּמָל אַבְרָהָם אֶת-יִצְחָק בְּנוֹ, בֶּן-שְׁמֹנַת יָמִים, כַּאֲשֶׁר צִוָּה אֹתוֹ, אֱלֹהִים.
And Abraham circumcised Isaac his son at eight days old, as God had commanded him.note
5
וְאַבְרָהָם, בֶּן-מְאַת שָׁנָה, בְּהִוָּלֶד לוֹ, אֵת יִצְחָק בְּנוֹ.
And Abraham was 100 years old when Isaac his son was born to him.note
6
וַתֹּאמֶר שָׂרָה--צְחֹק, עָשָׂה לִי אֱלֹהִים: כָּל-הַשֹּׁמֵעַ, יִצְחַק-לִי.
And Sarah said: "Laughter God has made for me; everyone who hears will laugh for me."note
7
וַתֹּאמֶר, מִי מִלֵּל לְאַבְרָהָם, הֵינִיקָה בָנִים, שָׂרָה: כִּי-יָלַדְתִּי בֵן, לִזְקֻנָיו.
And she said: "Who would have declared to Abraham: Sarah nurses children! — for I have borne a son in his old age."note
8
וַיִּגְדַּל הַיֶּלֶד, וַיִּגָּמַל; וַיַּעַשׂ אַבְרָהָם מִשְׁתֶּה גָדוֹל, בְּיוֹם הִגָּמֵל אֶת-יִצְחָק.
And the child grew, and was weaned; and Abraham made a great feast on the day Isaac was weaned.note
9
וַתֵּרֶא שָׂרָה אֶת-בֶּן-הָגָר הַמִּצְרִית, אֲשֶׁר-יָלְדָה לְאַבְרָהָם--מְצַחֵק.
And Sarah saw the son of Hagar the Egyptian, whom she had borne to Abraham — laughing.note
10
וַתֹּאמֶר, לְאַבְרָהָם, גָּרֵשׁ הָאָמָה הַזֹּאת, וְאֶת-בְּנָהּ: כִּי לֹא יִירַשׁ בֶּן-הָאָמָה הַזֹּאת, עִם-בְּנִי עִם-יִצְחָק.
And she said to Abraham: "Drive out this slave-woman and her son — for the son of this slave-woman shall not inherit with my son, with Isaac."note
11
וַיֵּרַע הַדָּבָר מְאֹד, בְּעֵינֵי אַבְרָהָם, עַל, אוֹדֹת בְּנוֹ.
And the thing was very grievous in Abraham's eyes, on account of his son.note
12
וַיֹּאמֶר אֱלֹהִים אֶל-אַבְרָהָם, אַל-יֵרַע בְּעֵינֶיךָ עַל-הַנַּעַר וְעַל-אֲמָתֶךָ--כֹּל אֲשֶׁר תֹּאמַר אֵלֶיךָ שָׂרָה, שְׁמַע בְּקֹלָהּ: כִּי בְיִצְחָק, יִקָּרֵא לְךָ זָרַע.
And God said to Abraham: "Let it not be grievous in your eyes over the boy and over your slave-woman. All that Sarah says to you — listen to her voice; for in Isaac shall seed be called for you.note
13
וְגַם אֶת-בֶּן-הָאָמָה, לְגוֹי אֲשִׂימֶנּוּ: כִּי זַרְעֲךָ, הוּא.
And also the son of the slave-woman — I will make him into a nation, for he is your seed."note
14
וַיַּשְׁכֵּם אַבְרָהָם בַּבֹּקֶר וַיִּקַּח-לֶחֶם וְחֵמַת מַיִם וַיִּתֵּן אֶל-הָגָר שָׂם עַל-שִׁכְמָהּ, וְאֶת-הַיֶּלֶד--וַיְשַׁלְּחֶהָ; וַתֵּלֶךְ וַתֵּתַע, בְּמִדְבַּר בְּאֵר שָׁבַע.
And Abraham rose early in the morning, and took bread and a skin of water and gave them to Hagar — set them on her shoulder — and the child, and sent her away. And she went, and wandered in the wilderness of Beersheba.note
15
וַיִּכְלוּ הַמַּיִם, מִן-הַחֵמֶת; וַתַּשְׁלֵךְ אֶת-הַיֶּלֶד, תַּחַת אַחַד הַשִּׂיחִם.
And the water from the skin was finished; and she cast the child under one of the bushes.note
16
וַתֵּלֶךְ וַתֵּשֶׁב לָהּ מִנֶּגֶד, הַרְחֵק כִּמְטַחֲוֵי קֶשֶׁת, כִּי אָמְרָה, אַל-אֶרְאֶה בְּמוֹת הַיָּלֶד; וַתֵּשֶׁב מִנֶּגֶד, וַתִּשָּׂא אֶת-קֹלָהּ וַתֵּבְךְּ.
And she went and sat herself down opposite, far off — about a bowshot — for she said: "Let me not see the death of the child." And she sat opposite, and lifted up her voice, and wept.note
17
וַיִּשְׁמַע אֱלֹהִים, אֶת-קוֹל הַנַּעַר, וַיִּקְרָא מַלְאַךְ אֱלֹהִים אֶל-הָגָר מִן-הַשָּׁמַיִם, וַיֹּאמֶר לָהּ מַה-לָּךְ הָגָר; אַל-תִּירְאִי, כִּי-שָׁמַע אֱלֹהִים אֶל-קוֹל הַנַּעַר בַּאֲשֶׁר הוּא-שָׁם.
And God heard the voice of the boy; and the angel of God called to Hagar from the heavens, and said to her: "What is it, Hagar? Do not be afraid — for God has heard the voice of the boy, there where he is.note
18
קוּמִי שְׂאִי אֶת-הַנַּעַר, וְהַחֲזִיקִי אֶת-יָדֵךְ בּוֹ: כִּי-לְגוֹי גָּדוֹל, אֲשִׂימֶנּוּ.
Rise, lift up the boy, and hold him fast with your hand — for I will make him into a great nation."note
19
וַיִּפְקַח אֱלֹהִים אֶת-עֵינֶיהָ, וַתֵּרֶא בְּאֵר מָיִם; וַתֵּלֶךְ וַתְּמַלֵּא אֶת-הַחֵמֶת, מַיִם, וַתַּשְׁקְ, אֶת-הַנָּעַר.
And God opened her eyes, and she saw a well of water; and she went and filled the skin with water, and gave the boy a drink.note
20
וַיְהִי אֱלֹהִים אֶת-הַנַּעַר, וַיִּגְדָּל; וַיֵּשֶׁב, בַּמִּדְבָּר, וַיְהִי, רֹבֶה קַשָּׁת.
And God was with the boy, and he grew; and he lived in the wilderness, and became an archer.note
21
וַיֵּשֶׁב, בְּמִדְבַּר פָּארָן; וַתִּקַּח-לוֹ אִמּוֹ אִשָּׁה, מֵאֶרֶץ מִצְרָיִם. {פ}
And he lived in the wilderness of Paran; and his mother took a wife for him from the land of Egypt.note
22
וַיְהִי, בָּעֵת הַהִוא, וַיֹּאמֶר אֲבִימֶלֶךְ וּפִיכֹל שַׂר-צְבָאוֹ, אֶל-אַבְרָהָם לֵאמֹר: אֱלֹהִים עִמְּךָ, בְּכֹל אֲשֶׁר-אַתָּה עֹשֶׂה.
And so it was at that time: Abimelech — with Phicol, the commander of his army — said to Abraham: "God is with you in all that you are doing.note
23
וְעַתָּה, הִשָּׁבְעָה לִּי בֵאלֹהִים הֵנָּה, אִם-תִּשְׁקֹר לִי, וּלְנִינִי וּלְנֶכְדִּי; כַּחֶסֶד אֲשֶׁר-עָשִׂיתִי עִמְּךָ, תַּעֲשֶׂה עִמָּדִי, וְעִם-הָאָרֶץ, אֲשֶׁר-גַּרְתָּה בָּהּ.
Now, then — swear to me by God, here, that you will not deal falsely with me, or with my offspring, or with my posterity. Like the kindness I have done with you, you shall do with me, and with the land where you have sojourned."note
24
וַיֹּאמֶר, אַבְרָהָם, אָנֹכִי, אִשָּׁבֵעַ.
And Abraham said: "I will swear."note
25
וְהוֹכִחַ אַבְרָהָם, אֶת-אֲבִימֶלֶךְ, עַל-אֹדוֹת בְּאֵר הַמַּיִם, אֲשֶׁר גָּזְלוּ עַבְדֵי אֲבִימֶלֶךְ.
And Abraham reproved Abimelech on account of the well of water that Abimelech's servants had seized.note
26
וַיֹּאמֶר אֲבִימֶלֶךְ--לֹא יָדַעְתִּי, מִי עָשָׂה אֶת-הַדָּבָר הַזֶּה; וְגַם-אַתָּה לֹא-הִגַּדְתָּ לִּי, וְגַם אָנֹכִי לֹא שָׁמַעְתִּי--בִּלְתִּי הַיּוֹם.
And Abimelech said: "I do not know who did this thing. You did not tell me either — nor have I heard of it, except today."note
27
וַיִּקַּח אַבְרָהָם צֹאן וּבָקָר, וַיִּתֵּן לַאֲבִימֶלֶךְ; וַיִּכְרְתוּ שְׁנֵיהֶם, בְּרִית.
And Abraham took sheep and cattle and gave them to Abimelech, and the two of them cut a covenant.note
28
וַיַּצֵּב אַבְרָהָם, אֶת-שֶׁבַע כִּבְשֹׂת הַצֹּאן--לְבַדְּהֶן.
And Abraham set seven ewe-lambs of the flock by themselves.note
29
וַיֹּאמֶר אֲבִימֶלֶךְ, אֶל-אַבְרָהָם: מָה הֵנָּה, שֶׁבַע כְּבָשֹׂת הָאֵלֶּה, אֲשֶׁר הִצַּבְתָּ, לְבַדָּנָה.
And Abimelech said to Abraham: "What are these seven ewe-lambs that you have set by themselves?"note
30
וַיֹּאמֶר--כִּי אֶת-שֶׁבַע כְּבָשֹׂת, תִּקַּח מִיָּדִי: בַּעֲבוּר תִּהְיֶה-לִּי לְעֵדָה, כִּי חָפַרְתִּי אֶת-הַבְּאֵר הַזֹּאת.
And he said: "These seven ewe-lambs you shall take from my hand, so that they may be a witness for me that I dug this well."note
31
עַל-כֵּן, קָרָא לַמָּקוֹם הַהוּא--בְּאֵר שָׁבַע: כִּי שָׁם נִשְׁבְּעוּ, שְׁנֵיהֶם.
Therefore that place is called Beersheba — for there the two of them swore.note
32
וַיִּכְרְתוּ בְרִית, בִּבְאֵר שָׁבַע; וַיָּקָם אֲבִימֶלֶךְ, וּפִיכֹל שַׂר-צְבָאוֹ, וַיָּשֻׁבוּ, אֶל-אֶרֶץ פְּלִשְׁתִּים.
And they cut a covenant at Beersheba. Then Abimelech rose — with Phicol, the commander of his army — and they returned to the land of the Philistines.note
33
וַיִּטַּע אֶשֶׁל, בִּבְאֵר שָׁבַע; וַיִּקְרָא-שָׁם--בְּשֵׁם יְהוָה, אֵל עוֹלָם.
And he planted a tamarisk at Beersheba, and called there on the name of Jehovah, El Olam — the Everlasting God.note
34
וַיָּגָר אַבְרָהָם בְּאֶרֶץ פְּלִשְׁתִּים, יָמִים רַבִּים. {פ}
And Abraham sojourned in the land of the Philistines many days.note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–7 · צְחֹק עָשָׂה לִי אֱלֹהִים tsechoq asah li Elohim

Visited — and the laughter lands. "Jehovah visited Sarah" — paqad, the attending-verb that cuts both ways: it visits a son on a barren woman here, and in Jeremiah it "visits" a city's doings upon her — same verb, opposite freight (KJV "visited" with us; the 1917 JPS softens to "remembered"). The birth comes "at the appointed time" — la-moed, the word hung with the lights on day four. And then the name, banked for three chapters, is finally paid: Abraham laughed face-down (17:17); Sarah laughed inside the tent and denied it (18:12-15); now — "Laughter God has made for me" (the Hebrew fronts the word, and so does this translation; KJV "God hath made me to laugh") — and the boy is Yitschaq, "he laughs." One shadow in the sunlight: "everyone who hears will laugh li" — for me, or at me? The preposition is open, and the woman who once laughed in fear knows both readings. Verse 7's "who would have declared" uses a verb (millel) so rare it is nearly a private word — as if ordinary speech had no form for this sentence. The eighth-day circumcision keeps 17:12 at the first opportunity; Abraham is 100.

Verses 8–13 · מְצַחֵק metsacheq

The boy caught "Isaac-ing." At the weaning feast (two or three years on), Sarah sees the son of Hagar — laughing: metsacheq, the participle of the very verb that names Isaac. Whatever Ishmael was doing, in Hebrew he was doing Isaac — playing the laughter, or playing the heir. The verdicts on the shelf are a spectrum: KJV "mocking"; Douay, from the Vulgate, the gentlest — "playing"; the Septuagint adds "with her son Isaac"; and Paul, reading through his own battles, "persecuted" (Galatians 4:29). This translation prints the neutral surface — laughing — so the pun stays visible, and shelves the verdicts here. Sarah's demand uses the Bible's expulsion-verb: "Drive out" (garesh) — the word that emptied Eden (3:24) and exiled Cain (4:14). Note, too, the vocabulary shift: chapter 16's shifchah (slave-girl) has hardened into amah (slave-woman) — the household's words for Hagar have aged with the trouble.

"Listen to her voice." God's ruling contains a phrase this site has met before, in the garden: shema beqolah — the exact words of 3:17, where "you listened to the voice of your wife" opened the indictment. Here the same phrase is a command: "all that Sarah says to you — listen to her voice." The words that once named a fall now carry the plan forward — "for in Isaac shall seed be called for you" (KJV nearly verbatim) — with the double promise attached: Ishmael too becomes a nation, "for he is your seed."

Verses 14–21 · כִּי-שָׁמַע אֱלֹהִים אֶל-קוֹל הַנַּעַר ki-shama Elohim el-qol hanna'ar

Dawn, a skin of water, and an arithmetic problem. "Abraham rose early in the morning" — remember the habit; it returns on a far worse morning, one chapter from now. Bread, a skin of water (KJV, delightfully, "a bottle"), "and the child" — and here the honest note: by the book's own numbers (16:16; 21:5, plus a weaning), Ishmael is a teenager, yet the scene reads like a small child — carried, cast under a bush, wept over from a distance. Readers who track the Torah's source-seams hear two tellings braided; harmonizers picture an exhausted sixteen-year-old collapsed in the heat. The seam is shown, not sanded. Hagar wanders the wilderness of Beersheba until the water is finished, casts the child under a bush, and sits "about a bowshot" away — the distance measured, with terrible economy, in the weapon her son will one day master (v20).

The name that happens as a verb. Now notice what the narrator has been doing: in this entire chapter the boy is never once called Ishmael — "the son of Hagar," "the child," "the boy." The name is withheld so that it can happen: "And God heard (shama Elohim) the voice of the boy" — Yishma-El, "God hears," paid as an event, exactly as promised at his announcement (16:11). "There where he is" (ba'asher hu sham) — the rabbis loved that clause: judged where he is, not where he will be. "And God opened her eyes, and she saw a well" — for the woman who named God "the One who sees" (16:13), sight returned as rescue: Hagar's two desert scenes close on the same exchange of seeing. "God was with the boy"; he grows, an archer in Paran; and his mother takes him a wife "from the land of Egypt" — Hagar the Egyptian (16:1), full circle.

Verses 22–26 · אֱלֹהִים עִמְּךָ Elohim immekha

Abimelech returns — with a general and a request. The king of Gerar, last seen returning Sarah with a thousand pieces of silver (ch. 20), arrives with Phicol, the commander of his army — a treaty delegation with the muscle standing visibly behind it. His opening line is the chapter's quiet verdict from outside: "God is with you in all that you are doing" — said to the very man whose last appearance in Gerar was a half-truth that nearly wrecked a kingdom. The oath he asks reaches down three generations (nin and nekhed — offspring and posterity, a rare legal pair), invoking the chesed — the covenant-kindness — he showed first. Abraham's answer is two words in Hebrew: "I will swear." Then, business: the seized well. In the Negev, water rights are the real diplomacy, and the narrative gives the dispute more verses than the oath.

Verses 27–34 · בְּאֵר שָׁבַע … אֵל עוֹלָם Be'er Sheva … El Olam

Seven ewes, and a name with two readings that are one. Abraham sets seven ewe-lambs apart; Abimelech's acceptance of them is a receipt — "a witness for me that I dug this well": property law by livestock, the Negev's paperwork. And the place-name banks the whole transaction twice over: Be'er Sheva — "well of the seven" and "well of the oath" — because Hebrew's verb "to swear" (nishba) is literally to seven oneself: the two etymologies the text offers (v30, v31) are one word wearing both faces. Beersheba enters the story here and never leaves it — "from Dan to Beersheba" will become the Bible's own way of saying the whole land.

The tamarisk and the fourth El-name. Abraham plants an eshel — a tamarisk, a desert tree that lives for centuries: planting one at a well is filing a claim on the future in slow motion (KJV, following an older guess, "planted a grove"; ASV "a tamarisk tree" with us). And there he calls on the name of Jehovah, El Olam — the Everlasting God: the fourth El-name this site has collected at a landmark — El Elyon at Salem (ch. 14), El Roi at Hagar's first well (ch. 16), El Shaddai at the covenant (ch. 17), now El Olam at a tree beside a sworn well (NWT, keeping its clock, "the indefinitely lasting God"). "The land of the Philistines" carries the standing honesty flag: the Philistines of the archaeological record arrive on this coast centuries after the patriarchs — the narrator, as with "Ur of the Chaldeans," speaks the reader's geography, not Abraham's.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "Jehovah visited Sarah" with the KJV (against JPS's softer "remembered"); "in Isaac shall seed be called" nearly KJV verbatim; "cut a covenant," as always.

Where it goes its own way: "laughing" for metsacheq (KJV "mocking," Douay "playing" — the verdicts shelved in the note, the pun kept visible in the text); "a skin of water" (KJV "a bottle"); "tamarisk" (KJV "grove"); "slave-woman" for amah, kept distinct from chapter 16's shifchah; and "Laughter God has made for me" — the Hebrew's fronting kept.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: the laughter thread — Abraham's laugh (17:17), Sarah's hidden laugh (18:12), now a name and a song; "listen to her voice" — the phrase of the garden's indictment (3:17), inverted into a command; garesh, the expulsion-verb of Eden and Cain; "God heard" — Ishmael's name, promised at (16:11), paid as an event; eyes opened for the namer of El Roi (16:13); the eighth day, commanded at (17:12) and kept; and Abimelech's arc from the sister-ruse (20:15) closed with a treaty. Planted: "rose early in the morning" — the habit that returns on the worst morning of all (22:3); Beersheba, the patriarchal address from here on; El Olam joining the El-name series; and the bowshot that became an archer.

Next installment in this book: Genesis 22 — the Aqedah: "Take your son, your only one, whom you love…" — the hardest chapter in the book.

Genesis 22 — The MiSTeR Translation

1
וַיְהִי, אַחַר הַדְּבָרִים הָאֵלֶּה, וְהָאֱלֹהִים, נִסָּה אֶת-אַבְרָהָם; וַיֹּאמֶר אֵלָיו, אַבְרָהָם וַיֹּאמֶר הִנֵּנִי.
And so it was, after these things, that God tested Abraham; and he said to him: "Abraham." And he said: "Here I am."note
2
וַיֹּאמֶר קַח-נָא אֶת-בִּנְךָ אֶת-יְחִידְךָ אֲשֶׁר-אָהַבְתָּ, אֶת-יִצְחָק, וְלֶךְ-לְךָ, אֶל-אֶרֶץ הַמֹּרִיָּה; וְהַעֲלֵהוּ שָׁם, לְעֹלָה, עַל אַחַד הֶהָרִים, אֲשֶׁר אֹמַר אֵלֶיךָ.
And he said: "Take now your son, your only one, whom you love — Isaac — and go, you yourself, to the land of Moriah; and bring him up there as a burnt offering, on one of the mountains that I will tell you of."note
3
וַיַּשְׁכֵּם אַבְרָהָם בַּבֹּקֶר, וַיַּחֲבֹשׁ אֶת-חֲמֹרוֹ, וַיִּקַּח אֶת-שְׁנֵי נְעָרָיו אִתּוֹ, וְאֵת יִצְחָק בְּנוֹ; וַיְבַקַּע, עֲצֵי עֹלָה, וַיָּקָם וַיֵּלֶךְ, אֶל-הַמָּקוֹם אֲשֶׁר-אָמַר-לוֹ הָאֱלֹהִים.
And Abraham rose early in the morning, and saddled his donkey, and took his two young men with him, and Isaac his son; and he split the wood for the burnt offering, and rose, and went to the place that God had told him.note
4
בַּיּוֹם הַשְּׁלִישִׁי, וַיִּשָּׂא אַבְרָהָם אֶת-עֵינָיו וַיַּרְא אֶת-הַמָּקוֹם--מֵרָחֹק.
On the third day Abraham lifted up his eyes, and saw the place from afar.note
5
וַיֹּאמֶר אַבְרָהָם אֶל-נְעָרָיו, שְׁבוּ-לָכֶם פֹּה עִם-הַחֲמוֹר, וַאֲנִי וְהַנַּעַר, נֵלְכָה עַד-כֹּה; וְנִשְׁתַּחֲוֶה, וְנָשׁוּבָה אֲלֵיכֶם.
And Abraham said to his young men: "Stay here, you, with the donkey; and I and the boy will go over there, and we will worship, and we will return to you."note
6
וַיִּקַּח אַבְרָהָם אֶת-עֲצֵי הָעֹלָה, וַיָּשֶׂם עַל-יִצְחָק בְּנוֹ, וַיִּקַּח בְּיָדוֹ, אֶת-הָאֵשׁ וְאֶת-הַמַּאֲכֶלֶת; וַיֵּלְכוּ שְׁנֵיהֶם, יַחְדָּו.
And Abraham took the wood of the burnt offering and laid it on Isaac his son, and took in his hand the fire and the knife; and the two of them went together.note
7
וַיֹּאמֶר יִצְחָק אֶל-אַבְרָהָם אָבִיו, וַיֹּאמֶר אָבִי, וַיֹּאמֶר, הִנֶּנִּי בְנִי; וַיֹּאמֶר, הִנֵּה הָאֵשׁ וְהָעֵצִים, וְאַיֵּה הַשֶּׂה, לְעֹלָה.
And Isaac said to Abraham his father — he said: "My father." And he said: "Here I am, my son." And he said: "Here is the fire and the wood — but where is the lamb for a burnt offering?"note
8
וַיֹּאמֶר, אַבְרָהָם, אֱלֹהִים יִרְאֶה-לּוֹ הַשֶּׂה לְעֹלָה, בְּנִי; וַיֵּלְכוּ שְׁנֵיהֶם, יַחְדָּו.
And Abraham said: "God will see to the lamb for himself, for a burnt offering, my son." And the two of them went together.note
9
וַיָּבֹאוּ, אֶל-הַמָּקוֹם אֲשֶׁר אָמַר-לוֹ הָאֱלֹהִים, וַיִּבֶן שָׁם אַבְרָהָם אֶת-הַמִּזְבֵּחַ, וַיַּעֲרֹךְ אֶת-הָעֵצִים; וַיַּעֲקֹד, אֶת-יִצְחָק בְּנוֹ, וַיָּשֶׂם אֹתוֹ עַל-הַמִּזְבֵּחַ, מִמַּעַל לָעֵצִים.
And they came to the place that God had told him; and Abraham built the altar there, and arranged the wood, and bound Isaac his son, and laid him on the altar, on top of the wood.note
10
וַיִּשְׁלַח אַבְרָהָם אֶת-יָדוֹ, וַיִּקַּח אֶת-הַמַּאֲכֶלֶת, לִשְׁחֹט, אֶת-בְּנוֹ.
And Abraham stretched out his hand and took the knife to slaughter his son.note
11
וַיִּקְרָא אֵלָיו מַלְאַךְ יְהוָה, מִן-הַשָּׁמַיִם, וַיֹּאמֶר, אַבְרָהָם אַבְרָהָם; וַיֹּאמֶר, הִנֵּנִי.
And the angel of Jehovah called to him from the heavens, and said: "Abraham! Abraham!" And he said: "Here I am."note
12
וַיֹּאמֶר, אַל-תִּשְׁלַח יָדְךָ אֶל-הַנַּעַר, וְאַל-תַּעַשׂ לוֹ, מְאוּמָה: כִּי עַתָּה יָדַעְתִּי, כִּי-יְרֵא אֱלֹהִים אַתָּה, וְלֹא חָשַׂכְתָּ אֶת-בִּנְךָ אֶת-יְחִידְךָ, מִמֶּנִּי.
And he said: "Do not stretch out your hand against the boy, and do nothing to him at all. For now I know that you are one who fears God — and you have not withheld your son, your only one, from me."note
13
וַיִּשָּׂא אַבְרָהָם אֶת-עֵינָיו, וַיַּרְא וְהִנֵּה-אַיִל, אַחַר, נֶאֱחַז בַּסְּבַךְ בְּקַרְנָיו; וַיֵּלֶךְ אַבְרָהָם וַיִּקַּח אֶת-הָאַיִל, וַיַּעֲלֵהוּ לְעֹלָה תַּחַת בְּנוֹ.
And Abraham lifted up his eyes and looked — and there, behind, a ram caught in the thicket by its horns. And Abraham went and took the ram, and brought it up as a burnt offering in place of his son.note
14
וַיִּקְרָא אַבְרָהָם שֵׁם-הַמָּקוֹם הַהוּא, יְהוָה יִרְאֶה, אֲשֶׁר יֵאָמֵר הַיּוֹם, בְּהַר יְהוָה יֵרָאֶה.
And Abraham called the name of that place Jehovah-Yireh — of which it is said to this day: "On the mount of Jehovah, it is seen to."note
15
וַיִּקְרָא מַלְאַךְ יְהוָה, אֶל-אַבְרָהָם, שֵׁנִית, מִן-הַשָּׁמָיִם.
And the angel of Jehovah called to Abraham a second time from the heavens,note
16
וַיֹּאמֶר, בִּי נִשְׁבַּעְתִּי נְאֻם-יְהוָה: כִּי, יַעַן אֲשֶׁר עָשִׂיתָ אֶת-הַדָּבָר הַזֶּה, וְלֹא חָשַׂכְתָּ, אֶת-בִּנְךָ אֶת-יְחִידֶךָ.
and said: "By myself I have sworn — declares Jehovah — because you have done this thing, and have not withheld your son, your only one,note
17
כִּי-בָרֵךְ אֲבָרֶכְךָ, וְהַרְבָּה אַרְבֶּה אֶת-זַרְעֲךָ כְּכוֹכְבֵי הַשָּׁמַיִם, וְכַחוֹל, אֲשֶׁר עַל-שְׂפַת הַיָּם; וְיִרַשׁ זַרְעֲךָ, אֵת שַׁעַר אֹיְבָיו.
that I will surely bless you, and surely multiply your seed like the stars of the heavens, and like the sand that is on the shore of the sea; and your seed shall possess the gate of his enemies.note
18
וְהִתְבָּרְכוּ בְזַרְעֲךָ, כֹּל גּוֹיֵי הָאָרֶץ, עֵקֶב, אֲשֶׁר שָׁמַעְתָּ בְּקֹלִי.
And in your seed shall all the nations of the earth be blessed — because you listened to my voice."note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–5 · קַח-נָא … וְלֶךְ-לְךָ qach-na … velekh-lekha

The hardest chapter in the book, and the word love enters it. Jewish tradition calls this the Aqedah, "the Binding" — named not for the near-killing but for one verb in verse 9. It opens with a warning the reader gets and Abraham does not: God tested him (nissah; KJV "did tempt," a word that has drifted — the old sense was put to the proof, not lure into evil; NIV "tested"). Then the command, built to wound one clause at a time — "your son… your only one… whom you love… Isaac." That word, ahav, is the first appearance of "love" in the whole Bible — and it enters at the exact moment love is asked to be surrendered. The Septuagint softened yechid ("only one") to agapēton, "beloved," the very word the voice at Jesus' baptism will use; the tradition heard the rhyme early.

Go, you yourself — the bookend. "And go, you yourself" — lekh-lekha: the emphatic doubled command that appears exactly twice in the Torah, and both times to Abraham — at (12:1), "go from your land," which began everything, and here, "go to the land of Moriah," which nearly ends it. The first lekh-lekha gave up his past; the second asks for his future. The rest is famous for its restraint: no inner monologue, no protest (this is the man who argued God down to ten righteous over Sodom — and here says nothing). He "rose early in the morning" (wayyashkem, the very habit banked one chapter ago at Hagar's dawn, 21:14 — now on a far darker morning), split the wood with his own hands, and walked three days into silence. And in verse 5 a sentence the reader cannot weigh: "we will worship, and we will return to you" — plural. Resolve, or faith, or a father unable to say otherwise; the Hebrew leaves it (Hebrews 11:19 read it as faith that God could raise the dead).

Verses 6–8 · וַיֵּלְכוּ שְׁנֵיהֶם יַחְדָּו vayelkhu shenehem yachdav

The son carries the wood, and the only conversation. "Abraham took the wood… and laid it on Isaac his son" — the boy carries up the hill the wood he is to die on, a detail the Christian tradition has read as figure since the church fathers; the note records the reading and lets the Hebrew stand. Between them passes the only dialogue of the chapter, and it turns on the word that has been the family's whole life: Isaac says "My father," Abraham answers "Here I am, my son" (hineni again — the second of three; the man is completely available to God, to his son, and, at the knife, to the angel). The child's question is the unbearable one: "where is the lamb?" And the answer is the chapter's theological center: "God will see to the lamb for himself" — Elohim yireh-lo, the seeing-verb that will name the mountain (v14). KJV "God will provide himself a lamb" — and "provide" is exactly right, because in English too, to provide is to see ahead (Latin pro-videre): God's provision is God's seeing. The line bookends with "and the two of them went together" (yachdav) — said in verse 6 and again in verse 8, an envelope around the ascent; when they come down, only one is named as returning (v19), and the rabbis never stopped noticing.

Verses 9–14 · וַיַּעֲקֹד … יְהוָה יִרְאֶה vayya'aqod … Jehovah-Yireh

The binding, and the hand that stops. The whole episode is named from one word here: "he bound Isaac his son" — vayya'aqod, the verb aqad, which occurs only this once in all of scripture, and gave the scene its Hebrew title, the Aqedah. Jewish memory rests on the binding, not the near-death: an old, consenting Isaac (the midrash makes him thirty-seven) laid on the wood. The narrative slows to single motions — stretched out the hand, took the knife — and then the interruption, doubled for urgency: "Abraham, Abraham!" (as "Simon, Simon" and "Saul, Saul" will later mark a life turned mid-motion). "Now I know" (v12) states the test's whole purpose — not that God learned a fact he lacked, but that the fear of God was made visible, driven into the open where it could be seen. "You have not withheld your son" — lo chasakhta: Paul reverses this exact phrase across the canon — God "did not spare his own Son" (Romans 8:32).

The ram, and the mountain of seeing. "Abraham lifted up his eyes and looked" — the same gesture as the woman at the other well, whose "eyes God opened, and she saw" (21:19): the seeing-thread of this book keeps closing on rescue. "A ram caught in the thicket by its horns" — the substitute, offered up "in place of his son" (tachat beno): a life for a life, the logic of every altar to come. And Abraham names the place Jehovah-Yireh, "Jehovah sees / provides" (KJV "Jehovah-jireh"), sealing the pun the chapter has been building — yireh (he sees to it, v8), yera'eh (it is seen to, v14). This site has collected the seeing-God before: Hagar's El Roi, "the God who sees" (16:13); now the mountain bears the name. And there is one more thread the note owes the reader: 2 Chronicles 3:1 says Solomon built the temple "on Mount Moriah" — so the tradition set this altar exactly where the later altar would stand, and "on the mount of Jehovah it is seen to" became a saying about a specific hill in Jerusalem.

Verses 15–19 · בִּי נִשְׁבַּעְתִּי bi nishba'ti

The one oath God swears by himself. "By myself I have sworn" — the only time in the Torah God binds himself with an oath, and the tradition weighed it heavily: there being no one greater to swear by, he swore by himself (Hebrews 6:13 makes the whole argument from this verse). What follows is the seventh and final restatement of the promise first spoken at 12:1-3, and now — after the knife — sealed under oath: seed like the stars of the heavens (the count Abram was shown on a night long ago, 15:5) and now also like the sand on the seashore, a second, earth-bound multitude. And the old universal note returns, its verb upgraded: not just "in you shall all families be blessed" (12:3) but "in your seed shall all the nations of the earth be blessed" — the channel named. The reason given is the chapter's last quiet echo: "because you listened to my voice" (shama beqoli) — the phrase that named a fall in the garden (3:17, "you listened to the voice of your wife"), was turned into a command one chapter ago (21:12, "listen to her voice"), and here becomes the ground of the world's blessing: an obedience that undoes a disobedience. Then the descent, and a return "to Beersheba" — exactly where chapter 21 left him, the well of the oath now holding a second, greater one.

Verses 20–24 · וּבְתוּאֵל, יָלַד אֶת-רִבְקָה uVtu'el yalad et-Rivqah

A quiet coda that turns the whole book forward. After the mountain, an ordinary piece of family news arrives from the old country — "your brother Nahor" (left behind in Haran, 11:29) has a houseful of sons. It reads like a genealogical footnote, twelve names of Aramean clans (Uz, where Job will live; Buz; Aram, the people whose language the later scriptures will partly speak). But the list is built to deliver one name, and it lands almost in passing, in verse 23: "and Bethuel fathered Rebekah." The chapter that nearly ended the line of promise closes by naming, without fanfare, the girl who will carry it on — the wife Isaac has not yet met, two chapters early. The son was spared on the mountain; here, quietly, the narrative begins arranging for him to have a future. It is the book's characteristic move: after the unbearable, the ordinary, already turning toward tomorrow.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation sides with the literal shelf: "you have not withheld your son" (KJV) — kept, because Paul reverses this exact verb; "Jehovah-Yireh" left as the name it is (KJV "Jehovah-jireh"); "the two of them went together," the envelope kept in both verses.

Where it goes its own way: "tested" for nissah (KJV "tempt," aged out of its old proving-sense); "God will see to the lamb for himself" for yireh-lo (KJV "provide" — the note keeps both, since to provide is to see-ahead); "bound" for aqad, the hapax that titles the whole scene; "your only one" for yechid (KJV "only son"), the word the Septuagint rhymed toward "beloved."

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: lekh-lekha, "go, you yourself" — the twin command that brackets Abraham's whole story (12:1 and here); "rose early in the morning," the dawn-habit banked at (21:14); the seeing-God — Hagar's El Roi (16:13) and the eyes opened at the well (21:19), now the name of a mountain; the stars Abram counted (15:5), joined by the sand; "all the families/nations blessed" — the 12:3 promise (12:3) sealed under oath; "listened to my voice" — the garden's fatal phrase (3:17), turned to obedience; and Beersheba (21:33), the well of the oath, holding a second. Planted: Rebekah, named in the coda, waiting for chapter 24; Isaac's own unrecorded descent, which the rabbis never stopped reading; and Moriah — the mountain of seeing that 2 Chronicles will make the Temple Mount.

Next installment in this book: Genesis 23 — the death of Sarah, and the cave of Machpelah: Abraham buys a grave, and the promise of the land begins with four hundred shekels and a field.

and would have none of my reproof —note
26
גַּם-אֲנִי, בְּאֵידְכֶם אֶשְׂחָק; אֶלְעַג, בְּבֹא פַחְדְּכֶם.
I too will laugh at your calamity,
I will mock when your dread comes,note
27
בְּבֹא כשאוה כְשׁוֹאָה, פַּחְדְּכֶם-- וְאֵידְכֶם, כְּסוּפָה יֶאֱתֶה; בְּבֹא עֲלֵיכֶם, צָרָה וְצוּקָה.
when your dread comes like a storm,
and your calamity arrives like a whirlwind, when distress and anguish come upon you.note
28
אָז יִקְרָאֻנְנִי, וְלֹא אֶעֱנֶה; יְשַׁחֲרֻנְנִי, וְלֹא יִמְצָאֻנְנִי.
Then they will call me, but I will not answer;
they will seek me, but they will not find me.note
29
תַּחַת, כִּי-שָׂנְאוּ דָעַת; וְיִרְאַת יְהוָה, לֹא בָחָרוּ.
Because they hated knowledge,
and did not choose the fear of Jehovah;note
30
לֹא-אָבוּ לַעֲצָתִי; נָאֲצוּ, כָּל-תּוֹכַחְתִּי.
they would have none of my counsel,
they spurned all my reproof —note
31
וְיֹאכְלוּ, מִפְּרִי דַרְכָּם; וּמִמֹּעֲצֹתֵיהֶם יִשְׂבָּעוּ.
so they shall eat from the fruit of their way,
and be filled with their own devices.note
32
כִּי מְשׁוּבַת פְּתָיִם תַּהַרְגֵם; וְשַׁלְוַת כְּסִילִים תְּאַבְּדֵם.
For the waywardness of the simple kills them,
and the complacency of fools destroys them.note
33
וְשֹׁמֵעַ לִי, יִשְׁכָּן-בֶּטַח; וְשַׁאֲנַן, מִפַּחַד רָעָה. {פ}
but whoever listens to me will dwell secure,
and be at ease, without dread of harm."note

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–7 · יִרְאַת יְהוָה, רֵאשִׁית דָּעַת yirat Jehovah, reshit da'at

A new kind of book — and its whole toolkit in seven verses. This is the site's first wisdom book, and it does not tell a story or deliver an oracle: it teaches. The Hebrew is poetry — every line a couplet, the second half echoing, sharpening, or countering the first (this translation sets the two halves on two lines so the parallel shows). The title-word is the book's own name: mishlei, "the proverbs of Solomon" — a mashal is a comparison, a saying that rules by likeness (the root means "to be like," and also "to rule"). Verses 2–6 are one long sentence of purpose, and they lay out the book's whole vocabulary — and Hebrew keeps these words distinct, so this translation does too (each has its own dictionary entry): chokhmah, wisdom, the master-skill for living; da'at, knowledge, what is known (from yada, "to know"); binah, understanding — from bin, "to discern between," the faculty that tells one thing from another; mezimmah, discretion, shrewd forethought (v4); and haskel, insight, prudence that actually succeeds (v3). They are not synonyms stacked for volume: wisdom is knowledge and understanding put to work. And musar — which this translation renders discipline, with the ASV and NWT, against the KJV's softer "instruction" — is the odd one out: not a kind of knowing at all, but the correction that forms a person, the training with a rod behind it ("do not despise the discipline of Jehovah," 3:11). The two audiences: the simple (peti — the wide-open, gullible young person) and the already-wise, told to keep learning (v5). The prize offered the simple in verse 4 is ormah, "shrewdness" — and here the shelf keeps a secret this project loves: KJV renders it "subtilty," the very word it used for the serpent in (Genesis 3:1), "more subtil than any beast." The serpent's root and Wisdom's are the same (ʿarum / ormah): the craftiness that ruined Eden is exactly what Wisdom gives the naive to survive — shrewdness redeemed.

The motto of the book. Then verse 7, the sentence everything else hangs from: "The fear of Jehovah is the beginning of knowledge" (ASV reads it exactly so — "the fear of Jehovah is the beginning of knowledge" — while KJV prints its small-caps divine name in the same slot). Two things the shelf can't quite carry at once: reshit is "beginning" — and it is the exact word that opens the whole Bible, bereshit, "in the beginning" (Genesis 1:1) — but it also means "the first / chief part," the firstfruits, the best. So the fear of Jehovah is both where knowledge starts and its finest portion. And "fear" (yirah) is not fright but reverent awe — the same posture the angel found in Abraham at the altar, "now I know that you fear God" (22:12). The opposite party is named too: the evilim, the fools who despise wisdom and discipline. That verb (bazu) is a perfect — and the NWT, at its careful most-literal, carries it as "have despised"; this translation reads it as the gnomic perfect that Hebrew wisdom favors — a standing truth stated as already settled, which idiomatic English renders in the present. A considered choice, not a default: the sense is that fools always have and always do. Either way, the evilim are the first entry in the book's long rogues' gallery of the unwise.

Verses 8–19 · אִם-יְפַתּוּךָ חַטָּאִים im-yefattukha chatta'im

The first lecture: a father's voice, a mother's law. "Hear, my son" (shema beni) — the address that will open lecture after lecture; wisdom in Proverbs is handed down at home, across a table, parent to child. And it comes from both parents: "your father's instruction and your mother's teaching" — where "teaching" is torah, the very word for the five books of Moses, here given to a mother's home-lesson (KJV "the law of thy mother"): Torah begins on a lap. The reward is jewelry — "a wreath of grace for your head" (KJV "an ornament of grace"): the disciplined life as something you wear.

"If sinners entice you." The verb is patah — and this site has heard it before, in the rawest place: it is the word Jeremiah threw at God, "you enticed me, Jehovah, and I was enticed" (Jeremiah 20:7). The same seduction the prophet felt from heaven, the son is warned against from the street-gang — whose pitch the father then quotes at length, and it is chillingly modern: easy money, belonging ("one purse for us all"), the thrill of the ambush. The trap-image is precise: even a bird won't fly into a net it can see being spread (v17) — yet these men spread it for themselves, "they lie in wait for their own blood" (v18). The moral is stated as physics, not sermon: "such are the paths of everyone greedy for gain — it takes away the life of its owner" (v19). Greed is a boomerang; the loot ends up costing exactly the thing it was traded for.

Verses 20–33 · חָכְמוֹת בַּחוּץ תָּרֹנָּה chokhmot bachuts taronnah

Lady Wisdom takes the street. Now the book's most startling move: Wisdom stands up and preaches. She is personified — a woman (the noun is feminine), and the plural form chokhmot reads as majestic, "Wisdom" at full stature (KJV "Wisdom" for the plural). And she doesn't sit in a school: she "cries aloud in the street," in the squares, "at the head of the noisy streets," "at the openings of the gates" — the market, the courthouse, the town's loudest corners. This is a prophet's posture, not a professor's; she sounds like Jeremiah at the temple gate. Her first words are the prophets' own weary question: "How long?" — aimed at the whole rogues' gallery at once: the simple (petayim) who love their simplicity, the scoffers (letsim — the cynics who mock everything; KJV "scorners"), and the fools (kesilim — the thick, the dull; a different fool from verse 7's evil) who "hate knowledge." Hebrew has a whole thesaurus for foolishness, and this chapter opens the drawer.

The most frightening promise in the book — and the softest. What follows is hard to hear: the same Wisdom who begs in verse 23 ("turn back… I will pour out my spirit to you") warns that a day is coming when the call reverses — "I too will laugh at your calamity" (v26), and "then they will call me, but I will not answer" (v28). It is the exact inversion of every prophet's promise that God answers those who call; refuse wisdom long enough, the poem says, and you unfit yourself to receive it — not because the door is shut in spite, but because "they hated knowledge and did not choose the fear of Jehovah" (v29), and a life reaps "the fruit of its own way" (v31) the way the greedy men of verse 19 did. The Hebrew here even carries a scribe's fingerprint — verse 27's ketiv/qere, the written and read forms of "storm" standing side by side, the manuscript's honesty this site first met in Daniel. But the chapter does not end in the whirlwind. Its last line drops the volume all the way down to a promise anyone can keep: "whoever listens to me will dwell secure, and be at ease, without dread of harm" (v33). The whole book's offer, in one quiet couplet — safety, not from trouble, but from fear.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation follows the most literal witnesses: "Jehovah" where most of the shelf prints the small-caps substitute for the Name (with the ASV and NWT, the two versions that read this verse most exactly); "Wisdom cries" for the majestic plural; and — the point of the whole prologue — both word-families kept distinct rather than blurred: the fools (peti simple, lets scoffer, kesil and evil, two different fools) and the five wisdom-words (wisdom, knowledge, understanding, discretion, insight), each rendered by its own English word, as Hebrew keeps them.

Where it goes its own way: the couplets set on two lines, to show the Hebrew parallelism the prose shelf hides; "discipline" for musar — with the ASV and NWT, against the softer "instruction" of KJV and NIV — because musar is correction that forms, not mere information (an earlier draft of this page reached for the familiar "instruction"; the sharper word is the right one); "shrewdness" for ormah (KJV "subtilty" — the note keeps the KJV's serpent-echo, since that is the whole point); "your mother's teaching" for torah (KJV "law"), to let a mother's Torah be heard as Torah; and "the beginning / chief part" both kept in the note for reshit.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: ormah, Wisdom's "shrewdness," the serpent's craft (3:1) turned to a survival skill; reshit, "beginning," the Bible's own first word (Genesis 1:1); the fear of God, found in Abraham at the altar (22:12); and patah, "entice," the verb Jeremiah hurled at heaven (Jeremiah 20:7), now the sinners' pitch. Planted: Lady Wisdom, who will rise to her full height in Proverbs 8 (and whom the New Testament will hear behind the voice of Christ); the fools' gallery, filled in chapter by chapter; and "the fear of Jehovah," the refrain the whole book is tuned to.

Next installment in this book: Proverbs 2 — "if you seek her like silver, and search for her like hidden treasure": wisdom as the thing worth digging for.

Translator's Notes — verse by verse

Each note explains this translation's choice and compares the seven versions on the shelf. Quotations from copyrighted versions (NIV, TLB, NWT) are kept to short phrases for comparison; KJV, Geneva, Douay-Rheims and ASV are public domain.

Verses 1–6 · גֵּר-וְתוֹשָׁב אָנֹכִי ger-vetoshav anokhi

The only matriarch whose age and death the Torah records. Sarah dies at 127 — scripture gives no other woman's lifespan — and the chapter opens the parashah the tradition calls Chayei Sarah, "the Life of Sarah," a title that names a death. She dies at Kiriath-arba ("town of Arba," a giant of the Anakim, or "town of the four") — the narrator adds, as he does with Ur and the Philistines, the name the reader knows: "that is, Hebron." And Abraham "came to lament for Sarah and to weep for her" — two distinct words this translation keeps apart: sapad, the formal, public mourning-rite (the eulogy, the wailing), and bakah, plain tears. The Masoretes even wrote the kaf of "weep" (velivkotah) as a miniature letter in the scroll — a scribal whisper the sages read as private grief, the tears kept small behind the public lament.

"A resident alien and a settler." Abraham's self-description is two precise legal words, and the shelf mostly blurs them: ger and toshav (KJV "a stranger and a sojourner"; NWT "an alien resident and a settler," keeping them two). A ger is a resident foreigner — living among a people, protected, but with no citizenship and no right to own land; a toshav is a settled outsider, a step more rooted, still landless. That is the whole problem of the chapter stated in a breath: the man to whom God has promised all this land as an everlasting holding (17:8) cannot legally bury his wife in it — he owns not one square foot. And the word he uses for what he needs is loaded: achuzzah, a holding — the permanent, inheritable land-possession (from achaz, "to grasp, hold fast"), the very term 17:8 used for the promised land itself. The sons of Heth answer with Eastern courtesy and real respect: "you are a prince of God among us" — nesi Elohim (NWT "a chieftain of God"; KJV flattens the divine name to a superlative, "a mighty prince"). Both readings live in the phrase — "a prince of God" and "a godlike, mighty prince" — and this translation keeps the literal one, since the whole point is that these Hittites can see whose man Abraham is.

Verses 7–16 · בְּכֶסֶף מָלֵא … עֹבֵר לַסֹּחֵר bekhesef male … over lassocher

A masterclass in Near-Eastern bargaining. Every move here is ritual, and to a modern ear it reads like theater — because it is. Abraham bows (twice), speaks only through intermediaries at first, and insists on paying full silver (kesef male, v9) — no gift, no debt, no lien a future heir could contest. Ephron plays his part with lavish generosity: "the field I give you, and the cave — I give it you!" (v11) — said three times, in public, at the city gate. It is not an offer to donate; it is the opening of a negotiation, the seller's flourish before the number. And the number, when it slips out, is dressed as a trifle between friends: "land worth four hundred shekels of silver — between me and you, what is that?" (v15). Four hundred shekels was a small fortune (Jeremiah later buys a whole field for seventeen, 32:9); Ephron names a steep price with a shrug, and Abraham, who wanted no favors, pays it without a word of haggling. He "weighed out" the silver — coinage was centuries away; you cut and weighed metal — "four hundred shekels, at the merchant's standard" (over lassocher, KJV "current money with the merchant"): full weight, trade-grade, no thumb on the scale. The scene is a legal record disguised as a courtesy.

Verses 17–20 · וַיָּקָם שְׂדֵה עֶפְרוֹן vayyaqom sedeh Efron

The deed, itemized — and the first foothold. The transfer is recorded with a surveyor's care: the field, the cave, "and every tree in the field, within all its border round about" — Hittite and Mesopotamian land-deeds itemized the trees exactly so, because trees were taxable and disputable. The verb for what happened to the property is a legal one: it "rose" — vayyaqom — meaning it stood confirmed, was made over, passed valid title (KJV "were made sure"; the same root that says a covenant is "established"). Witnessed by the whole assembly "at the gate of his city" — the ancient courthouse — it is a transaction no one could ever overturn. And that is the quiet thunder of this chapter: the man promised an entire land dies owning a single field with a cave in it — a grave. The whole inheritance of Israel, in Abraham's lifetime, comes down to Machpelah ("the double" — from kaphal, "to fold, to double") — the first legally-held parcel of the Promised Land, and the down payment on all the rest. It becomes the patriarchal tomb: Abraham himself, Isaac and Rebekah, Jacob and Leah are all laid here (25:9; 49:31; 50:13), and the Herodian enclosure over the cave at Hebron still stands, one of the oldest continuously-venerated sites on earth. Faith buries its dead in the ground of the promise — and holds the receipt.

Patterns worth carrying forward

Where this translation keeps the words distinct: "a resident alien and a settler" for ger and toshav (with the NWT, against KJV's merged "stranger and sojourner") — two different legal statuses, both landless; "lament" and "weep" for sapad and bakah (the public rite and the private tears); and "a prince of God" for nesi Elohim (with the NWT's "chieftain of God," against KJV's "mighty prince"), keeping the divine name the Hittites actually spoke.

Where it goes its own way: "a holding" for achuzzah (KJV "possession"), to let it ring against 17:8's promised holding; "was made over" for the legal vayyaqom (KJV "were made sure"); "at the merchant's standard" for over lassocher (KJV "current money with the merchant"); and numerals for Sarah's 127 years.

Echoes paid and planted. Paid: achuzzah, the "everlasting holding" God promised at (17:8) — and here, in Abraham's lifetime, a burial holding is all of it he owns; Mamre, the oaks where Abraham pitched his tent (13:18), now facing the family tomb; "I will make your name great" (12:2), visible in the Hittites' "prince of God"; and the land promised "to your seed" (12:7), of which this single field is the first legal parcel. Planted: Machpelah, the patriarchal tomb (Isaac and Jacob will come here); Rebekah, named two chapters back, whom the next chapter goes to fetch for the now-motherless Isaac; and Hebron, which will be Israel's first royal capital under David.

Next installment in this book: Genesis 24 — the longest chapter in the book: a servant, ten camels, a prayer at a well, and Rebekah's "I will go" — how Isaac gets a wife.
